==PAGE:1==
Master Thesis
Assessing the Hydrological Impact of Bioretention
Units in Residential Areas using SWMM, MIKE
URBAN and the Simpliﬁed WSUD-Potential Tool
Department of Environmental Engineering
Technical University of Denmark, Kongens Lyngby
June 12th 2016
Katrine Bækmark Schiolborg (s103346)
Maja Korsgaard Lindbladh (s103355)
Supervisors: Sara Maria Lerer, Francisco Laurito Torres and Peter Steen Mikkelsen
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Increased precipitation is expected in Denmark due to climate change. Together with high
imperviousness of urbanised areas, increased precipitation will result in increased runoﬀ.
The current drainage system might therefore not fulﬁl the service level in the future. This
has led to more focus on low impact development (LID), which enhances the natural water
balance through increased inﬁltration and evaporation. Several types of LIDs exist, such
as bioretention units, permeable pavement and green roofs. A simple and user friendly
tool, referred to as the WSUD-potential tool, has previously been developed, estimating
the water balance and overﬂow return period of diﬀerent LIDs.
The aim of this project was to assess the hydrological impact of bioretention units in
residential areas in Copenhagen, Denmark. Analyses were made in the advanced mod-
elling tools MIKE URBAN and SWMM, together with the WSUD-potential tool. Initially
the performance of bioretention units in MIKE URBAN was tested by comparing results
with results obtained in SWMM. The comparison showed that the two programs produce
similar results. A sensitivity analysis was conducted, where the inﬂuence of the biore-
tention unit input parameters in MIKE URBAN was evaluated based on evaporation,
inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency. Area ratio and conductivity of native soil were iden-
tiﬁed as the most inﬂuential parameters, where area ratio is the ratio between the area
of the bioretention unit and the catchment area.
Based on these two most inﬂuential
parameters, regressions were made, evaluating evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow fre-
quency for three diﬀerent bioretention designs. The regressions were used to update the
WSUD-potential tool in order to achieve more detailed results.
The performance of MIKE URBAN and the WSUD-potential tool was investigated using
two homeowner’s associations in Copenhagen. The assessment showed that the two tools
give similar results for all three bioretention designs. Furthermore, the tools indicate that
implementation of bioretention units enhance the natural water balance and reduce the
runoﬀ.
The advanced modelling tools are diﬃcult to use without modelling skills and technical
knowledge within the ﬁeld. Based on the analyses conducted in this project, the updated
WSUD-potential tool can advantageously be used in the initial phase of LID projects,
to achieve rough estimations of the hydrological impact and enhance the communication
between stakeholders.
However, none of the models used in this project were neither
calibrated nor validated, as they are used to design future constructions. Furthermore,
a thorough uncertainty analysis was not carried out. This should be taken into account
when using the tools and interpreting the results of this project.

Sammenfatning
Klimaforandringerne forventer at medføre øget nedbør i Danmark. Øget nedbør vil, sam-
men med høj befæstelsesgrad i urbane omr˚ader, føre til mere afstrømning. Det nuværende
aﬂøbssystem kan dermed f˚a svært ved at leve op til service kravene i fremtiden. Dette har
medført et større fokus p˚a lokal aﬂedning af regnvand (LAR), som forstærker den naturlige
vandbalance gennem øget inﬁltration og fordampning. Der ﬁndes adskillige typer LAR,
for eksempel regnhaver, permeabel belægning samt grønne tage. Et simpelt og bruger-
venligt værktøj, LAR-potentiale værktøjet, er tidligere blevet udviklet til estimering af
vandbalancen og gentagelsesperioden for overløb fra forskellige LAR typer.
Form˚alet med dette projekt er at undersøge den hydrologiske eﬀekt af at implementere reg-
nhaver i urbane omr˚ader i København, Danmark. Forskellige analyser blev foretaget i de
avancerede programmer MIKE URBAN og SWMM samt i LAR-potentiale værktøjet. Ef-
fekten af at implementere regnhaver i MIKE URBAN blev undersøgt ved at sammenligne
resultater med resultater fra SWMM. Sammenligningen viste at de to programmer produc-
erer sammenlignelige resultater. En sensitivitetsanalyse blev foretaget, hvor indﬂydelsen
af forskellige input parametre for regnhaver i MIKE URBAN blev undersøgt, baseret
p˚a fordampning, inﬁltration og overløbsfrekvens.
Arealforholdet og konduktiviteten af
jorden blev identiﬁceret som de parametre med størst indﬂydelse p˚a resultaterne. Areal-
forholdet er deﬁneret som forholdet mellem arealet af regnhaverne og arealet af oplandet.
P˚a baggrund af disse to mest indﬂydelsesrige parametre, udarbejdedes regressioner, for
at evaluere fordampning, inﬁltration og overløbsfrekvens for tre forskellige typer af regn-
haver. Regressionerne blev brugt til at opdatere LAR-potentiale værktøjet, for at f˚a mere
detaljerede resultater.
Brugbarheden af MIKE URBAN og LAR-potentiale værktøjet blev undersøgt p˚a to grun-
dejerforeninger i København. Undersøgelsen viste at de to modeller giver sammenlignelige
resultater for alle tre typer af regnhaver. Det blev ydermere fundet, at implementering af
regnhaver forstærker den naturlige vandbalance og reducerer afstrømningen.
Det er svært at benytte de avancerede programmer uden erfaring med modellering og
teknisk viden indenfor omr˚adet. P˚a baggrund af analyserne fortaget i dette projekt, vur-
deres det at LAR-potentiale værktøjet med fordel kan benyttes i opstartsfasen af LAR
projekter, for at opn˚a grove estimeringer af den hydrologiske eﬀekt og forstærke kom-
munikationen mellem interessenter. Ingen af modellerne i dette projekt er dog hverken
kalibreret eller valideret, eftersom de bruges til at designe fremtidige konstruktioner. En
grundig usikkerhedsanalyse blev ikke udført i projektet. Dette er vigtigt at tage højde for
n˚ar man bruger modellerne og resultaterne fra dette projekt.

Preface
This master thesis is the ﬁnal project in the Master of Science in Environmental Engi-
neering. The project is written at the Department of Environmental Engineering at the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in collaboration with the company DHI. The
thesis accounts for 30 ECTS points, and the report is due 12th of June 2016.
The modelling, data analysis and the writing of the report have been made in collab-
oration between the two group members, and the work load was equally distributed.
We would like to thank our supervisors PhD student Sara Maria Lerer and Professor
Peter Steen Mikkelsen from DTU Environment and MSc in Civil Engineering Francisco
Laurito Torres from DHI for good instructions and supervision throughout the project.
We would also like to thank Numerical Engine Software Developer at DHI Eduardo Javier
Mu˜noz Queupumil and Postdoc at DTU Luca Locatelli for support regarding the Monte
Carlo sensitivity analysis. Furthermore, we would like to thank DHI for the collaboration
and Greater Copenhagen Utility (HOFOR) for inputs and data related to the homeowner’s
associations used as case studies in the project.

1
Introduction
1
Introduction
Urbanisation results in an increase of impermeable areas in cities. As less water can inﬁl-
trate into the ground, the natural water balance is disturbed and more runoﬀis created
during rain events (Butler and Davies, 2011). In Denmark the precipitation is expected to
increase due to climate changes (IPCC, 2014; DMI, 2014a). The increased precipitation
will, as urbanisation, also result in more runoﬀ, which will enter the drainage system and
put a higher pressure on the system. As the amount of water the drainage system can
manage is limited, more runoﬀwill result in more frequent and severe ﬂoods and combined
sewer overﬂows (CSO). Due to a high population and assets density, cities are generally
vulnerable to ﬂooding. Furthermore, CSO can aﬀect the water quality in the recipients.
Diﬀerent approaches can be used in order to manage the increased precipitation, e.g. the
capacity of the drainage system can be increased or low impact development (LID) can
be implemented (Zhou et al., 2012; Brown Jr., 2005).
In this project, LID is the term used for structures aiming to reach a more natural wa-
ter balance, by enhancing inﬁltration and evaporation. This can include structures such
as bioretention units, green roofs and permeable surfaces. Several terms are used to de-
scribe more or less the same technology, where a common term for all the diﬀerent names
are stormwater control measures (SCMs) (Fletcher et al., 2015). By managing stormwa-
ter locally LIDs reduce the amount of stormwater entering the drainage system and the
wastewater treatment plant (WWTP). Furthermore, CSO can be reduced, biodiversity
can be improved and recreational value can be added (Zhou et al., 2012; Moore and Hunt,
2012).
Within the last decade Denmark has experienced more extreme rain events (cloudbursts).
As a consequence, the utility companies, municipalities and the citizens of Copenhagen
have invested in SCMs. The Municipality of Copenhagen has developed a cloudburst man-
agement plan and a climate adaptation plan. Furthermore, a co-ﬁnancing scheme has been
approved allowing utility companies to ﬁnance private owned climate adaptation projects.
This activity has resulted in many stakeholders with diﬀerent interests in the ﬁeld (Lerer
et al., 2015b). Architects, citizens and municipalities, for example, value the recreational
and aesthetic aspect of LIDs, whereas the utility companies focus on performance of the
system and water infrastructures. As there are many stakeholders involved who value
diﬀerent aspects of LIDs the communication between the stakeholders is important (Lerer
et al., 2015b; Fratini et al., 2012).
Models are very useful as they can predict how a system will perform in diﬀerent situa-
tions. If changes are proposed for an existing system, the eﬀect and eﬃciency of diﬀerent
changes can be tested with a model before making the ﬁnal decision of what changes to
apply. Several advanced modelling tools are available for simulation of drainage system
and LIDs, e.g. the Storm Water Management Model (SWMM) provided by the United
States Environmental Protection Agency (US EPA) and MIKE URBAN developed by
DHI. Since 2016 a new release of MIKE URBAN is available, where diﬀerent LIDs can be
implemented in a catchment or network (DHI, 2016b). Similar LID structures have been
available in SWMM since 2009 (US EPA, 2009). A more simple and user friendly tool is
useful in the start of LID projects, in order to get a quick overview of diﬀerent possibilities,
and to enhance the communication and understanding between diﬀerent stakeholders in
projects (Lerer et al., 2015b). Based on this need, the WSUD-potential tool was devel-
oped in a project run by the Foundation for Development of Technology in the Danish
1
==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
Water Sector (VTU-Fonden). The WSUD-potential tool can estimate the return period
of overﬂows from LIDs and predict how LIDs aﬀect the water balance of a catchment. In
the current version of the tool, three types of LIDs can be included: Permeable surfaces,
bioretention units and detention units (Lerer et al., 2015b). The eﬀects of these LIDs are
based on lookup tables, showing results obtained in SWMM for diﬀerent combinations of
area ratios and conductivities of the native soil. The area ratio is deﬁned as the ratio
between the area of the LID and the catchment area.
The main objective for this master thesis is to assess the hydrological impact of biore-
tention units in residential areas using SWMM, MIKE URBAN and the WSUD-potential
tool.
In order to reach this objective, the following objectives are formulated for this
thesis:
 Assess the performance of bioretention units in MIKE URBAN by comparing the
results with results obtained in SWMM.
 Evaluate the inﬂuence of bioretention unit parameters in MIKE URBAN by per-
forming a Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis.
 Further develop the WSUD-potential tool by making regressions for diﬀerent biore-
tention designs, based on the most inﬂuential parameters.
 Investigate the potential hydrological eﬀects of diﬀerent bioretention designs in
homeowner’s associations in Copenhagen, using MIKE URBAN and the WSUD-
potential tool.
 Discuss the applicability of MIKE URBAN and the WSUD-potential tool for estima-
tions of the water balance and overﬂow frequency for bioretention units in urbanised
areas.

==PAGE:12==
2
Theory
2
Theory
2.1
Stormwater Control Measures
Around the world diﬀerent terms have emerged for describing management of urban
stormwater.
For example low impact development (LID), sustainable urban drainage
systems (SUDS), water sensitive urban design (WSUD) and best management practice
(BMP). The corresponding Danish expression is lokal aﬂedning af regnvand (LAR). The
diﬀerent terms originate from diﬀerent parts of the world and include slightly diﬀerent
concepts. However, the terms are widely used and the deﬁnition can vary depending on
the context (Fletcher et al., 2015). In some cases, the deﬁnition also includes ﬂood pro-
tection. According to Fletcher et al. (2015) stormwater control measures (SCMs) should
be the universal agreement for referring to structures managing stormwater.
In this project, SCMs are used for describing city development including all surface and
subsurface solutions that manage stormwater. LID is the term used for structures aiming
to reach a more natural water balance, e.g. bioretention units, green roofs and permeable
surfaces. LIDs can be used to enhance inﬁltration, evaporation, detention, transportation
and treatment of stormwater. The term LID is used in this project as the modelling tools
SWMM and MIKE URBAN refer to diﬀerent green solutions as LIDs. Furthermore, the
term WSUD is used when referring to the simpliﬁed WSUD-potential tool. The term
WSUD originates from Australia and is used to describe similar structures as included in
LIDs, though with slightly diﬀerent designs and purposes, as Australia experiences both
ﬂooding and drought (Madsen et al., subm).
According to a new conceptual framework, proposed by Madsen et al. (subm), the tech-
nologies of SCMs go through four diﬀerent stages in the stabilization process: New technol-
ogy, testing, opportunity and agreement. During the ﬁrst stage a new technology emerges
as the surrounding system experiences changes. In the second stage, the technology is
tested and improved by few involved actors. In the third stage confrontations arise as new
actors are involved. Finally, in the fourth stage, the confrontations are solved and the
actors come to an agreement (Madsen et al., subm). Denmark entered the new technology
stage in the 1990s, when the term LAR was ﬁrst introduced (Fletcher et al., 2015; Mad-
sen et al., subm). The second stage was reached in the period from 1995 - 2005, where
the focus was on soakaways and detention basins. Since 2005, Denmark has been in the
opportunity stage, as there are many diﬀerent opinions on how the LID technologies shall
be used. The agreement stage has not yet been reached in Denmark (Madsen et al., subm).
When implementing LIDs, one aim is to enhance the natural water balance by reducing the
impervious area. Surfaces in cities are generally highly impervious due to buildings, pave-
ments, etc. This result in several impacts on the environment, such as decreasing water
quality, reducing biodiversity and disturbing the natural water balance. Figure 2.1 shows
how the processes in the water balance are aﬀected by urbanisation. As the increased
imperviousness reduces the inﬁltration and recharge of groundwater, a larger fraction of
the precipitation becomes runoﬀ. The water runs faster on paved surfaces compared to
on natural surfaces. Urbanisation therefore also results in higher peak ﬂows and reduced
evaporation (Butler and Davies, 2011). Haase (2009) investigated the long-term eﬀects
on the water balance due to increased urbanisation in Leipzig, Germany. The results con-
ﬁrm the eﬀect shown in Figure 2.1, with increasing runoﬀand decreasing inﬁltration and
evaporation.
3
==PAGE:13==
2
Theory
Figure 2.1: Water balance pre- and post-urbanisation (Butler and Davies, 2011).
2.1.1
Precipitation
Knowledge about precipitation is important when designing SCMs. Since 1979 the Dan-
ish Meteorological Institute (DMI) and the Water Pollution Committee of the Society of
Danish Engineers (SVK) have measured precipitation at diﬀerent stations in Denmark
using tipping-bucket rain gauges. Based on this, long historical rain series are available
describing the historical precipitation at diﬀerent locations in Denmark (DMI, 1993). The
historical rain series can be used to deﬁne the precipitation in rainfall-runoﬀmodels and
thereby predict the amount of runoﬀentering the drainage system or LIDs.
SVK has performed comprehensive statistical analyses including rain series from 66 sta-
tions (SVK, 2006). This analysis describes the regional variation of extreme rain events in
Denmark, referred to as the regional model. Based on the statistical analysis, Intensity-
Duration-Frequency (IDF) curves are constructed, where the rain intensity and duration
are plotted for diﬀerent return periods. Chicago Design Storms (CDS) can also be created
for a speciﬁc region in Denmark. A CDS rain is a single rain event based on statistics,
describing the intensity and duration of a rain event with speciﬁed return period. E.g.
based on statistics, a CDS rain with a return period of 5 years (5-year CDS rain) will occur
once every ﬁfth year, corresponding to a frequency of 1/5 per year. CDS rains are often
used in models when designing or testing the performance of drainage systems. When this
method is used, it is assumed that overﬂow occurs with the same return period as the
return period of the rain (Winther et al., 2013). This might underestimate the frequency
of overﬂow, as several small rain events in short time also can lead to overﬂow, as the
system might not recover between rain events.
In a report issued by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), the ob-
served and expected climate changes are analysed and presented. From 1951 - 2010 an
increased precipitation have been observed in the Northern Europe (IPCC, 2014). Ac-
cording to IPCC (2014) it is very likely that the intensity and frequency of rain events
will increase further in many parts of the world due to climate changes. The expected
future climate changes are estimated based on diﬀerent scenarios, ranging from optimistic
to conservative. Based on the report by IPCC, DMI has analysed the expected climate
changes in Denmark. The mean annual precipitation in Denmark is about 700 mm based
on data from 1961 - 1990. During the last 150 years, the annual precipitation in Denmark
has increased with about 100 mm (DMI, 2014a). Rain events are in general expected to
4
==PAGE:14==
2
Theory
become more extreme with higher intensity and frequency. In 2081 - 2100 the annual
precipitation is expected to increase about 1.6% - 6.9% compared to the precipitation
observed in 1986 - 2005. The highest increase of 3.1% - 18.0% is expected in the winter,
whereas the precipitation in summer is expected to decrease with about 0.5% - 16.6%.
Despite the decrease in summer precipitation, more extreme events are expected to occur
during the summer (DMI, 2014a). In 2100 a 10-year rain event is expected to have a 30%
higher intensity compared with the present 10-year events (Arnbjerg-Nielsen et al., 2014).
Climate factors can be used in order to take the increased precipitation into account when
using rain data in models. SVK (2008) recommends climate factors of 1.2, 1.3 and 1.4 for
2, 10 and 100-year rain events respectively.
In Denmark a cloudburst is an extreme rain event, deﬁned as a rain intensity of at least
15 mm of rain in a period of 30 minutes or less (DMI, 2015). One of the most extreme
cloudburst occurred in Copenhagen the 2nd of July 2011, where 50 mm of rain was mea-
sured in 30 minutes at some locations. The highest rain intensity measured was 3.1 mm
per minute during 10 minutes. The Botanical Garden in Copenhagen received the highest
amount of water, with approximately 130 mm in 24 hours (DMI, 2012). According to
Arnbjerg-Nielsen et al. (2015) the most intensive period of this event is estimated to have
a return period of more than 2 000 years in some locations.
2.1.2
The Three Points Approach
The three points approach (3PA) was ﬁrst introduced in the 11th International Confer-
ence of Urban Drainage in 2008 (Fratini et al., 2012).
The 3PA is a decision making
tool for stormwater management and aims to provide a platform for managers and op-
erators within the ﬁeld to communicate, discuss and reﬂect about future scenarios and
solutions (Fratini et al., 2012). It includes three domains used to design SCMs. According
to Fratini et al. (2012) the ﬁrst domain is the technical optimization domain focusing on
the drainage infrastructure design. The second domain is the domain of urban resilience,
which includes extreme rain and focuses on mitigating ﬂooding. The third domain is the
day-to-day domain including frequent small rain events and focuses on the way urban
space is maintained (Fratini et al., 2012).
Figure 2.2 shows the illustration of the 3PA, made by Sørup et al. (2016). This inter-
pretation puts the rain depth in relation to the return period for the three domains A,
B and C. The ﬁgure shows that the day-to-day domain (A) has a return period of 0.2
years with 20 mm rain, the technical optimization domain (B) has a return period of 10
years, corresponding to 70 mm rain and the urban resilience domain (C) has a return
period of 100 years with 110 mm rain. Furthermore, the annual water volume managed
at the diﬀerent domains is also indicated in the ﬁgure. Domain A, B and C are expected
to manage 75%, 99% and 100% of the annual precipitation respectively (Sørup et al., 2016).
Current drainage systems are designed to manage the technical optimization domain. The
values for this domain vary and are set locally depending on precipitation, population and
rate of urbanisation. In Denmark the national design guidelines are deﬁned with a return
period of 10 years and do not include the potential risk of ﬂooding (Fratini et al., 2012).
The purpose of looking at the 3PA is to include not only the design domain, but to address
all three domains collectively to make the urban areas more resilient to ﬂooding and to
improve the recreational value (Fratini et al., 2012).
5
==PAGE:15==
2
Theory
Figure 2.2: The three points approach (3PA) according to Sørup et al. (2016).
A: Day-to-day domain, B: Technical optimization domain, C: Urban resilience domain.
2.1.3
Stormwater Control Measures in Copenhagen
During the last years Copenhagen has experienced more frequent extreme rain events,
corresponding with the predictions by IPCC and DMI described in Section 2.1.1. Five
extreme events have occurred the last six years, causing ﬂooding and high damage costs.
The cloudburst on the 2nd of July in 2011 resulted in damages of 6 billion DKK. Based
on this event, ﬂooding is estimated to cause damages of about 16 billion DKK in Copen-
hagen, during the next 100 years, if no action is taken to mitigate ﬂooding (Københavns
Kommune, 2015).
When preparing Copenhagen for the increased precipitation, the Municipality of Copen-
hagen expects 30% more precipitation in the future (Københavns Kommune, 2015). In
collaboration with neighbouring municipalities, utility companies and citizens, the Mu-
nicipality of Copenhagen is planning SCMs to cope with the increased precipitation. Ac-
cording to the climate adaptation plan, adopted in 2011, the solutions shall include both
surface solutions and cloudburst pipes. In order to compensate for the 30% increased
precipitation, the Municipality of Copenhagen aims to disconnect 30% of the impervi-
ous areas from the drainage system (Københavns Kommune, 2011a). In the cloudburst
management plan from 2012, Copenhagen is divided into seven catchment areas, based
on elevation alone. The deﬁned service level requires that the future cloudburst manage-
ment system can manage a 100-year rain event with a maximum water level of 10 cm
on roads (except on cloudburst roads) (Københavns Kommune, 2012). The detailing of
cloudburst management, which was ﬁnalised in 2014, divides the seven catchments into
60 cloudburst branches, and describes the adaptation plans in more detail (Københavns
Kommune, 2015).
Approximately 300 projects are planned to be implemented over the next 20 - 30 years,
including four cloudburst pipes aiming to discharge stormwater into the harbour. The
cloudburst pipes are expected to be completed within the next 7 - 9 years (Københavns
Kommune, 2012, 2015). High risk areas, where severe ﬂooding has occurred, are of high-
est priority. SCMs of the inner city of Copenhagen have therefore already begun. Sankt
Kjelds is selected as the climate neighbourhood of Copenhagen, where several climate
adaptation projects have been implemented (Københavns Kommune, 2015).
6
==PAGE:16==
2
Theory
Diﬀerent types of solutions are planned on surface level, such as cloudburst roads, re-
tention roads, retention spaces and green roads. By standardising the solutions, the cost
of implementation will be reduced. The Municipality of Copenhagen has carried out a
socio-economic assessment for the planned SCMs.
As mentioned earlier, if no climate
adaptation is made, the cost of damage due to ﬂooding is estimated to be 16 billion DKK
during the next 100 years. Upgrading the drainage system, so it can manage the predicted
increased precipitation, is estimated to cost about 20 billion DKK. The total construction
cost for the planned SCMs is estimated to 11 billion DKK, based on prices for 2015. This
indicates that climate adaptation combining both surface and subsurface solutions is the
cheapest alternative (Københavns Kommune, 2015).
There are though some diﬃculties related to the use of surface solutions. Runoﬀthat
is disconnected from the drainage system evaporates, inﬁltrates or is discharged to lakes,
streams or the sea.
The EU Water Framework Directive restricts the inﬁltration and
discharge, in order to protect the quality of groundwater and recipients. In Copenhagen
diﬀerent bodies of water are considered important to protect. This includes the harbour,
the groundwater and Utterslev Mose among others (Københavns Kommune, 2016b). The
runoﬀshall be treated diﬀerently depending on the types of surfaces it has been in con-
tact with. Roads are for example considered more contaminated than roofs (Københavns
Kommune, 2016b). However, no general limits have been deﬁned for the quality and the
amount of water being discharged. Instead, limits are deﬁned for each individual case. If
treatment is needed diﬀerent approaches can be used, e.g. inﬁltration through engineered
soil or ﬁrst ﬂush, where the initial runoﬀis led to the drainage system (Københavns
Kommune, 2016b). One issue regarding pollution is the large amounts of salt applied on
roads during winter, making the runoﬀsaline. The saline runoﬀis not allowed to inﬁl-
trate, as it might pollute the groundwater. Saline and freshwater runoﬀshall therefore be
treated separately (Københavns Kommune, 2015). Besides the restrictions, the conductiv-
ity is generally low in Copenhagen, which inhibits the inﬁltration capacity. Furthermore,
there are several contaminated sites in Copenhagen, where inﬁltration shall be avoided
(Københavns Kommune, 2016b).
The SCMs in Copenhagen requires cooperation between the municipalities, utility compa-
nies, homeowner’s associations and citizens. The Municipality of Copenhagen is planning
to involve diﬀerent stakeholders, including citizens, from an early stage of the projects
(Københavns Kommune, 2015). This is likely to increase the accountability and support
from the citizens (Refsgaard et al., 2007; Walker et al., 2003).
The responsibility of the Greater Copenhagen Utility (HOFOR) is to map the drainage
system, identify where there is a risk of ﬂooding, and to ensure that the solutions are
made in accordance to the service level set by the Municipality of Copenhagen. The Mu-
nicipality of Copenhagen identiﬁes areas in the climate adaptation plan where LIDs will
be useful and they give authorization for inﬁltration and discharge to recipients (HOFOR,
2016a). The Secretariat for Water Supplies is a governmental secretariat in Denmark.
The secretariat needs to approve the adaptation projects and deﬁnes the upper limit of
charges on water and wastewater. The cloudburst pipes are ﬁnanced by HOFOR through
water charges, approved by the Secretariat for Water Supplies.
The surface solutions
are planned to be ﬁnanced by the municipalities and by private co-ﬁnancing projects
(Københavns Kommune, 2015).
7
==PAGE:17==
2
Theory
2.1.3.1
Co-Financing Scheme
As described above, the plan is to disconnect 30% of the impervious area from the drainage
system in Copenhagen. This also includes private areas owned by e.g. homeowner’s as-
sociations. It is estimated that about one third of the areas intended for disconnection
are private areas. It is therefore necessary that the utility companies cooperate with the
landowners in order to disconnect the suﬃcient amount of areas (Københavns Kommune,
2015). The co-ﬁnancing scheme from 2013 enables utility companies to co-ﬁnance invest-
ment and operational costs for projects on areas owned by municipalities or on private
owned areas. The project is still owned and administrated by the landowner, who has
to ﬁnance the project e.g. by obtaining a loan. During the following 10 - 25 years the
instalments and interest rate are paid by the utility company, ﬁnancing the projects with
the income from wastewater charges. Until the end of 2015 it was possible to achieve
100% co-ﬁnancing. The level has since been reduced to a maximum of 75% of the total
investment costs. However, operational costs can still be 100% co-ﬁnanced (Konkurrence-
og Forbrugerstyrelsen, 2015).
In order to obtain co-ﬁnancing, an agreement between the project owner and the util-
ity company needs to be made. This agreement needs to be approved by the Secretariat
for Water Supplies. There are several requirements for a project to be co-ﬁnanced. The
project shall manage runoﬀand needs to be carried out in/on roads, streams or recre-
ational areas. This does not include inﬁltration on private areas. The project shall be
more cost-eﬀective than a traditional solution with pipes or basins reaching the same ser-
vice level. Furthermore, only installations with a hydraulic purpose are co-ﬁnanced. This
excludes modernization, recreational value, unnecessary expensive materials etc. In these
cases the extra cost shall be ﬁnanced by the project owner. Furthermore, the project needs
to reduce the risk of ﬂooding in the project area or in downstream areas (Konkurrence-
og Forbrugerstyrelsen, 2015; Naturstyrelsen, 2015).
The co-ﬁnancing scheme is very relevant for homeowner’s associations who want to adapt
their neighbourhood to the increased precipitation.
HOFOR and the Municipality of
Copenhagen have great interest in climate projects on the large number of private roads
in the city. They therefore encourage homeowner’s associations in Copenhagen to use
the possibility to co-ﬁnance projects that can reduce the amount of rainwater entering
the drainage system (HOFOR and Københavns Kommune, 2015). Several projects have
been approved for co-ﬁnancing (Naturstyrelsen, 2016). In the future more co-ﬁnancing
projects are expected to be approved and carried out with homeowner’s associations. In
December 2015, about 60 homeowner’s associations applied for 100% co-ﬁnancing with
HOFOR, where about 30 projects got approved. Many of the homeowner’s associations
who applied, wanted to manage runoﬀfrom the entire impervious area, including roofs.
HOFOR does however not co-ﬁnance transportation of runoﬀgenerated on roofs, imply-
ing that runoﬀfrom roofs cannot be managed in solutions placed on the roads (HOFOR,
2016b). Several of the applications were therefore rejected by HOFOR. Furthermore, the
maximum cost accepted by HOFOR, is 500 DKK per disconnected m2 (HOFOR, 2016b).
Some of the applications went above this limit, which also resulted in rejections.
Two homeowner’s associations, Trekanten and Utterslevhøj, are analysed as case studies
in this project. These are chosen in collaboration with HOFOR, as they are both located
in Copenhagen and applied for co-ﬁnancing in December 2015, where they were rejected.
Trekanken is described in more detail in Section 3.6 and the analysis of Utterslevhøj can
be found in Appendix F. The case studies will be used in order to assess what impact
8
==PAGE:18==
2
Theory
bioretention units can have on the hydrology in residential areas. Furthermore, the case
studies will be used to test the applicability of MIKE URBAN and the WSUD-potential
tool and to compare their performance.
2.2
Models
2.2.1
Advanced Modelling Tools
Storm Water Management Model (SWMM) is a widely used advanced modelling tool for
analysis of various urban water processes. It is a free software program provided by US
EPA. The ﬁrst version of the tool was developed in 1971 (US EPA, 2015). SWMM is con-
tinuously improved, and since 2009 it is possible to model the eﬀects of LIDs in urbanised
areas (US EPA, 2009).
MIKE URBAN, developed by DHI, is an ArcGIS-based interface for several engines
(MOUSE, SWMM and MIKE 1D) used for urban water modelling.
MIKE 1D is the
most recent developed engine, gathering the collection system in MOUSE with the river
simulation in MIKE 11. The new MIKE 1D is thereby intended to replace MOUSE and
MIKE 11 in the future. However, it is still quite new and needs more testing to fully
replace the older and more known engines. The simulation in MIKE 1D is divided into
several modules, including rainfall-runoﬀand pipe ﬂow (DHI, 2016a). Furthermore, since
2016 it is possible to add diﬀerent LIDs in the MIKE 1D model.
In both SWMM and MIKE URBAN diﬀerent models can be used to describe the pro-
cesses in the system. Only rainfall-runoﬀmodels are included in this project. Diﬀerent
rainfall-runoﬀmodels are brieﬂy described in the section below, followed by a more detailed
presentation of the model used in this project.
2.2.1.1
Rainfall-RunoﬀModels
In MIKE URBAN two diﬀerent kinds of hydrological models, surface runoﬀmodels and
continuous hydrological models, can be applied in order to describe rainfall-runoﬀ. Con-
tinuous hydrological models are suitable for long-term simulations in undeveloped areas,
as it includes sub-surface runoﬀand takes previous rain events into account (DHI, 2016b).
The surface runoﬀmodels on the other hand only consider overland ﬂow, where runoﬀonly
depends on the current rain event, resulting in discontinuous runoﬀhydrographs. Surface
runoﬀmodels are useful when simulating runoﬀfrom a single rain event in urbanised areas.
Diﬀerent surface runoﬀmodels can be used in MIKE URBAN: Time/area method (Model
A), kinematic wave (non-linear reservoir, Model B), linear reservoir (Model C) and unit
hydrograph surface runoﬀmodel (UHM).
In Denmark Model A is often used to describe the rainfall-runoﬀin models. However,
when modelling LIDs in MIKE 1D, only the kinematic wave equation (Model B) can be
used. In SWMM a non-linear reservoir model is used to simulate the rainfall-runoﬀ(US
EPA, 2016). This model corresponds to Model B used in MIKE URBAN. Model B is
described in the following section.
2.2.1.2
Kinematic Wave Equation (Model B)
When using Model B for the rainfall-runoﬀmodel, the computation occurs in two steps:
Eﬀective precipitation calculation and hydraulic routing (DHI, 2016c).
The steps are
9
==PAGE:19==
2
Theory
illustrated in Figure 2.3, where the actual precipitation is illustrated with a hyetograph,
wetting and storage are shown as depths and evaporation and inﬁltration are illustrated as
continuous losses. The wetting, storage, evaporation and inﬁltration are subtracted from
the actual precipitation, giving the eﬀective precipitation. Based on hydraulic routing, the
eﬀective precipitation results in runoﬀ, shown in Figure 2.3 as a hydrograph. The steps
and processes are described below.
Figure 2.3: Illustration of Model B (DHI, 2016c). I(t): Actual precipitation, ys: Storage depth,
yw: Wetting depth, IE(t): Evaporation loss, II(t): Inﬁltration loss, Ieff(t): Eﬀective precipitation,
Q(t): Runoﬀ.
Eﬀective Precipitation
The eﬀective precipitation (Ieff(t)) [LT−1] is calculated using Equation 2.1. It is the part
of the precipitation that becomes runoﬀwhen all losses have been subtracted from the
actual precipitation (DHI, 2016c):
Ieff(t) = I(t) −IE(t) −IW (t) −II(t) −IS(t),
where
Ieff(t) ≥0
(2.1)
Where I(t) is the actual precipitation, considered uniform for the entire catchment. IE(t)
is the evaporation loss and II(t) is the inﬁltration loss. Both IE(t) and II(t) are contin-
uous losses. IW (t) is the wetting loss (wetting dry soil) and IS(t) is the surface storage
loss (ﬁlling depressions). Both IW (t) and IS(t) are discontinuous initial losses occurring
in the beginning of a rain event. The losses occur in the order mentioned in Equation
2.1. They all have the unit [LT−1] and none oﬀthe losses can be negative (DHI, 2016c;
Winther et al., 2013). In the model, evaporation and inﬁltration are considered true losses,
whereas wetting and surface storage are storages within the system, which eventually will
end up as losses due to either evaporation or inﬁltration.
If suﬃcient water is available in the system evaporation is assumed equal to the po-
tential evaporation, which depends on site speciﬁc parameters such as temperature and
wind speed (Chow et al., 1988). Inﬁltration starts when the initial loss due to wetting
is completed. The inﬁltration depends on several parameters such as soil porosity, mois-
ture content, groundwater level and storage capacity. Diﬀerent models can be used to
describe the inﬁltration, e.g. Horton or Green-Ampt. Green-Ampt is a physical based
model using Darcy’s law and the parameters required are conductivity, porosity and suc-
tion head (Akan, 1993). Model B in MIKE URBAN uses Horton inﬁltration (IH(t)), see
Equation 2.2, which is an empirical equation describing how the inﬁltration rate decreases
exponentially with time (DHI, 2016c; Winther et al., 2013):
IH(t) = IImin + (IImax −IImin)e−kat
(2.2)
10
==PAGE:20==
2
Theory
Where IImin and IImax [LT−1] is the minimum inﬁltration rate for fully saturated soil and
the maximum inﬁltration rate for dry soil respectively. ka [T−1] is a decay constant for the
speciﬁc soil and t [T] is the time that has elapsed since the beginning of the rain event. In
order to describe the recovery of the inﬁltration capacity, the inverse form of the Horton
equation is used (DHI, 2016c).
The Horton equation is only applicable for rain events with intensity greater than the
inﬁltration capacity. The inﬁltration capacity decreases according to Equation 2.2, inde-
pendent of the amount of water entering the soil. It is possible to take this into account by
using an integrated form of the Horton equation. However, the standard Horton equation
is default in MIKE URBAN (DHI, 2016c) and will be used in this project.
Hydraulic Routing
In Model B the surface runoﬀhydrograph is determined based on the kinematic wave
equation, which is a non-linear reservoir model. Runoﬀis generated as long as the eﬀec-
tive precipitation is positive. The kinematic wave equation calculates the runoﬀas open
channel ﬂow and assumes uniform ﬂow. The runoﬀdepends on gravitation and friction
alone, where the bottom slope and the friction slope is assumed equal. In the kinematic
wave equation local acceleration, convective acceleration and pressure force are neglected,
and backwater eﬀects cannot be described. The runoﬀ(Q(t)) is calculated using Equation
2.3 (DHI, 2016c; Chow et al., 1988):
Q(t) = M · B · I1/2 · yR(t)5/3
(2.3)
Where M [L1/3T−1] is the Manning’s number, I [h] is the slope of the bottom and B [L] is
the width of the ﬂow channel, calculated as the catchment area [L2] divided by the length
[L]. yR(t) [L] is the runoﬀdepth, which is calculated based on the continuity equation
shown in Equation 2.4 (DHI, 2016c):
Ieff(t) · A −Q(t) = dyR
dt · A
(2.4)
Where Ieff(t) [LT−1] is the eﬀective precipitation described in Equation 2.1. A [L2], dt
[T] and dyR [L] are the catchment area, time step and change in runoﬀdepth respectively
(DHI, 2016c).
Catchment Deﬁnition
The surface runoﬀvaries for diﬀerent surfaces. Model B in MIKE URBAN distinguishes
between ﬁve diﬀerent types of surfaces: Steep impervious surfaces (e.g. roofs), ﬂat imper-
vious surfaces (e.g. roads) and pervious surfaces with small, medium or large inﬁltration.
It is up to the user to decide how to divide the area into the diﬀerent catchment types
(DHI, 2016c). In SWMM the catchment is only divided into a pervious and impervious
part (US EPA, 2015). Runoﬀis calculated for each surface type individually and added
to obtain the total runoﬀfrom the catchment. Depending on the surface, diﬀerent losses
will be taken into account. For the pervious surfaces all four losses in Equation 2.1 are in-
cluded, whereas for the impervious surfaces inﬁltration is neglected. Furthermore, surface
storage is not included for steep impervious surfaces in MIKE URBAN (DHI, 2016c).
Low Impact Developments
As a recent development of the MIKE 1D engine, it is possible to model the hydrological
eﬀects of LIDs in urbanised areas. LIDs can be added to the model in two diﬀerent ways.
The ﬁrst possibility is to use the LID controls, where LIDs are added in the rainfall-runoﬀ
11
==PAGE:21==
2
Theory
model as properties to a speciﬁc catchment. In this approach the dimensions and proper-
ties of the LID are deﬁned together with the collecting area. In this project the collecting
area is deﬁned as the area contributing with runoﬀto the LID. The exact location of the
LID is not taken into account and direct connection to the existing drainage system is
not modelled. This approach is highly inspired by the way LIDs are modelled in SWMM,
where LID controls have been included since 2009 (DHI, 2016b; US EPA, 2009). The
second option to implement LIDs in MIKE URBAN is a hydraulic approach, where LIDs
are added as soakaway nodes in the existing drainage system (DHI, 2016b). In this project
only the performance of LID controls in the rainfall-runoﬀmodel will be analysed.
In the LID controls, the simulation of LIDs is based on the water balance, where the
inputs and losses of diﬀerent processes are estimated. Several types of LIDs can be mod-
elled in the rainfall-runoﬀmodel in MIKE 1D and SWMM, including bioretention units,
inﬁltration trenches, porous pavement, rain barrels, vegetative swales, rain gardens and
green roofs (DHI, 2016b; US EPA, 2015).
2.2.1.3
Bioretention Units
This project focuses on diﬀerent designs of bioretention units, referred to as bioretentions.
A bioretention is an inﬁltration unit collecting runoﬀfrom upstream areas.
It has a
vegetated surface layer and can furthermore include a soil layer, a storage layer and a
drain. Figure 2.4 shows a conceptual model of a bioretention including all three layers
and a drain. The ﬁgure also illustrates the processes included in the water balance. The
layers and processes are described below.
Figure 2.4: Conceptual model of a bioretention (Inspired by DHI (2016b) and US EPA (2015)).
Surface Layer
The surface layer is a depression with vegetation and volume to store water before it inﬁl-
trates, evaporates or overﬂows. If water enters the bioretention while the entire capacity
of the surface layer is used, water will run onto the surrounding area. In this project
this is deﬁned as overﬂow, and considered a new event if no overﬂows have occurred the
previous hour. The surface layer is essential in the design, as water enters the bioretention
12
==PAGE:22==
2
Theory
faster than it can inﬁltrate. Furthermore, the depression shall be vegetated to increase the
transpiration from plants. The most simple bioretention only includes the surface layer,
where water is stored before it inﬁltrates directly to the native soil.
Soil Layer
If a soil layer is included in the bioretention design, it often consists of engineered soil.
Engineered soil is a special designed soil with suitable porosity and conductivity, enhanc-
ing the inﬁltration capacity and treatment of the water. If engineered soil is not included
in the bioretention design more contaminants might inﬁltrate into deeper layers and po-
tentially contaminate the groundwater (FAWB, 2009).
Storage Layer
The storage layer increases the capacity of the bioretention. It is a volume with high
porosity (a soakaway), where water can be stored before it slowly inﬁltrates into the na-
tive soil or discharges through the drain.
Drain
A drain can be added to the bioretention, transporting water out of the storage layer into
a drainage system or a recipient. The discharge through the drain is calculated based on
Equation 2.5 (US EPA, 2015):
q = C · hn
(2.5)
Where q [LT−1] is the discharge, h [L] is the water level above of the drain, n [-] is the
drain exponent and C is the drain coeﬃcient. The unit of C depends on the value of
the drain exponent. If no drain is applied in the model, C shall be set to 0. A drain
can be applied in three diﬀerent ways: As an oriﬁce, with maximum draining time or
with maximum ﬂow limit. If the drain is modelled as an oriﬁce, n shall be 0.5 and C is
calculated as 60 000 times the total slot area divided by the area of the LID. If the drain
shall have a maximum draining time, n shall still be 0.5 in order to simulate an oriﬁce,
and C shall be calculated as 2D1/2T −1, where T [T] is the maximum allowed draining
time and D [L] is the height of the bioretention above the drain. In the last option, where
the drain has a ﬂow limit, C shall be set to the ﬂow limit and n shall be 0 (US EPA, 2015).
Besides the parameters used in Equation 2.5, the oﬀset height of the drain shall also
be deﬁned, if a drain is applied in the model. This height aﬀects how much water inﬁl-
trates to the native soil or drains from the storage layer. If there is any volume below the
drain, all the water in the lowest part of the storage layer will eventually inﬁltrate into the
native soil, as it cannot discharge through the drain located above. If the drain is neither
located in the top or the bottom of the storage layer, the storage layer is divided into a
retention part below the drain and a detention part above the drain. Retention will act
as storage and results in inﬁltration to the native soil, whereas detention will reduce and
delay the peak ﬂow. According to Locatelli et al. (2015a) a combination of both retention
and detention gives the best performance of a soakaway.
Water Balance
In the programs, LIDs can only be implemented on initially impervious surfaces. The
inﬂow to the bioretention consists of direct precipitation and runon from the collecting
area, which can only contain impervious surfaces. Once in the bioretention water can
inﬁltrate into the native soil, evaporate or overﬂow to the surrounding area (DHI, 2016b;
US EPA, 2015). Knowledge about the conductivity of the native soil is essential for the
13
==PAGE:23==
2
Theory
performance of the bioretention, which should be dry after a couple of days in order to
be ready for another rain event (Backhaus et al., 2010). It might therefore be ineﬃcient
to construct inﬁltration units in low conductivity areas. Furthermore, inﬁltration can be
reduced with time due to clogging. The engineered soil therefore needs to be renewed
after approximately 20 years (Naturstyrelsen, 2013).
Direct precipitation is deﬁned based on rain data loaded in the model and the area of
the LID. The runon is calculated based on the precipitation on the collecting area. Evap-
oration from the LID is determined by the potential evaporation deﬁned in the model. In
both SWMM and MIKE URBAN the inﬁltration in the LID can be modelled based on
conductivity. In that case the Green-Ampt model is used to calculate the inﬁltration. In
MIKE URBAN it is furthermore possible to model the inﬁltration based on the capacity.
In the capacity based approach, only the porosity, ﬁeld capacity and wilting point is used
in combination with inﬁltration and leakage capacity for a more simple calculation of the
inﬁltration (DHI, 2016b).
Bioretention Designs
As described above, bioretentions can be designed in diﬀerent ways. If a bioretention only
includes the surface layer and soil layer, the design corresponds to the design of a rain
garden in the LID controls in both MIKE URBAN and SWMM. The rain garden has
currently a signiﬁcant limitation in both programs, as the conductivity of the native soil
cannot be speciﬁed when using the conductivity based approach. To avoid this limitation,
bioretentions without a storage layer can still be modelled as a bioretention, by assigning
the storage layer with a minimum height (SWMM Knowledge Base, 2015).
Model Input and Output
In both MIKE URBAN and SWMM many input parameters are required for both the
catchment and the diﬀerent LID layers. For an impervious catchment approximately 7
parameters are required, and for a bioretention about 19 parameters need to be speciﬁed.
After a simulation a summary ﬁle is created. The summary ﬁle shows the accumulated
value for diﬀerent processes.
The processes included in the summary ﬁle from MIKE
URBAN are listed in Table 2.1. Similar results are obtained in SWMM.

subtracting the inﬁltration in the LID from the total loss, which is the method used in this
project. In SWMM the total inﬁltration and total evaporation are reported individually.

In both programs time series, showing the diﬀerent processes during the simulation, can

2.2.2
WSUD-Potential Tool
The WSUD-potential tool, developed in a project run by VTU-Fonden, is a simple and
user friendly tool to evaluate hydrological eﬀects of LIDs in residential areas. The purpose
of the tool is to make rough estimations in the initial planning of LID projects, and to
support the communication between the many diﬀerent stakeholders (Lerer et al., 2015b).
Currently the tool consists of two parts, area deﬁnition in a map (e.g. Google Maps)
and calculations in a spreadsheet. The tool is inspired by the 3PA, and calculates the
return period of overﬂow from LIDs. Furthermore, the water balance is estimated. Three
diﬀerent types of LIDs can currently be evaluated in the tool: Permeable surfaces, de-
tention units and bioretentions (Lerer et al., 2015b). Two types of bioretentions can be
modelled: A simple bioretention only consisting of a surface layer, and a bioretention in-
cluding all three layers and a drain.
The WSUD-potential tool is a new tool, still under development.
It is expected that
future work will result in a coupling of the two parts of the tool, so the calculated results
are shown directly in combination with the map. More types of LIDs will also be included,
and the uncertainty will be analysed. An economic estimation might be included in the
program (Lerer et al., 2015b).
2.2.2.1
Low Impact Development (LID)
A minimum number of parameters need to be deﬁned in the WSUD-potential tool. Table
2.2 lists the input parameters together with the outputs.
For the catchment only the
impervious area and soil conductivity shall be deﬁned. For the LIDs the number of units
and the area of each unit shall be speciﬁed. Furthermore, it shall be deﬁned whether the
LIDs are placed on initially impervious or pervious surfaces. The collecting area of the
LID is assumed only to consist of impervious surfaces. Runoﬀfrom pervious surfaces is
thereby neglected.
Table 2.2: Input parameters and outputs for the WSUD-potential tool.
Inputs
Outputs
Impervious catchment area [m2]
Return period for overﬂow [year]
Hydraulic conductivity of catchment [m/s]
Available volume per event [mm]
Number of LIDs [-]
Annual inﬁltration [%]
Area of each LID [m2]
Annual evaporation [%]
LIDs placed on impervious or pervious catchment area [-]
Delayed [%]
Runoﬀ[%]
The results of the tool include graphs showing the return period of overﬂows and the
water balance. These results are based on a large number of simulations in SWMM per-
formed by Sara Maria Lerer, PhD student at DTU Environment. The simulations were
carried out with a varying ratio between the area of the catchment and the area of the LID
and a varying conductivity of the native soil. This method was based on the assumption
that these are the most inﬂuential input parameters. The simulations resulted in tables
15
==PAGE:25==
2
Theory
indicating the annual evaporation, annual inﬁltration and overﬂow return period for dif-
ferent scenarios, combining seven diﬀerent area ratios and four diﬀerent conductivities.
When estimating the performance of the LIDs in the WSUD-potential tool, the tables
are used as lookup tables, where the result of the combination closest to the parameters
in the speciﬁc project is used. This method adds an uncertainty to the output, as the
result can only be selected from 28 combinations of predeﬁned area ratios and conductivi-
ties, which might be signiﬁcantly diﬀerent from the parameters used in the speciﬁc project.
Figure 2.5 shows an example of the results from the WSUD-potential tool, analysing
a 100% impervious catchment of 1 000 m2, with a conductivity of 1 · 10−6 m/s and a
simple bioretention of 100 m2. The water balance in Figure 2.5a shows how 7% of the pre-
cipitation evaporates and the rest becomes runoﬀbefore the bioretention is implemented.
After implementing the bioretention, the water balance changes drastically and more than
80% inﬁltrates and only 2% becomes runoﬀ. In Figure 2.5b the return period and retained
runoﬀper event can be seen. This graph is inspired by the 3PA, where the three domains
are represented with dotted lines. It can be noted that the investigated example results in
an overﬂow return period of 1 year, with 37 mm retained runoﬀper event. This indicates
that the bioretention will overﬂow approximately once every year, which is between the
day-to-day domain and the technical optimisation domain.
(a) Annual water balance.
(b) Return period of overﬂows.
Figure 2.5: Results from the WSUD-potential tool. The results are based on a 100% impervious
catchment of 1 000 m2, with a conductivity of 1 · 10−6 m/s and a simple bioretention of 100 m2.
X: Without bioretention. A: Bioretention.
2.2.3
Modelling Process
The process of constructing a model consists of several steps, where calibration and val-
idation is one of the steps. Throughout the process uncertainties are introduced to the
model (Refsgaard et al., 2007).
Below, the concepts of calibration and validation are
brieﬂy described, followed by a description of model uncertainty.
2.2.3.1
Calibration and Validation
Calibration and validation of models are important in order to increase and assess the
accuracy of the model outputs. Both calibration and validation require measured data.
During calibration, the input parameters of the model are changed in order for the model
to best reproduce measured data. When validating a model, the accuracy is assessed by
comparing the outputs with measured data, which is diﬀerent from the data used for cali-
bration (Oberkampf et al., 2004; Trucano et al., 2006; Refsgaard et al., 2007). Split sample
approach is a method used for calibration and validation, where one part of a dataset is
used for calibration and the other part is used for validation.
16
==PAGE:26==
2
Theory
Even though a model is successfully calibrated and validated, it is not possible to ar-
gue that the model correctly describes reality. It is only possible to prove if a model does
not adequately reﬂect reality. It shall also be taken into account that measured data is
uncertain and might not be representative. Furthermore, models shall be used with cau-
tion if they are applied outside of the calibrated and validated area (Oberkampf et al.,
2004; Walker et al., 2003).
It is important to diﬀerentiate between a model output being accurate or precise.
A
precise output implies that the values lie within a small range from each other. An ac-
curate result, on the other hand, is a result close to a target value or measured value
(Walker et al., 2003). If no data is available for calibration and validation, a model can
be compared to other models. In this case it is not possible to assess the accuracy of the
model.
2.2.3.2
Uncertainty and Sensitivity
Within the urban drainage ﬁeld the use of modelling tools has increased (Vezzaro et al.,
2013). This has led to increased awareness of the uncertainty of models. Making a model
without any uncertainty is inevitable, as a model is a simpliﬁcation of the reality. Un-
certainties can be reduced, but not eliminated.
It is important to understand where
uncertainties originate from and how they aﬀect the results. Uncertainties can originate
from all steps in the modelling process. Usually the model uncertainty is inconsistently
evaluated when the model is ﬁnalised (Refsgaard et al., 2007; Warmink et al., 2010; Deletic
et al., 2012). However, Refsgaard et al. (2007) argue that the uncertainty shall be assessed
throughout the modelling process. Even though it might not be possible to quantify the
uncertainty, it is important to analyse the uncertainty if decisions are made based on the
model (Refsgaard et al., 2007).
Walker et al. (2003) considers three dimensions of uncertainty: Location, level and nature.
The location determines where in the model the uncertainty is located. Uncertainties can
e.g. originate from the conceptualisation of the model, the algorithms included in the
model, model resolution or the input parameters. The uncertainty level ranges from de-
terministic knowledge to total ignorance. The nature can be characterised as either epis-
temic or variability uncertainty. Epistemic uncertainties are caused by lack of knowledge,
whereas variability uncertainties are caused by inherent variability in the described pro-
cess, such as natural processes or human activities (Refsgaard et al., 2007; Walker et al.,
2003). Warmink et al. (2010) argue that it might in some cases be diﬃcult to correctly
deﬁne the nature of the uncertainty.
Several methods exist for uncertainty analysis. Refsgaard et al. (2007) describes 14 meth-
ods, e.g. expert elicitation, extended peer review, Monte Carlo analysis, multiple model
simulations, uncertainty matrix and sensitivity analysis. A sensitivity analysis investigates
how a model output is aﬀected due to a change in the inputs of the model. Saltelli et al.
(2000) provide following deﬁnition: ”Sensitivity analysis studies the relationships between
information ﬂowing in and out of the model”. The purpose of a sensitivity analysis is to
identify the parameters with the highest inﬂuence on the outputs. This will enable the
modeller to have special focus on the inﬂuential parameters when assigning values to the
input parameters (Saltelli et al., 2000).
17
==PAGE:27==
3
Method and Material
3
Method and Material
Initially the performance of bioretentions in MIKE URBAN is compared with bioretentions
in SWMM. This is considered the best method to test the performance, as no measured
data is available. In order to identify the most inﬂuential parameters of bioretentions
in MIKE URBAN, sensitivity analyses are performed. Based on the knowledge obtained
through the sensitivity analyses, the WSUD-potential tool is updated in order to reduce
the uncertainty caused by the lookup tables. Finally the use of MIKE URBAN and the
WSUD-potential tool is tested by assessing the bioretention potential in two homeowner’s
associations in Copenhagen. All the methods are described in details below, starting with
a description of the base model and input parameters used in the simulations.
3.1
Base Model
Several simulations in both MIKE URBAN and SWMM are evaluated in this project.
Therefore a base model is deﬁned and used as a default in the simulations. The base model
is a 100% impervious catchment area of 1 000 m2 with a slope of 1%. A bioretention of
100 m2 is implemented collecting water from the entire catchment area. Only the rainfall-
runoﬀmodel is considered, using the kinematic wave equation (Model B). This implies
that the bioretention is analysed using the LID controls. The catchment parameters for
the base model can be seen in Table 3.1.
Table 3.1: General catchment parameters for the base model.

The width of the catchment area is calculated as 2 · √area, suggested by Borup (2015).
The wetting and storage are deﬁned based on the default values in MIKE URBAN. ASCE
(1994) suggests using a depression storage of 1.3 - 2.5 mm for impervious surfaces. How-
ever, the MIKE URBAN default value of 0.6 mm was chosen. The roughness for overland
ﬂow on impervious surfaces are based on the Manning number for concrete suggested by
Chow et al. (1988).
When implementing a bioretention in the programs, several parameters need to be de-
ﬁned for the surface layer, soil layer, storage layer, drain and deployment. The parameters
can be seen in Table 3.2, together with the baseline values and ranges deﬁned in this
project. In the base model all three layers are included, but no drain is added. Initial
saturation and clogging are neglected.
It is assumed that a suitable size of a bioretention is 1% - 30% of the catchment area. As
the catchment area in the base model is 1 000 m2, the LID area can thereby vary between
10 - 300 m2, with a width of 1 - 15 m.
