Københavns Kommune (2011b), [10] SVK (2015), [11] Bockhorn et al. (2013).
In the surface layer water can pond before inﬁltrating, evaporating or overﬂowing. Gabriel
et al. (2012) suggest a depth of 30 cm for the surface layer and the Municipality of Copen-
hagen recommends a maximum depth of 15 - 20 cm (Københavns Kommune, 2011c).
Based on this a height of 20 cm and a range from 5 - 30 cm is used. The parameter for
vegetative cover deﬁnes how much volume plants account for in the total volume of the
surface layer. This fraction is assumed to be very small, and is therefore assigned with a
baseline value of 0, and a range of 0 - 0.1. The surface roughness is the Manning’s overland
ﬂow within the bioretention. The range is based on values from Chow et al. (1988) for
light to medium vegetation, ranging from 20 - 30 m1/3/s.
The soil layer consists of engineered soil. The Municipality of Copenhagen recommends
a height of the soil layer of 0.50 - 0.75 m (Københavns Kommune, 2011c). The range of
0.25 - 0.75 m is analysed with a baseline value of 0.50 m. Together the porosity, ﬁeld
capacity and wilting point deﬁne how much water the soil can contain and the amount of
water able to inﬁltrate and evaporate from the soil. US EPA (2015) presents values for
several diﬀerent soil types. The baseline values are chosen to describe loamy sand. The
ranges are deﬁned taking into account that the ﬁeld capacity shall always be higher than
the wilting point and lower than the porosity.
When running the base model in SWMM and MIKE URBAN the conductivity based
inﬁltration is used in the soil layer, as this is the only option in SWMM. This approach
includes the parameters: Conductivity, conductivity slope and suction head. If the con-
ductivity is too low a much larger area of the bioretention is needed for the inﬁltration.
However, if the conductivity is too high contaminants might not be suﬃciently removed
19
==PAGE:29==
3
Method and Material
from the water and plants might not survive. The optimal conductivity depends on the cli-
mate. In temperate climate a recommended value of the conductivity is between 2.8·10−5
- 8.3 · 10−5 m/s (FAWB, 2009). The baseline value is chosen within this interval, however,
a wider range is analysed. The conductivity slope is deﬁned as the slope of the relation-
ship between the logarithmic conductivity and the soil moisture. The values are based on
information in SWMM Knowledge Base (2011), where conductivity slopes of about 5 and
12 are assigned to sand and clay respectively. The baseline value of 5.7 describes loamy
sand. The suction head describes the capillary suction at the wetting front. The values
are based on US EPA (2015), ranging from sand to silty clay, with a baseline value for
loamy sand.
The storage layer is the soakaway part of a bioretention. The height of the storage layer
is assumed to range from 0.20 - 1.5 m. According to SVK (2015) the porosity is 0.95 for
plastic soakaways, which are often used in Denmark. The inﬁltration capacity depends
on the conductivity of the native soil.
The conductivity can vary within a very large
range. According to Chow et al. (1988) the conductivity of clay can range between 10−11
- 10−7 m/s. The geology in Copenhagen mainly consists of boulder clay, which is glacial
deposits that can be highly heterogeneous (Bockhorn et al., 2013). Bockhorn et al. (2013)
tested the conductivity in Høje Taastrup, close to Copenhagen, where the conductivity
was found to vary from about 10−9 - 10−5 m/s. When dimensioning LIDs, the Municipal-
ity of Copenhagen recommends to use a value of 5 · 10−5 m/s if no inﬁltration tests are
made in the area (Københavns Kommune, 2016a). The range found by Bockhorn et al.
(2013) is used as range for the conductivity in this project, with a baseline value of 2·10−7
m/s. The clogging factor is the ratio of clogging in the bottom. Clogging will decrease
the conductivity over time. This is neglected in this project.
3.2
Data Input
In the following the data inputs describing the boundary conditions of the programs are
presented, including evaporation and precipitation.
3.2.1
Evaporation
The potential evaporation time series is based on data from 1989 - 2010 collected by DMI
(2012). Figure 3.1 shows the potential evaporation per month in Denmark. The time
series was made for 1980 - 2014, both as a .dat-ﬁle and .dfs0-ﬁle to be used in SWMM
and MIKE URBAN respectively.
When adding the evaporation time series as boundary condition in MIKE URBAN it
will not automatically be used if Model B is applied. Instead a predeﬁned recovery rate of
1.2 mm/day will be used during dry periods. In order to make the program use the evapo-
ration time series, it is necessary to take a copy of the dhiapp.ini-ﬁle and place the copy in
the working folder. Changes can now be made in the copied .ini-ﬁle, where RUNOFF B
EVAPORATION=OFF shall be switched ON in the section for Model B. This will enable
the use of the evaporation time series and ignore the predeﬁned recovery rate.
20
==PAGE:30==
3
Method and Material
Figure 3.1: Average monthly potential evaporation in Denmark, based on data collected by DMI
(2012) from 1989 - 2010.
3.2.2
Precipitation
Both CDS rain and historical rain series are used in this project. A CDS rain is used
to run simple and short simulations where the results can be analysed easily.
This is
relevant in order to understand the processes and how the models calculate. However, if
the performance of a LID is analysed it is more relevant to use historical rain, as it includes
diﬀerent kinds of rain events and dry periods, providing a more realistic simulation. When
analysing drainage systems with long term simulations (LTS) it is common to ignore dry
periods in order to reduce the simulation time. However, when analysing the performance
of LIDs the dry periods are important in order to model the annual water balance, as
evaporation and inﬁltration are slow processes. When using the historical rain the entire
rain series is therefore used, including the dry periods. The CDS rain and historical rain
used in this project are presented below.
3.2.2.1
CDS Rain
The CDS rain represents a 5-year rain event with a duration of four hours, see Figure 3.2.
The .dfs0-ﬁle used in MIKE URBAN is provided by DHI and the .dat-ﬁle for SWMM is
created so it corresponds to the data used in MIKE URBAN.
Figure 3.2: The 5-year CDS rain.
21
==PAGE:31==
3
Method and Material
3.2.2.2
Historical Rain
The historical rain time series used in this project is based on data from station 5740
Kløvermarksvej in Copenhagen, Denmark, collected by DMI and SVK. The series goes
from 07.01.1979 - 09.12.2015 and is provided by DTU as a .KM2-ﬁle, see Figure 3.3. The
.KM2-ﬁle contains a status line and rain intensities for every rain event. The status line
includes for example the date, time and duration of the rain event and the station number.
The rain intensities are presented for every minute in [µm/s]. A rain event is deﬁned as at
least two tips of the rain gauge within an hour. If there are more than one hour between
two tips, it is deﬁned as two separate rain events (DMI, 2014b).
The .KM2-ﬁle is modiﬁed in MIKE URBAN, and converted to a .dfs0-ﬁle including date,
time and rain intensities [µm/s] for every minute. This data is extracted as a .txt-ﬁle and
saved as a .dat-ﬁle with date, time and rain intensities [mm/h] for every minute. The
.dat-ﬁle is used when running the model in SWMM.
Figure 3.3: Historical rain series from 5740 Kløvermarksvej, 07.01.1979 - 09.12.2015.
Diﬀerent lengths of the time series are used for diﬀerent simulations in this project. In
some cases the 10 years from 01.01.2000 - 01.01.2010 are simulated and in other cases the
30 years from 01.01.1980 - 01.01.2010 are simulated. These two periods are referred to as
10 years rain and 30 years rain respectively. The time series are analysed and compared
to the regional model in order to evaluate if the 10 and 30 years rain are representative.
These analyses are performed using DTU Rain Analyst.
3.3
Comparison of MIKE URBAN and SWMM
The performance of bioretentions in MIKE URBAN is tested by comparing the results
with results from SWMM. When comparing the two models, the base model is simulated
in both programs to investigate how well the two models ﬁt. In order to achieve better un-
derstanding of the models and potential diﬀerences, the initial simulations are made very
simple. The precipitation, runoﬀand evaporation in the catchment are analysed when
applying the CDS rain and excluding the bioretention from the analysis. This analysis is
carried out in order to see if the two models produce similar results before applying the
bioretention. The bioretention is then added and the entire system is modelled with the
30 years rain.
The models are simulated using the baseline values shown in Table 3.1 and 3.2. There are
though some diﬀerences in the parameters to specify in the two programs. The geometry
22
==PAGE:32==
3
Method and Material
of the catchment can be described with length, width and area, where length multiplied by
width gives the area. In SWMM the width and the area of the catchment shall be deﬁned
as input, and in MIKE URBAN the length and area of the catchment is speciﬁed. As the
three parameters are related, it is not considered to cause any diﬀerence in the calculations.
The pore volume of the storage layer is deﬁned in diﬀerent ways in the two models.
In MIKE URBAN the porosity (φ) is deﬁned, whereas in SWMM the void ratio (e) is
deﬁned. The relationship between these two parameters is described by Equation 3.1 (US
EPA, 2015).
φ =
e
1 + e
(3.1)
The porosity can vary from 0 - 1, and apparently the same limitation accounts for the void
ratio in SWMM. As both the porosity and void ratio have to be below 1, it is not possible
to assign the baseline value of 0.95 for the porosity in SWMM, as this would require a
void ratio of 19. Therefore a porosity of 0.44 is used when SWMM and MIKE URBAN
are compared. This corresponds to a void ratio of 0.8. SWMM Knowledge Base (2014)
describes that many users of SWMM assume that void ratio is the same as percentage
voids and deﬁnes the porosity. Furthermore, porosity is a more widely used term within
the ﬁeld of hydrology, compared to void ratio, making porosity more intuitive to specify.
3.4
Sensitivity Analysis
A sensitivity analysis can be carried out in several diﬀerent ways. In this project the
inﬂuence of the bioretention parameters in MIKE URBAN are analysed through a one-
at-a-time (OAT) sensitivity analysis and Monte Carlo sensitivity analyses, using the base
model described in Section 3.1. The methods are described below.
One of the parameters analysed in the sensitivity analysis is the area ratio between the
area of the bioretention and the area of the catchment. In order to analyse the area ratio,
the catchment area is kept constant at 1 000 m2 in all the simulations, and only the area
of the bioretention is changed.
3.4.1
One-at-a-Time Sensitivity Analysis
An OAT sensitivity analysis can be performed in order to identify the most inﬂuential
parameters in a model. An OAT sensitivity analysis ranks the parameters showing a qual-
itative measure of the inﬂuence, but it does not quantify how inﬂuential each parameter
is. Furthermore, an OAT sensitivity analysis does not include possible interactions be-
tween the parameters. It is though an easy and fast method that can identify the most
interesting parameters for further investigations (Saltelli et al., 2000).
In this project the OAT approach is used as a screening sensitivity analysis to test the
inﬂuence of 15 bioretention parameters in the base model in MIKE URBAN. The 15 pa-
rameters are the once where a range is deﬁned in Table 3.2. Baseline values and ranges are
used for all the parameters included in the analysis. Simulations are carried out with 10
years rain, where the parameters are changed one at a time to both the lower and upper
boundary of the range. The base scenario refers to the simulation where all the parame-
ters have been assigned with their baseline value. The outputs from the simulations are
compared to the outputs from the base scenario. The most inﬂuential parameters can be
identiﬁed as the parameters that have the highest impact on the outputs (Saltelli et al.,
23
==PAGE:33==
3
Method and Material
2000). The outputs used to evaluate the sensitivity of the model are the total evaporation,
total inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency from the bioretention. The Matlab script shown
in Appendix A.1 is used to calculate the overﬂow frequency. The Matlab script loads
the surface ﬂow time series from the LID result ﬁle (.dfs0-ﬁle) and counts the number of
overﬂow events. An overﬂow event is considered a new event if no overﬂow has occurred
the previous hour.
3.4.2
Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis
A Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis is a global analysis where several parameters are
changed simultaneously. In this project a Matlab script is developed, in order to auto-
matically perform the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis with MIKE URBAN. The Matlab
script can be seen in Appendix A.2.
3.4.2.1
Multiple Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analyses
Two Monte Carlo sensitivity analyses are performed in this project. Initially the ﬁve most
inﬂuential parameters, found in the OAT sensitivity analysis, are tested in a Monte Carlo
sensitivity analysis, followed by a second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis keeping the most
inﬂuential parameters constant. The second analysis is performed in order to evaluate if
other less inﬂuential parameters are inﬂuential when the most inﬂuential parameters are
not varied.
3.4.2.2
Performing a Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis
A Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis consists of ﬁve steps (Saltelli et al., 2000). Each step
is described below.
Step 1: Deﬁne Parameters, Ranges and Distribution
Initially the parameters included in the analysis are identiﬁed. This can be based on re-
sults of an OAT sensitivity analysis, described in Section 3.4.1. Furthermore, a range and
distribution are deﬁned for each parameter (Saltelli et al., 2000).
In the OAT sensitivity analysis the area ratio, the height of the soil layer, ﬁeld capac-
ity, the height of the storage layer and the conductivity of the native soil are identiﬁed as
the ﬁve most inﬂuential parameters in the model, see Section 4.3.1. The inﬂuence of these
parameters is further investigated in the ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. Uniform
distribution is chosen for most of the parameters, due to lack of information regarding
the distribution. Carsel and Parrish (1988) showed that soil conductivity tends to follow
a log-normal distribution. Therefore a log-normal distribution is selected for the conduc-
tivity of the native soil. The baseline values and ranges shown in Table 3.2 are used in
the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. However, the mean and standard deviation of the
native soil conductivity is deﬁned as 1.94·10−7 m/s and 5.3·10−7 m/s respectively. These
values are used by Locatelli et al. (2015b) to describe silty clay loam, which is based on
the ﬁndings of Carsel and Parrish (1988).
In the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis the area ratio and conductivity of the native
soil is kept constant, as these are identiﬁed as the most inﬂuential parameters in the ﬁrst
Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. The conductivity is set to 2·10−7 m/s, corresponding to
the baseline value. The area of the bioretention is set to 80 m2 given an area ratio of 8%.
This is designed based on a 2-year CDS rain, using a spreadsheet for dimensioning LIDs,
24
==PAGE:34==
3
Method and Material
developed by SVK (2015). The parameters varied in the second Monte Carlo sensitivity
analysis are the height of the surface, soil and storage layer and the porosity and ﬁeld
capacity of the soil layer. All of the parameters are uniformly distributed.
Step 2: Sampling
In the second step of the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis, the speciﬁc parameter values
for each simulation are determined. This can be done more or less random. In this project
latin hypercube sampling (LHS) is used, which is a stratiﬁed sampling method ensuring
coverage of the entire range deﬁned in step 1. LHS can sample parameters so they get
uniform, normal or log-normal distribution. LHS divides the range of a parameter into n
number of intervals, where n is the number of simulations carried out in the Monte Carlo
sensitivity analysis. Finally one random value is selected within each interval, resulting in
n number of values covering the entire range with the deﬁned distribution (Saltelli et al.,
2000). The ﬁnal result of the sampling step is a n x p matrix with n random values for
each parameter (p). This gives n diﬀerent parameter sets used to evaluate the model in
the third step. The LHS procedure is included in the Matlab script.
Step 3: Evaluate Model
Prior to the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis a .m1dx-ﬁle is exported from MIKE URBAN.
This ﬁle is used to perform remote simulations in Matlab. Based on the parameter matrix
deﬁned in step 2, the 5 parameters of interest are changed in the .m1dx-ﬁle for every
simulation. MIKE URBAN is run from Matlab n number of times applying 10 years rain.
As the MIKE URBAN result ﬁles are large, the results of interest are extracted after each
simulation, and the result ﬁles are overwritten in the next simulation. The results used in
the analysis are the evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency from the bioretention.
Step 4: Uncertainty Analysis
The number of simulations (n) shall be suﬃciently large in order to reduce the uncertainty
caused by varying input samples. The necessary size of n depends on the complexity of
the model and the number of parameters varied in each simulation. The size of n can be
analysed by calculating the mean and standard deviation of both inputs and outputs of
the model depending on the size of n (Saltelli et al., 2000).
Previous studies show large variations for the size of n. Locatelli et al. (2015b) made
1 300 simulations in order to analyse 14 parameters for an inﬁltration trench and Vezzaro
et al. (2013) made 10 000 simulations when evaluating 7 parameters in a rainfall-runoﬀ
model. Li et al. (2014) analysed the inﬂuence of 12 parameters in SWMM, based on 1
000 simulations. Lindgreen and Dreier (2014) analysed 5 catchment parameters in MIKE
URBAN and assumed that 400 simulations would be adequate. Taking into account the
previous studies and the simulation time of about 10 minutes for each simulation, it is
decided that 500 simulations are adequate in order to analyse 5 parameters in this project.
Step 5: Sensitivity Analysis
The result of the sensitivity analysis can be analysed in diﬀerent ways. Scatter plot is a
simple and visual method, providing a qualitative understanding of whether a parameter
is inﬂuential and how a change in a parameter will aﬀect the model output (Saltelli et al.,
2000). Several quantitative methods can be used to calculate how inﬂuential the param-
eters are. An often used method is standardized regression coeﬃcients (SRCs) where the
model is assumed linear. This method is applied in the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis
in this project and is included in the Matlab script.
25
==PAGE:35==
3
Method and Material
For SRCs, Equation 3.2 is ﬁtted to the model input (x) and output (y), providing a
regression coeﬃcient (b) (Sin et al., 2011).
y = a
X
i
bi · xi
(3.2)
The SRCs (βi) are calculated using Equation 3.3, taking into account the standard devi-
ation of the input (σxi) and output (σy) (Sin et al., 2011).
βi = σxi
σy
· bi
(3.3)
SRCs can range from -1 - 1, where a value close to -1 or 1 indicates a highly inﬂuential
parameter and a value close to 0 indicates a non-inﬂuential parameter. For a linear model
SRCs thereby provide a standardized measure of the inﬂuence of each input in relation to
each output (Sin et al., 2011).
If the model is not linear, the SRCs are not valid.
The linearity can be analysed by
calculating the coeﬃcient of determination (R2). Saltelli et al. (2006) considers a model
suﬃciently linear if R2 > 0.7. Other methods can be applied to evaluate the sensitivity in
non-linear models (Saltelli et al., 2000). These methods are not used in this project, but
are discussed later in Section 5.2.2.
3.5
Regression Analysis
As described in Section 2.2.2 an uncertainty is added to the WSUD-potential tool as the
eﬀect of the LIDs are based on lookup tables for 28 combinations of seven area ratios and
four conductivities of the native soil. In order to reduce the uncertainty, it is investigated
whether regressions can replace the lookup tables. This is done by performing Monte Carlo
analyses in MIKE URBAN. For this analysis a modiﬁed version of the Matlab script pre-
sented in Appendix A.2 is used.
In the analysis the baseline parameters described in Section 3.1 are used, where only
area ratio and conductivity are varied. The area ratio is varied by keeping the area of the
catchment constant at 1 000 m2 and varying the area of the LID. Four diﬀerent designs of
bioretentions are analysed in order to update the WSUD-potential tool. The designs are
described in Section 3.5.1 below.
The conductivity can vary within a very large range. In order to cover a large range,
each regression is based on results from two separate Monte Carlo analyses. The ﬁrst
Monte Carlo analysis is performed with log-normally distributed conductivity, this will
ensure coverage of the low range of the conductivity. Regressions based only on this anal-
ysis cannot correctly describe the processes when the conductivity is in the high range.
Therefore a second Monte Carlo analysis is performed for each bioretention design, where
the conductivity is uniformly distributed. This increases the amount of results for high
conductivity values, making the regressions ﬁt better for these values. For both Monte
Carlo analyses the area ratio is uniformly distributed.
Each Monte Carlo analysis includes 500 simulations, where the 10 years rain is applied.
This gives 1 000 simulation results for each process in the diﬀerent bioretention designs,
which are used to ﬁt the regressions.
26
==PAGE:36==
3
Method and Material
3.5.1
Bioretention Designs
The four diﬀerent bioretention designs are presented in Figure 3.4. Bioretention A repre-
sents a simple construction only including the surface layer, implying that water inﬁltrates
directly into the native soil. Bioretention B is an enhancement of Bioretention A, as it
includes a soil layer with engineered soil. Bioretention C has in addition to the engineered
soil layer, a storage layer with a soakaway and Bioretention D furthermore includes a drain.
Bioretention A and D correspond to the designs already included in the WSUD-potential
tool, whereas Bioretention B and C are new designs added to the WSUD-potential tool in
this project.
When applying the bioretentions in MIKE URBAN, it would be logical to apply Biore-
tention A and B as rain gardens. Due to the limitation described in Section 2.2.1.3, all
the designs are though implemented as bioretentions. For the designs where some of the
layers are not included, the height of the speciﬁc layer is set to 1 mm.
(a) Bioretention A.
(b) Bioretention B.
(c) Bioretention C.
(d) Bioretention D.
Figure 3.4: The four bioretention designs included in the regression analysis.
The four diﬀerent bioretentions are designed based on the base model described in Sec-
tion 3.1. However, some of the parameters vary depending on the design, see Table 3.3.
Bioretention A does not include the soil layer and the storage layer. The height of these
two layers are therefore 1 mm and the porosity, ﬁeld capacity, wilting point, conductivity
slope and suction head are deﬁned with values for clay loam. Bioretention B does not
include the storage layer. The height of the storage layer is set to 1 mm and the porosity
is deﬁned as for the soil layer. Bioretention C and D are identical to the base model,
however, a drain is included in Bioretention D. The drain is located in the middle of the
storage height, as a combination of retention and detention is recommended by Locatelli
et al. (2015a). This combination is suggested as the retention reduces the annual runoﬀ
due to inﬁltration, and the detention delays and reduces the peak ﬂow. The ﬂow limit is
chosen so it fulﬁls the general guideline, that the discharge to a recipient shall not exceed
1 l/s/ha (Vestergaard, 2016). Equation 3.4 is used to calculate the drain coeﬃcient (C).

3.5.2
Processing Results and Fitting Regressions
The MIKE URBAN results obtained through the Monte Carlo analyses are processed
before ﬁtting the regressions. The area of the bioretention is recalculated to area ratio
indicating the percentage of the catchment area occupied by the bioretentions. Further-
more, log is taken to the conductivity, enabling a better plot of the parameter. During
the Monte Carlo analysis, the evaporation and inﬁltration are calculated as losses in [m3]
from the bioretention alone. These losses are recalculated as percentage losses in the water
balance for the entire catchment. The Matlab script used to process the results is shown
in Appendix A.3.
After the recalculations, the results for the ﬁrst and second Monte Carlo analysis, with
log-normally and uniformly distributed conductivity respectively, are analysed together.
The regressions are found using the curve ﬁtting tool cftool in Matlab, where three dimen-
sional surfaces are ﬁtted to the 1 000 MIKE URBAN results. Area ratio and conductivity
28
==PAGE:38==
3
Method and Material
are the independent variables in the regressions, with evaporation, inﬁltration or overﬂow
frequency as the dependent variable.
Through iterations the cftool estimates the coeﬃcients resulting in the best ﬁt for diﬀerent
degrees of polynomial regressions or customised equations. In the cftool the polynomial
regressions can be up to a ﬁve degree polynomial, regarding each independent variable.
The cftool does also calculates diﬀerent parameters for goodness of ﬁt.
According to Johnson et al. (2011) polynomial regressions are useful if the relation between
the parameters is unknown. This is the case in this project, and polynomial regressions
are therefore used for all the regressions. When choosing a polynomial regression, the
most optimal regression is the one with lowest degree, which can still describe the relation
adequately. The necessary degree can be tested starting with a ﬁrst and second degree
polynomial. If the second degree polynomial improves the ﬁt, a third degree polynomial is
tested to see if it improves the ﬁt. This process is continued until the ﬁt does not improve
(Johnson et al., 2011).
The goodness of ﬁt is evaluated based on plots, the adjusted R2 and root mean square error
(RMSE). The adjusted R2 indicates how well the regression can describe the results from
MIKE URBAN, taking the number of variables included in the regression into account.
The adjusted R2 can range from 0 - 1, where 1 indicates a perfect ﬁt. RMSE evaluates
the average error between the regression and the results from MIKE URBAN. RMSE ≥
0, where 0 is a perfect ﬁt (Montgomery, 2013). For some of the processes two regressions
might be necessary in order to describe the process adequately.
3.5.3
Updating the WSUD-Potential Tool
The WSUD-potential tool is updated, so the performance of the bioretentions is estimated
based on the regressions instead of the lookup tables. Bioretention A replaces the existing
rain garden and Bioretention B and C are new designs added to the tool. Bioretention D
is found to give unreliable results, see Section 4.4.1. No regressions are therefore found for
this design, and the design is not included in the updated WSUD-potential tool.
In speciﬁc situations some of the regressions report negative values. In these cases the
result is reported as 0, as evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency cannot be nega-
tive. For the water balance without bioretentions, the MIKE URBAN result for the base
model is used. In the water balance including bioretentions, the runoﬀis deﬁned as the
excess water when evaporation and inﬁltration is subtracted.
In the following the updated version of the WSUD-potential tool will be referred to as
the new WSUD-potential tool, whereas the tool estimating the LID performance based on
lookup tables, will be referred to as the old WSUD-potential tool.
3.6
Case Study: Trekanten
In this project, the potential of implementing bioretentions is investigated in two dif-
ferent homeowner’s associations in Copenhagen. These are Trekanten and Utterslevhøj
homeowner’s associations. In the end of 2015 both homeowner’s associations applied for
co-ﬁnancing of LID projects with the utility company HOFOR. Besides the analysis of
bioretention performance, Trekanten is used for comparing the diﬀerent modelling tools.
29
==PAGE:39==
3
Method and Material
Trekanten and the methods used for the analysis are described below. The analysis of
Utterslevhøj is not as comprehensive and can be seen in Appendix F.
3.6.1
Land Use
Trekanten is a homeowner’s association in a residential area in Valby, Copenhagen, pre-
sented in Figure 3.5. It was founded in 1898 and covers an area of about 48 000 m2 (∼5
ha). It has a triangular shape and contains the roads Vestre All´e, Nordre All´e and Søndre
All´e.
Figure 3.5: Trekanten homeowner’s association in Valby, Copenhagen. The map is based on an
ArcGIS analysis. The arrows indicate ﬂow directions on the roads.
The land use is analysed in ArcGIS using data available from the Danish Geodata Agency
(2016). The distribution is presented in Table 3.4. The impervious areas (buildings/roofs,
roads and sidewalks) account for approximately 40% and the pervious surfaces (private
gardens) account for the remaining 60%.

3.6.2
Elevation, Geology and Groundwater Level
The potential of implementing bioretentions in the area depends on several things, such
as elevation, geology and groundwater level. The elevation of the roads in Trekanten is
analysed using a tool developed by Septima (2016). The tool gives an elevation proﬁle
based on data from the Danish Geodata Agency. The proﬁles are used to determine the
ﬂow directions in the area, which are indicated in Figure 3.5. More detailed results of the
terrain analysis can be seen in Appendix E. As seen in Figure 3.5 all roads in Trekanten
will contribute with runoﬀto Valby Langgade.
Based on borehole information the geology in the upper 10 m is assumed to consist of
boulder clay, with chalk underneath. Occasionally the boulder clay is mixed with sand
and gravel. The groundwater level has previously been measured to around 4 m above
mean sea level, which is about 3 - 4 m below terrain (GEUS, 2016). Locatelli et al. (2015b)
analysed the impact of the groundwater depth on the performance of inﬁltration trenches
in three types of soil. It was concluded that if the unsaturated depth is greater than 11
- 12 m, the groundwater will not aﬀect the inﬁltration in silty clay loam. This limit is
lower for soils with higher conductivities. As the unsaturated depth decreases, the impact
increases. Since the unsaturated depth in Trekanten is expected to be around 3 - 4 m, the
groundwater is likely to aﬀect, but not prevent, inﬁltration from the bioretentions. The
Municipality of Copenhagen states that for optimal inﬁltration, the groundwater table
shall be at least 1 m below the bottom of the LID structure (Københavns Kommune,
2011b). The measurements of the groundwater level in Trekanten are several years old,
and the groundwater level might have changed since. Furthermore, it might vary through-
out the year.
In the area of Trekanten, there are few contaminated sites in the northern and eastern part
of the homeowner’s association, see Appendix E. The sites show contamination with oil
and benzene in both soil and groundwater (Danmarks Miljøportal, 2016). As inﬁltration
can result in a spreading of contamination, it might not be approved by the municipality
to enhance inﬁltration in the northern or eastern part of the homeowner’s association.
3.6.3
Trekanten in the Climate Adaptation Plan
Trekanten is located in the upstream part of the cloudburst branch G˚asebækrenden in the
Copenhagen West catchment area, see Figure 3.6. G˚asebækrenden covers an area of about
300 ha and has been categorised as an area with a medium risk of ﬂooding. Ten projects
are planned in the area, leading the water south through Valbyparken to the harbour.
The projects include cloudburst pipes, cloudburst roads, green roads, retention roads and
retention spaces. Both Valby Langgade and Søndre All´e are selected to become green
roads in order to retain and store water. This will take the pressure of the downstream
part of the area (Københavns Kommune, 2015). As Trekanten is located in the area of
G˚asebækrenden, it is very relevant to make LIDs in the area.

The location of Trekanten homeowner’s association in the cloudburst branch

3.6.4
Bioretentions in Trekanten
The impact of implementing bioretentions in Trekanten will be evaluated using MIKE UR-
BAN and the old and new WSUD-potential tool. Trekanten is also used for comparing the
performance of the three tools. Furthermore, an analysis of the impervious and pervious
fraction of the catchment is made.
The bioretention designs described in Section 3.5.1 are implemented in this case study.
The bioretentions are applied only on impervious areas in the catchment and the collect-
ing area can only contain impervious surfaces. A 100% impervious catchment is therefore
used for the simulations. The impervious area in Trekanten includes the roofs of build-
ings, roads and sidewalks. This gives a total area of approximately 19 000 m2. As seen in
Figure 3.5, water will ﬂow from the roads in the homeowner’s association towards Valby
Langgade. It is assumed that the bioretentions can be evenly distributed on roads and
sidewalks. In this project the bioretentions are only placed on the roads. This is done in
order not to remove any sidewalks. The roads in Trekanten are approximately 6 m wide.
The minimum width needed for a road lane is 3.5 m (Vejdirektoratet, 2013), resulting in
2.5 m available for bioretentions. The bioretentions deployed in this analysis are 10 m
long, which gives each bioretention an area of 25 m2. It is furthermore assumed that the
32
==PAGE:42==
3
Method and Material
bioretention shall be located with a distance of 15 m from each other in order not to block
the driveways to the houses. Based on the total length of the roads in Trekanten, it was
estimated that it is realistic to implement 28 bioretentions on the roads, with a total area
of 700 m2.
Diﬀerent scenarios are assessed in the analysis of Trekanten, see Table 3.5. As no mea-
sured conductivities are available for Trekanten, scenarios with diﬀerent conductivities are
analysed. The eﬀect of including and excluding the runoﬀgenerated from the roofs is also
evaluated.
The new WSUD-potential tool is furthermore used to investigate the area of the bioreten-
tions needed in Trekanten, in order to obtain a certain overﬂow frequency. This analysis
is carried out for the bioretention designs, for three diﬀerent conductivities and with and
without roofs in the collecting area.

4
Results
4
Results
4.1
Base Model
In order to get an understanding of the processes occurring in the bioretention in MIKE
URBAN and to evaluate the use of diﬀerent lengths of the historical rain series, the base
model, presented in Section 3.1, is analysed. The results are presented below.
4.1.1
Processes in the Bioretention
As described in Section 3.2.2, it is better to evaluate the LID performance based on histor-
ical rain series instead of using CDS rain. MIKE URBAN results are presented in Figure
4.1 and 4.2 showing the processes and water content in the diﬀerent layers when ﬁlling and
emptying the bioretention respectively. The ﬁgures show about six weeks each, extracted
from the 10 years simulation of the base model.
Figure 4.1 shows how the bioretention ﬁlls up.
Initially there is no water in the sur-
face layer and the soil moisture is at the wilting point of 0.047. Therefore no evaporation
occurs. The storage layer is furthermore empty and no water can inﬁltrate. When a rain
event occurs, an inﬂow to the bioretention can be seen. This leads to an increase of the
soil moisture. As soon as the soil moisture gets above the wilting point, evaporation starts
and when it increases above the ﬁeld capacity, water starts to enter the storage layer. As
seen in Figure 4.1, there are many rain events occurring in a short time span, resulting
in accumulating water in the storage layer, until the limit of 1 m is reached. As long as
water is present in the storage layer the inﬁltration rate is constant at 2 · 10−7 m/s (0.72
mm/h), which is the deﬁned conductivity of the native soil. At some point the soil mois-
ture reaches the porosity and water starts to accumulate in the surface layer. When the
surface depth reaches the maximum of 0.2 m, overﬂows occur, as the bioretention cannot
contain more water.
Figure 4.1: Processes and water content in the diﬀerent layers when ﬁlling up the bioretention in
MIKE URBAN. Runon and overﬂow are plotted on the secondary y-axis. The units are adjusted
so the rest of the processes can be plotted together on the primary y-axis.
In this example the most intensive rain events occur in the beginning of the period. As
the bioretention initially is empty, it can manage all the ﬁrst rain events without any
35
==PAGE:45==
4
Results
overﬂows. But since the bioretention does not recover between the rain events it even-
tually overﬂows even though the last rain events are not as intensive as the ﬁrst rain events.
Figure 4.2 shows the emptying of a bioretention.
In the beginning of the period, wa-
ter is contained in the soil and storage layer. The entire capacity of the bioretention is
not used, as the soil moisture is below the porosity of the engineered soil, the storage
depth only reaches about 0.6 m out of the total depth of 1 m and only very little water is
accumulated in the surface layer. Due to evaporation, inﬁltration and few rain events, the
bioretention is slowly emptied. Even though the bioretention is not initially full, it takes
a long time for the bioretention to empty.
Figure 4.2: Processes and water content in the diﬀerent layers when emptying the bioretention in
MIKE URBAN. Runon and overﬂow are plotted on the secondary y-axis. The units are adjusted
so the rest of the processes can be plotted together on the primary y-axis.
This analysis supports the statement that historical rain series are necessary in order
to evaluate the LID performance. Due to the slow processes and long recovery time of
the bioretention, evaluation based on a single CDS rain will not reﬂect the performance
suﬃciently, as it does not take previous rain events or dry periods into account.
4.1.2
Water Balance and Historical Rain
The 10 and 30 years rain are analysed in the DTU Rain Analyst in order to see whether
the periods are representative compared to the regional model. The detailed results of
the analyses are presented in Appendix B. Based on the analyses the two periods are
considered representative and can be used in the simulations.
To evaluate the historical rain series in MIKE URBAN, the base model was run with
the 10 and 30 years rain before and after implementing the bioretention. The water bal-
ances of the analysis are shown in Figure 4.3. It can be seen that the water balance for
10 and 30 years are very similar. Before applying the bioretention, 17% of the water in
the catchment evaporates. As the catchment is 100% impervious, the remaining water
becomes runoﬀ. After implementing the bioretention, the water balance changes so ap-
proximately 74% and 25% inﬁltrates and evaporates respectively. When applying the 10
years rain 1.3% of the water becomes runoﬀ, whereas for the 30 years rain 0.3% becomes
runoﬀ. The overﬂow frequency is 1.8 y−1 for both rain series.
36
==PAGE:46==
4
Results
As the results are very similar when using the 10 and 30 years rain, mainly the 10 years
rain is used for the rest of the simulations in this project. This reduces the simulation
time signiﬁcantly compared to simulations where the 30 years rain is applied.
Figure 4.3: Annual water balance for the base model in MIKE URBAN, applying 10 and 30 years
rain, with and without bioretention. X: Without bioretention, Base: With bioretention
4.2
Comparison of MIKE URBAN and SWMM
When comparing MIKE URBAN and SWMM the initial simulations are run with the
5-year CDS rain excluding the bioretention. The results are shown in Figure 4.4 and 4.5.
Figure 4.4 shows the precipitation and runoﬀin MIKE URBAN and SWMM. It can be
seen that the precipitation and runoﬀare identical in the two programs.
Figure 4.4: Precipitation (top graphs) and runoﬀ(bottom graphs) in MIKE URBAN and SWMM,
when applying the 5-year CDS rain.
Figure 4.5 shows the evaporation and precipitation in MIKE URBAN and SWMM for the
CDS rain. In MIKE URBAN the evaporation stays at the level deﬁned in the evaporation
time series loaded in the program, until there is no more water left to evaporate. The
evaporation in SWMM occurs with the deﬁned level only during the rain event. After
the rain event the evaporation decreases to a lower level. This decrease is also observed
when running the historical rain. It is not possible to ﬁnd any explanation for this decrease.
As seen in Figure 4.5, evaporation occurs in a long period after a rain event. This supports
the importance of include dry periods in the simulation of LID performance, as stated in
Section 3.2.2.

Table 4.1 shows the results for the base model applying 30 years rain in both SWMM
and MIKE URBAN. The precipitation is the same in the two models. The total losses
have a relatively low diﬀerence of about 0.2% in 30 years. However, the distribution in
the losses are very diﬀerent, as the evaporation in MIKE URBAN is about 500 m3 higher
than in SWMM and the inﬁltration is nearly 500 m3 lower.

than in MIKE URBAN. The diﬀerence between evaporation and inﬁltration can be due
to the reduction of evaporation observed in SWMM, leaving more water for inﬁltration.

the bioretention accounts for the entire inﬁltration in the catchment. The total input to
the bioretention is approximately 15 000 m3 for both models, the value from SWMM is
3.4% higher. Furthermore, 16% more runoﬀis produced in SWMM compared to in MIKE
URBAN. SWMM has both more initial and ﬁnal storage. The overﬂow frequency is 3.6
and 3.1 y−1 for SWMM and MIKE URBAN respectively, which is relatively similar.

4.3
Sensitivity Analysis
4.3.1
One-at-a-Time Sensitivity Analysis
The results of the OAT sensitivity analysis for the outputs evaporation, inﬁltration and
overﬂow frequency, including 15 bioretention parameters in MIKE URBAN, are presented
in Figure 4.6. Based on the analysis area ratio and conductivity are identiﬁed as the most
inﬂuential parameters, as they show the highest impact on the outputs.
Figure 4.6: Result [%] of the OAT sensitivity analysis. Changes larger than 50% are written in
the graph. lb: Lower boundary, ub: Upper boundary.
39
==PAGE:49==
4
Results
Area ratio highly inﬂuences all three outputs. When the area ratio is assigned with the
lower boundary value, inﬁltration and evaporation are reduced with 87% and 26% respec-
tively and the overﬂow frequency is increased with 9 200%. When the upper boundary
of the area ratio is used, the inﬁltration reduces with about 16%.
This is due to the
evaporation increases with more than 50%. Furthermore, no overﬂows occur, implying a
reduction of overﬂow frequency with 100%.
When applying the low value for conductivity of the native soil nearly no inﬁltration
occurs, resulting in an increased overﬂow frequency of 9 400%. When the high conduc-
tivity is simulated, the bioretention does not overﬂow. However, the inﬁltration is only
slightly increased with 1.5%.
Despite the area ratio and the conductivity, the height of the soil layer, the ﬁeld capac-
ity and the height of the storage layer are also identiﬁed as some of the most inﬂuential
parameters. The surface width of the LID and the roughness of the surface layer did not
show any inﬂuence on the three outputs.
4.3.2
Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis
4.3.2.1
Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis with the ﬁve most Inﬂuential Pa-
rameters
The input parameters for the ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis include area ratio, soil
height, ﬁeld capacity, storage height and conductivity of the native soil. The ﬁrst four pa-
rameters are uniformly distributed and the conductivity follows a log-normal distribution.
The distributions of the input parameters, SRCs and R2 can be seen in Appendix C.1.1
Figure 4.7 shows the results for evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency for the
ﬁve parameters. The scatter plot shows how the area ratio is very inﬂuential, especially
for the evaporation, but also for the two other outputs. The evaporation increases for
increasing area ratio. The relation looks linear, which is supported by the results of the
SRCs, see Appendix C.1.1. For the relation between area ratio and evaporation, SRC and
R2 are 0.99 and 0.98 respectively, indicating that the parameter is highly inﬂuential with
a linear regression. When the area ratio increases the inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency
both decreases. For these relations, R2 are though below the threshold of 0.7.
The conductivity does also show signs of being inﬂuential. The conductivity has SRCs of
0.41 and -0.35 for inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency respectively, which can be a slight
indication that this parameter is inﬂuential. However, R2 is below 0.2 for both situations,
and linearity can therefore not be assumed for the relations. In Figure C.2, Appendix
C.1.1, two other plots of the results for conductivity are presented.
The ﬁrst plot is
zoomed in on the low range of the conductivity, in order to see the results better. In
second plot, the conductivity is plotted on log scale. This shows that the conductivity
is highly inﬂuential to inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency. It is though not inﬂuential to
evaporation.
For the remaining input and output combinations, R2 is much lower than the thresh-
old of 0.7. This is supported by the scatter plot, where no relation for the remaining three
parameters can be observed. Even though area ratio and conductivity are identiﬁed as
the most inﬂuential parameters, it is not possible to argue that the other three parameters
are not inﬂuential.
40
==PAGE:50==
4
Results
Figure 4.7: Scatter plots for the ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. Aratio: Area ratio. h, soil:
Height of soil layer. FC, soil: Field capacity of soil layer. h, storage: Height of storage layer. K:
Conductivity of native soil.
4.3.2.2
Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis with Constant Area Ratio and Con-
ductivity
The results for the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis, where the most inﬂuential pa-
rameters are kept constant, are presented in this section. The ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity
analysis indicates that area ratio and the conductivity is the most inﬂuential parameters.
In the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis surface height, soil height, soil porosity,
ﬁeld capacity and storage height are varied. All ﬁve parameters are uniformly distributed.
Figure 4.8 shows the results for the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. The dis-
tributions of the input parameters, SRCs and R2 are shown in Appendix C.1.2. It can be
seen from the scatter plot that the ﬁeld capacity seems to be inﬂuential regarding evap-
oration. When the ﬁeld capacity increases the evaporation increases, as water can only
inﬁltrate when the soil moisture is above the ﬁeld capacity. If the ﬁeld capacity increases,
more water will therefore evaporate. R2 for the relation between ﬁeld capacity and evap-
oration is 0.53, which is below the threshold for a linear regression. This can also be seen
in the scatter plot in Figure 4.8, where the relation does not look linear.
The storage height is inﬂuential for both inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency. When the
storage height increases the inﬁltration increases and the overﬂow frequency decreases.
This is due to the increased capacity in the bioretention. R2 for the storage height is
higher than the threshold of 0.7 for both inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency, indicating
that the linear regression can be applied.
The scatter plot does though show slightly
curved relations.
The SRCs are 0.88 and -0.90 for inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency
respectively. This corresponds to the positive and negative relations seen in the scatter
plot. Based on the SRCs, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency are nearly equally sensitive
towards changes in the storage height.
41
==PAGE:51==
4
Results
Figure 4.8: Scatter plots for the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. h, surface: Height of
surface layer. h, soil: Height of soil layer. φ, soil: Porosity of soil layer. FC, soil: Field capacity
of soil layer. h, storage: Height of storage layer.
The remaining three parameters do not seem to be inﬂuential based on this analysis. The
result of the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis reveals inﬂuential parameters that
cannot be identiﬁed in the ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. In the ﬁrst Monte Carlo
sensitivity analysis the highly inﬂuential parameters probably hide the inﬂuence of less
inﬂuential parameters.
4.4
Regression Analysis
4.4.1
Analysis of Bioretention D
The four bioretention designs presented in Section 3.5 are supposed to be analysed in
order to ﬁnd regressions used to update the WSUD-potential tool. The third and fourth
design, Bioretention C and D, are very similar. The only diﬀerence is the drain included
in Bioretention D. It is expected that the drain will change the water balance compared
to the water balance for Bioretention C, as some of the water will discharge through the
drain instead of inﬁltrating. Furthermore, it is assumed that the evaporation will be lower
for Bioretention D than Bioretention C, as Bioretention D will empty faster, giving the
water less time to evaporate.
However, when running diﬀerent simulations with the drain in MIKE URBAN, unex-
pected results are observed. Table 4.2 shows the results reported in the summary ﬁle from
MIKE URBAN, when analysing the catchment of Trekanten implementing Bioretention
C and D. Using Equation 3.4, the drain coeﬃcient is calculated to be 9.8 mm/h based on
an area of the bioretention of 700 m2.
42
==PAGE:52==
4
Results
Table 4.2: Output [m3] reported in the summary ﬁle from MIKE URBAN, with 10 years rain.

The summary ﬁle, available after a simulation in MIKE URBAN, shows the accumulated
values of in- and outﬂows of both the entire catchment and the LID alone. As seen in
Table 4.2, the balance is close to 0 for both catchment and LID. This accounts for both
Bioretention C and D, and shows that the inﬂows equal the outﬂows, as intended.
The summary ﬁle states that the total loss in the catchment is the sum of evaporation
and inﬁltration. As the catchment is 100% impervious, the inﬁltration in the bioretention
accounts for all the inﬁltration in the catchment. This implies that the evaporation for the
whole catchment can be calculated by subtracting the inﬁltration from the total loss. This
is how the total evaporation shown in Table 4.2 is calculated. Looking at the values, it can
be seen that the evaporation for the catchment is much bigger in Bioretention D than in
Bioretention C. However, the evaporation within the bioretention is lower for Bioretention
D than for Bioretention C. It is unrealistic that the evaporation in the catchment is so
much higher after adding the drain.
Including a drain shall result in smaller runoﬀ. However, according to the summary ﬁle,
the runoﬀfor the catchment is larger for Bioretention D than for Bioretention C. This can
be explained if the discharge from the drain is included in the runoﬀfrom the catchment.
However, the discharge from the drain (94 400 m3) is signiﬁcantly larger than the total
runoﬀ(84 900 m3) in the catchment. The drain discharge can therefore not be included in
the runoﬀ. One possibility is that the discharge from the drain is transported back to the
catchment, where the water might increase the evaporation or ﬂow into the bioretention
once more. This explanation is though not considered suitable, and is not supported by
the documentation of the program, as it does not describe the fate of the drain discharge.
As no explanations can be found for neither the increased evaporation nor the increased
runoﬀ, it is decided not to include Bioretention D in the further analyses of the project.

==PAGE:53==
4
Results
4.4.2
Regressions for the Bioretention Designs
In this section the results of the regression analysis are described.
Using polynomial
regressions, acceptable ﬁts are found for Bioretention A, B and C. The regressions are all
three dimensional surfaces with area ratio and conductivity as independent variables and
either evaporation, inﬁltration or overﬂow frequency as dependent variable. An example of
the regressions is shown in Figure 4.9, where the regressions for inﬁltration in Bioretention
A are presented. The dots represent MIKE URBAN results used for the ﬁtting. The
detailed results including plots of the other regressions, residuals and the equations for
the regressions are presented in Appendix D.
(a) Low conductivity range.
(b) High conductivity range.
Figure 4.9: Inﬁltration for Bioretention A. The surfaces show the regressions for the total inﬁl-
tration in the catchment depending on area ratio (Aratio) [%] and conductivity of native soil (K)
[m/s]. The dots represent results generated in MIKE URBAN. Black results are included in the
ﬁtting, whereas grey results are excluded from the ﬁtting.
When ﬁtting polynomials to the MIKE URBAN results, the evaporation can be adequately
described with one regression for the entire range of both area ratio and conductivity. This
is the case in all three bioretention designs. In order to describe inﬁltration and overﬂow
frequency it is however necessary to use two regressions, one for the low conductivity range
(5 · 10−9 - 1 · 10−7 m/s) and another regression for the high conductivity range (1 · 10−7
- 4 · 10−5 m/s). This can be seen in Figure 4.9, where Figure 4.9a and 4.9b show the re-
gression for the low and high conductivity range respectively. In both cases the black dots
indicate the results used for ﬁtting, whereas the grey dots are results excluded from the
ﬁtting. If only one regression is used, it cannot adequately ﬁt the MIKE URBAN results.
A more detailed description of the need for two regressions are presented in Appendix D.4,
where the results of using single regressions are shown.
For all processes the regressions can be used within the ranges of 1% - 30% and 5 · 10−9
- 4 · 10−5 m/s for area ratio and conductivity respectively. The adjusted R2 ranges from
0.96 - 0.99 and the RMSE ranges from 0.57 - 5.4. The lowest RMSE is generally found
for evaporation, whereas the regressions for overﬂow frequency have the highest RMSE.
Looking at the residuals, shown in Appendix D, the evaporation is generally ﬁtted very
well, with residuals reaching approximately 3%.
The residuals for inﬁltration reaches
about 10% - 15% and the overﬂow frequency has residuals up to about 25 y−1. The com-
plexity of the regressions varies. The simplest regressions consist of 3 terms, but most of
the regressions consist of 14 - 15 terms. The simplest regressions are found for evaporation.
44
==PAGE:54==
4
Results
Figure 4.10 shows an overview of the regression results. For simplicity the results are
presented in two dimensional plots, for ﬁxed conductivities. However, the results are the
same shown in the three dimensional plots in Appendix D. The ﬁgure shows the evapora-
tion, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency for Bioretention A, B and C plotted against area
ratio for four diﬀerent conductivities. The conductivities include two conductivities in the
low range and two conductivities in the high range. Both the regressions showing the low
and high conductivity range are thereby represented in the ﬁgure.
Figure 4.10: Two dimensional illustration of the three dimensional regressions, showing results
for four conductivities (K) and varying area ratio (Aratio).
For the three designs, the evaporation increases for increasing area ratio. This is expected,
as a larger area ratio implies a larger surface area of the bioretention, where water can be
stored and have time to evaporate. The evaporation regressions for Bioretention B and C
are linear surfaces consisting of 3 terms, whereas the regression for Bioretention A consists
of 9 terms. For the simple design, Bioretention A, the conductivity has an impact on the
evaporation. This eﬀect cannot be seen for the other two designs. This is due to the small
capacity of Bioretention A, and that water inﬁltrates directly into the native soil. The
highest evaporation can therefore be seen for the lowest conductivity and lowest evapo-
ration for the highest conductivity, as more water will inﬁltrate for high conductivities.
The regressions for Bioretention B and C are almost identical. This can be explained as
45
==PAGE:55==
4
Results
water evaporates from the surface and soil layer. Since these two layers have the same
dimensions in Bioretention B and C, the results for evaporation are very similar.
As expected the inﬁltration increases for increasing conductivity.
In general the inﬁl-
tration also increases for increasing area ratio. However, in Bioretention B and C the
regressions for the three highest conductivities shown in the ﬁgure, starts to decrease after
a certain point. This might be due to the increasing evaporation, as the sum of evapo-
ration and inﬁltration can maximum reach 100%. Most of the regressions consist of 14
terms, where the best ﬁt is found for Bioretention A, followed by Bioretention B.
The regressions for the overﬂow frequency include 9 - 15 terms, where the best ﬁt is
found for Bioretention A and B. As seen in Figure 4.10 the overﬂow frequency decreases
for increasing area ratio. As the area ratio increases the capacity of the bioretention in-
creases.
With higher capacity, the bioretention can better manage the water and will
overﬂow less frequent. Furthermore, the overﬂow frequency decreases for increasing con-
ductivity, as more water will inﬁltrate.
For the three highest conductivities shown in the ﬁgure, the overﬂow frequency slightly
ﬂuctuate around 0 for high area ratios. This is incorrect, and is caused by the use of
polynomial regressions. Even though the results obtained in MIKE URBAN show over-
ﬂow frequency of 0 for a large part of the tested parameter combinations, the polynomial
regressions cannot describe the ﬂat surface, which is observed from the MIKE URBAN
results, see the plots of overﬂow frequency in Appendix D. The same ﬂuctuation can be
observed in the inﬁltration regressions for the two highest conductivities shown in Figure
4.10. This is also due to a ﬂat surface that cannot be correctly described with the poly-
nomial regressions.
In order to avoid ﬂuctuation of the overﬂow frequency, it is investigated, based on the
MIKE URBAN results, which combinations of area ratio and conductivity that give 0
overﬂows.
The combinations are presented in Appendix D.3.
These combinations are
taken into account when updating the WSUD-potential tool, decreasing the uncertainty
caused by the polynomial regression.
4.5
Comparison of Models
4.5.1
Old vs. New WSUD-Potential Tool
As Bioretention D is excluded from the regression analysis, Bioretention A is the only
design included in both the old and the new WSUD-potential tool. In order to compare the
two tools, Bioretention A is analysed with a catchment area of 1 000 m2 and a bioretention
area of 100 m2. Two scenarios are analysed, one with a conductivity of 1 · 10−7 m/s (K1)
and one with a conductivity of 2 · 10−7 m/s (K2).
The results can be seen in Figure
4.11, together with the water balance for a catchment before implementing bioretentions,
denoted X. The ﬁgure also shows the return period of overﬂows for K2 based on the new
WSUD-potential tool. The return periods for the three other analyses are too low to be
visual in the graph (T = 0.01 y).
46
==PAGE:56==
4
Results
(a) Old WSUD-potential tool.
(b) New WSUD-potential tool.
(c) A(K2) in the new WSUD-potential tool.
Figure 4.11: Annual water balance from the old and new WSUD-potential tool when analysing a
conductivity of 1 · 10−7 m/s (K1) and 2 · 10−7 m/s (K2), together with the overﬂow return period
and the amount of runoﬀretained per rain event for the new WSUD-potential tool with K2.
X: Without bioretention. A: Bioretention A.
In the new WSUD-potential tool the water balance for the catchment without bioretention
(denoted X in Figure 4.11), is based on results from MIKE URBAN. For this analysis the
base model without bioretention is run in MIKE URBAN, applying 10 years rain. 17% of
the water evaporates, and the rest is runoﬀ. No inﬁltration is observed as the catchment is
100% impervious. In the old WSUD-potential tool the water balance is based on a similar
simulation in SWMM, where only 7% evaporates. The lower observed evaporation in the
old WSUD-potential tool might be explained by the decrease in evaporation observed in
SWMM, see Section 4.2.
Looking at the results from the old WSUD-potential tool, it can be seen that the wa-
ter balances are identical for the two diﬀerent conductivities. In the old WSUD-potential
tool, the response is only deﬁned for four diﬀerent conductivities (1·10−5, 1·10−6, 1·10−7
and 1 · 10−8 m/s). The result is selected based on which of the four conductivities that
are closest to the analysed conductivity. The two conductivities used in this analysis are
both closest to 1·10−7 m/s, resulting in the same output in the old WSUD-potential tool.
The water balances obtained in the new WSUD-potential tool, are diﬀerent for the two
diﬀerent conductivities. As expected more water inﬁltrates for the highest conductivity.
Furthermore, the water balance for the low conductivity is fairly similar in the old and
new WSUD-potential tool. The evaporation is 7% and 15% and the inﬁltration is 42%
and 43% for the old and new WSUD-potential tool respectively.
The overﬂow return period shown in Figure 4.11 is 0.04 years and corresponds to 7 mm
runoﬀretained per event. It can be seen that the bioretention is dimensioned to cope
with rain events smaller than the day-to-day domain in the 3PA, which is indicated by
the green dotted line.

==PAGE:57==

Results
4.5.2
WSUD-Potential Tool vs. MIKE URBAN
Figure 4.12 shows results for Bioretention A, including results from both MIKE URBAN
and the old and new WSUD-potential tool. The ﬁgures to the left show the evaporation,
inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency, plotted against area ratio. For these results the con-
ductivity is kept constant at 1 · 10−7 m/s. To the right, the three processes can be seen
with a constant area ratio of 10% and a varying conductivity.
(a) Constant conductivity of 1 · 10−7 m/s.
(b) Constant area ratio of 10%.
Figure 4.12: Evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency in a catchment with Bioretention
A. Includes results from both the old and the new WSUD-potential tool and MIKE URBAN (MU)
evaluated with 10 years rain. The overﬂow return period in the old WSUD-potential tool is changed
to overﬂow frequency in order to compare the results.
It can be noted that the results from MIKE URBAN and the new WSUD-potential tool
are similar. This is because the regressions are based on the results obtained in MIKE
URBAN. However, there are some places where the regressions over- and underestimates
compared to the results from MIKE URBAN. This is especially valid for low conductivities
for evaporation, where the regression overestimates compared to MIKE URBAN, and for
high area ratios for inﬁltration, where the regression underestimates.
Looking at the results for the old WSUD-potential tool, it can be seen how the curve
jumps due to the seven predeﬁned area ratios and four conductivities. The uncertainty in
the old WSUD-potential tool can be reduced using interpolation. In this way jumps are
avoided and a more smooth result is obtained.
It can be seen that for varying area ratio the evaporation starts at a lower level in the old
WSUD-potential tool than in the other two tools. This is also observed in Figure 4.11,
48
==PAGE:58==
4
Results
and is explained by the decrease in evaporation after a rain event in SWMM, which is
not observed in MIKE URBAN, see Section 4.2. The evaporation increases slightly faster
in the old WSUD-potential tool compared to the new WSUD-potential tool and MIKE
URBAN. Despite the jumps in the curves for the old tool, the results for inﬁltration
are fairly similar for all three models when varying area ratio. The overﬂow frequency
in the old WSUD-potential tool is constant at 100 overﬂows per year up until an area
ratio of 15%, where it decreases to 50 overﬂows per year. The results for the other two
models both show that the overﬂow frequency smoothly decreases for increasing area ratio.
To the right in the ﬁgure the results are shown for varying conductivity. The results in
the old WSUD-potential tool are deﬁned for four diﬀerent conductivities. It can be noted
that the evaporation is constant at 15% independent of the conductivity. The results for
the inﬁltration follow the same tendency as the results from the new WSUD-potential tool
and MIKE URBAN. However, as the result in the old WSUD-potential tool only increases
in few sudden steps, the inﬁltration is constant for large ranges of conductivity. The same
is valid for overﬂow frequency, where the value only changes once.
For the new WSUD-potential tool, it can be seen that the two regressions for low and
high conductivities are well connected, as the gap is relatively small where they meet at
1 · 10−7 m/s. This accounts for both inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency.
4.5.3
Comparison of the Bioretention Designs
In the following, the three bioretention designs are compared by looking at results from
the new WSUD-potential tool and MIKE URBAN. For the simulations in MIKE URBAN,
the 10 years rain and the baseline parameters are used. The values for area ratio and
conductivity are 10% and 2 · 10−7 m/s respectively, corresponding to the base model.
Figure 4.13 shows the water balance for the three bioretention designs, together with the
water balance for the baseline catchment without bioretention (denoted X in the ﬁgure).
(a) MIKE URBAN.
(b) New WSUD-potential tool.
Figure 4.13: Annual water balance for the three bioretention designs (A, B and C), and the base
model without bioretention (X). Includes results from both the new WSUD-potential tool and MIKE
URBAN evaluated with 10 years rain.
The results are very similar in the two tools. Comparing the water balances for the three
designs, the results are as expected. Bioretention A produces most runoﬀ, as this design
has the smallest capacity. Bioretention C has the lowest runoﬀand the highest inﬁltration,
as this design can contain most water. The amount of runoﬀgenerated in Bioretention B
is nearly right between the runoﬀgenerated for Bioretention A and C. Compared to the
catchment where no bioretention is implemented, more water evaporates when a biore-
49
==PAGE:59==
4
Results
tention is present. The evaporation in Bioretention B and C is slightly larger than in
Bioretention A, as water in the soil layer in Bioretention B and C can also evaporate.
The overﬂow frequency is 27, 15 and 8 y−1 for Bioretention A, B and C respectively
in new WSUD-potential tool. Similar results are found in MIKE URBAN, with overﬂow
frequencies of 27, 17 and 1.8 y−1 for Bioretention A, B and C respectively. As expected
Bioretention A has the highest overﬂow frequency and Bioretention C the lowest, as these
designs have the smallest and largest capacity respectively.
4.5.4
Area Analysis with the New WSUD-Potential Tool
Based on the new WSUD-potential tool, the needed area ratio is estimated, if speciﬁc
overﬂow frequencies shall be obtained. The result of the analysis is shown in Table 4.3.
In the table the necessary area ratio can be found for four diﬀerent return periods and
for three diﬀerent conductivities. The result is provided for the three diﬀerent designs of
bioretentions. The area ratio can be used to calculate the total area of bioretentions if
the size of the total collecting area is known. Table 4.3 can therefore be useful for e.g.
homeowner’s associations considering climate change adaptations. Based on the results
in the table they can evaluate what is realistic in the speciﬁc area and what bioretention
design to implement. This does though require that they know the collecting area and the
conductivity of the native soil.
As expected the necessary area ratio increases for increasing return period, as a larger
bioretention is necessary to reduce the frequency of overﬂows. Furthermore, the area ratio
increases for decreasing conductivity, as less water can inﬁltrate into low conductivity soil,
resulting in a need for larger bioretentions. Table 4.3 also shows that larger bioretentions
are needed when the design of the bioretention gets more simple, e.g. Bioretention A shall
be bigger than Bioretention B and C in order to obtain the same level of performance.
This is expected as the capacity decreases for a more simple design. This is also supported
by the result shown in Figure 4.13, where more runoﬀis generated when the design gets
more simple.

The return periods presented in this analysis include 0.2, 1, 5 and 10 years. As a com-

4.6
Case Study: Trekanten
4.6.1
Catchment Analysis
Figure 4.14 shows the water balance for Trekanten running diﬀerent simulations applying
10 years rain in MIKE URBAN. The values presented in Table 3.6 are used. In the ﬁrst
simulation the catchment is deﬁned based on the land use distribution, where 40% and
60% are impervious and pervious respectively. For the other simulations, the catchment
is divided into the pervious and impervious parts, which are analysed separately.
When pervious surfaces are included in the catchment, it is not possible to distinguish
between evaporation and inﬁltration based on the summary ﬁle. The results are therefore
based on the .res1d-ﬁle, which is analysed using the Matlab script presented in Appendix
A.4.
For the 100% impervious catchment, Figure 4.14 shows results based on both the .res1d-
ﬁle and the summary ﬁle. This is presented in order to compare the results from the two
ﬁles.
Figure 4.14: Annual water balance for the entire catchment of Trekanten homeowner’s associa-
tion, together with the impervious (Imp.) and pervious (Perv.) parts separately. No bioretention is
applied. The results are based on simulations in MIKE URBAN with the 10 years rain. All results
are based on the .res1d-ﬁle, except the bottom simulation, which is based on the summary ﬁle.
As expected the pervious part has the highest loss, due to inﬁltration in the pervious
areas and increased storage depressions. It can be noted that there is about 20% runoﬀ
generated at the pervious areas, which is a fairly high amount.
In the impervious catchment, where the evaporation is calculated from the .res1d-ﬁle,
only 5% evaporates. However, when the result is based on the summary ﬁle, 17% evapo-
rates. This is due to unexplainable inconsistencies between the results obtained from the
.res1d-ﬁle and the summary ﬁle. The results originate from the same simulation and shall
therefore give the same result. The summary ﬁle is furthermore based on the results in
the .res1d-ﬁle.

4.6.2
Including Roofs
Figure 4.15 and Table 4.4 show the water balance and overﬂow frequency for the scenarios
including roofs in Trekanten. A 100% impervious catchment is analysed with both a low
(2 · 10−7 m/s) and high (1 · 10−5 m/s) conductivity and for the three bioretention designs.
Results are shown for 10 years simulations in MIKE URBAN and the new WSUD-potential
tool.
(a) Low K, MIKE URBAN.
(b) High K, MIKE URBAN.
(c) Low K, new WSUD-potential tool.
(d) High K, new WSUD-potential tool.
Figure 4.15: Annual water balance for Trekanten including roofs in the collecting area. The results
are based on simulations in MIKE URBAN running 10 years rain and the new WSUD-potential
tool. Low K: 2 · 10−7 m/s, High K: 1 · 10−5 m/s.
It can be seen that the results obtained in MIKE URBAN and in the new WSUD-potential
tool are fairly similar. As expected, a higher conductivity results in less runoﬀand more
inﬁltration. Furthermore, the water balances for the three designs are quite similar for the
scenario with low conductivity, where between 40% and 55% becomes runoﬀ. This is due
to the inﬁltration capacity already reached in Bioretention A. Adding the engineered soil
and storage layer only increases the volume of the bioretention, but it does not change the
inﬁltration capacity to the native soil. However, a small decrease in runoﬀcan be seen
when the complexity of the design increases. This trend is more obvious for the high con-
ductivity, where a larger diﬀerence between the designs can be observed. The evaporation
does not change much, neither for the diﬀerent designs nor for the two conductivities.
The overﬂow frequencies show the same trend as observed for the runoﬀin the water
balances, where the overﬂow frequency is highest for Bioretention A and smallest for
Bioretention C. Comparing MIKE URBAN and the new WSUD-potential tool, the over-
ﬂow frequencies in the two programs have the same magnitude. It can though be seen
that for the low conductivity, MIKE URBAN shows higher overﬂow frequencies, whereas
for the high conductivity the WSUD-potential tool gives higher overﬂow frequencies.

4.6.4
Area Analysis
Based on the result presented in Table 4.3, it is estimated how large the bioretentions in
Trekanten shall be in order to reach a certain return period of overﬂows. The result can
be seen in Table 4.6.
In Section 3.6.4 it is estimated that it will be realistic to implement up to 28 bioretentions
in Trekanten, each with an area of 25 m2. This gives a total area of bioretentions of 700
m2, corresponding to 3.7% or 7.4% of the total catchment if the roofs are included or
excluded respectively. In Table 4.6 the black and grey color indicates if the area is below
or above the estimates limit. As the limit is based on rough estimations, areas slightly
above the limit are also considered realistic.
54
==PAGE:64==
4
Results
If Trekanten wants the bioretentions to manage runoﬀfrom both roads, sidewalks and
roofs, the bioretentions need a design similar to Bioretention C, as too large areas are nec-
essary for Bioretention A and B. However, Bioretention C can only manage this amount
of runoﬀif the conductivity is around 1 · 10−5 m/s or higher. All of the three bioretention
designs need an unrealistic large area if the conductivity is in the magnitude of 1 · 10−7
m/s. This accounts both for scenarios including and excluding roofs in the collecting area.
If roofs are excluded from the collecting area, and if the conductivity is about 1 · 10−6
m/s or higher, it is realistic to implement Bioretention C and reach return periods up to
10 years. Bioretention B can only reach a return period of 10 years if the conductivity
is around 1 · 10−5 m/s or higher, where for a conductivity of 1 · 10−6 m/s it is possible
to reach a return period of 0.2 years. If Bioretention A is implemented it is only realistic
to reach a return period up to 1 year, requiring that the conductivity is approximately
1 · 10−5 m/s or higher.

shown in Table 4.3 the runoﬀfrom the roof can easily be managed in the garden. For the
most demanding scenario, where Bioretention A is implemented in a low conductivity soil
(1 · 10−7 m/s), a 10 year return period of overﬂows can be achieved with a bioretention
area of about 30 m2 (Aratio = 23%). This can be implemented in the 350 m2 large garden
as one big bioretention or several smaller bioretentions.
