4.6.5
Reﬂection on Case Studies
When considering climate adaptation in residential areas several aspects and possibilities
need to be taken into account. The possibilities depend for example on the access to a
nearby recipient. Trekanten is surrounded by residential areas, without any access to a
recipient. Water discharged through a drain will therefore have to be discharged into the
drainage system. On the contrary, Utterslevhøj is located close to Utterslev Mose. Biore-
tentions with drain can therefore advantageously be used in Utterslevhøj, as the drain
water can be discharged in Utterslev Mose.
Discharging water into Utterslev Mose is
though restricted, due to the risk of contamination (Københavns Kommune, 2016b).
Another important consideration is elevation.
In hilly residential areas, high elevated
houses rarely have problems with ﬂooding, whereas low lying houses are more easily
ﬂooded. Furthermore, enhanced inﬁltration can result in increased groundwater table.
This poses a risk of subsidence damage of houses.
This risk has to be assessed when
planning inﬁltration units (Københavns Kommune, 2016b).
In December 2015 both Trekanten and Utterslevhøj applied for co-ﬁnancing of LID projects
with HOFOR. Both homeowner’s associations have previously experienced ﬂooding from
terrain and drainage. Mitigation of ﬂooding was therefore the primary motivation for LID
projects in both associations. Through contact with representatives from both Trekanten
and Utterslevhøj, it is possible to assess interests and concerns for the residents in the
areas. Prior to the application, implementation of LIDs was discussed in Utterslevhøj
homeowner’s association. The general opinion is that a LID project shall be a good solu-
tion for all the residents, not just move the problem downstream to low laying houses. In
the association there is also a concern for the risk of subsidence damage. Furthermore, the
project shall be ambitious and eﬀective, managing runoﬀfrom nearly 100% of the impervi-
ous surfaces (Utterslevhøj homeowner’s association, 2016). Besides mitigation of ﬂooding,
Trekanten homeowner’s association is also interested in the recreational value LIDs can
add to their neighbourhood, and the possibility to renovate the roads when implementing
LIDs. Currently cars park along the roads in Trekanten. In order not to reduce the possi-
bility of parking, Trekanten is interested in permeable pavement (Trekanten homeowner’s
association, 2016).
Both applications from Trekanten and Utterslevhøj look very similar, as they are devel-
oped with the same consulting company. Both applications include the entire impervious
area in the collecting area of the LIDs. The applications are based on calculations using
the old WSUD-potential tool. No inﬁltration tests were carried out in relation to the ap-
plications and a conductivity of 1·10−6 m/s was used. The aim for Trekanten is to manage
a 1-year rain event with LIDs, whereas Utterslevhøj wants to manage a 3-year rain event.
As the calculations are based on the old WSUD-potential tool, it is a rough assumption,
that a overﬂow return period obtained in the tool will correspond to the return period of
the rain. The estimations in the old WSUD-potential tool are furthermore very rough,
especially for overﬂow return period, see Figure 4.12.
In Trekanten the total impervious area was estimated to be 15 400 m2 (Trekanten home-
owner’s association, 2015). In order to manage all the runoﬀgenerated on this area, it
was estimated that bioretentions, corresponding to the design of Bioretention D, shall
be applied on 896 m2, in order to manage a 1-year rain event (Trekanten homeowner’s
association, 2015). In this project, the most similar scenario analysed, is when applying
Bioretention C, where the necessary bioretention area is 1 180 or 588 m2 when including
56
==PAGE:66==
4
Results
or excluding roofs in the collecting area respectively, see Table 4.6. Bioretention C does
though not include a drain, and the calculations are therefore not directly comparable.
Both the application from Trekanten and Utterslevhøj was rejected by HOFOR. The home-
owner’s associations want to manage runoﬀfrom the entire impervious areas. HOFOR
can though not ﬁnance transportation of runoﬀfrom roofs onto the roads. The applica-
tions also included implementation of permeable pavement, which made the projects too
expensive. HOFOR does though still have an interest to implement LIDs in the areas of
Trekanten and Utterslevhøj.
As mentioned earlier, Søndre All´e in Trekanten and Valby Langgade are both selected
to become green roads in the climate adaptation plan of Copenhagen. They are thereby
intended to retain and store water. Looking at the ﬂow directions in Trekanten, see Figure
3.5, it can be seen that both Vestre All´e and Nordre All´e will lead the water north to Valby
Langgade. Søndre All´e transports the water east to Valby Langgade. It might be more
beneﬁcial to make Vestre All´e a green road, as it also receives water from Nordre All´e.
It is though not possible to ﬁnd the argumentation for selecting Søndre All´e and Valby
Langgade in the climate adaptation plan.
In this project, the analysis of the two homeowner’s associations shows similar results.
It is though important to emphasise that several assumptions have been made. First of all
the conductivity of the native soil is not measured. However, it inﬂuences the inﬁltration
capacity signiﬁcantly. It is furthermore a very uncertain parameter that can vary greatly.
Another important assumption is the fact that the area of the homeowner’s associations
is considered as isolated catchments. This is a signiﬁcant simpliﬁcation as water is likely
to ﬂow in and out of the area, depending on elevation. Based on the sensitivity analysis
carried out in this project, these two assumptions are considered highly inﬂuential to the
result, as conductivity and area ratio showed the highest inﬂuence on the bioretention
performance.
If the homeowner’s associations still consider climate adaptation, it is recommended that
they perform conductivity tests in the area to determine the local inﬁltration capacity. If
possible, it is also recommended that all impervious surfaces are included in the collecting
area of the bioretentions. A 5-year return period of overﬂow is considered acceptable as
this is the service level for separate systems (Københavns Kommune, 2008). It is suggested
that this is achieved by managing roof runoﬀin bioretentions in the individual gardens.
Depending on the conductivity, one of the three designs can be implemented. It is though
recommended that either Bioretention B or C is used on the roads, as Bioretention A does
not treat the water. If bioretentions are only implemented on the roads, it might not be
possible to obtain an overﬂow return period of 5 years. In that case it is recommended
to also implement bioretentions on sidewalks. Alternatively Bioretention D can be imple-
mented, where a drain is included in the design. Utterslevhøj does also have access to
a green area, Enemærket. This area can advantageously be used to detain runoﬀduring
large rain events, which is also the intention according to the climate adaptation plan in
Copenhagen (Københavns Kommune, 2015).
57
==PAGE:67==
5
Discussion
5
Discussion
5.1
Performance of the Advanced Modelling Tools
The LID controls in SWMM and MIKE URBAN are very similar in the way they are
constructed.
In the following the similarities, assumptions and limitations of the two
advanced modelling tools are discussed, with focus on the bioretention.
5.1.1
Excluded Parameters
Throughout this project the clogging factor in the storage layer and initial saturation are
neglected. As a period of 10 years is analysed, the initial saturation is not considered to
have a signiﬁcant impact on the results, as it will only aﬀect the results in the beginning
of the simulation.
According to the Municipality of Copenhagen soakaways shall be washed periodically,
due to reduced conductivity caused by clogging (Københavns Kommune, 2011b). Clog-
ging of the storage layer might therefore be relevant to take into account in the model.
Several studies have shown that clogging occurs in the engineered soil. Coustumer et al.
(2009) showed how the conductivity in bioﬁlters signiﬁcantly decreases over time and Lind-
sey et al. (1992) found that after 4 years of operation the inﬁltration capacity in the LID
was reduced by 60% due to clogging. In a project by Orbicon (2015) it was noticed that
the inﬁltration capacity in the engineered soil was substantially higher in the beginning
of the project. To avoid clogging, maintenance of the system is crucial (Bergman et al.,
2011). Naturstyrelsen (2013) recommends that the engineered soil shall be renewed after
approximately 20 years. It is not possible to deﬁne clogging of the soil layer in neither
SWMM nor MIKE URBAN. Neglecting clogging leads to an overestimation of the LID
performance. In order to take clogging of the engineered soil into account, the conductiv-
ity can be assigned with the rate, which is expected after several years of performance.
As the drain in MIKE URBAN did not always show reasonable results, see Section 4.4.1,
the drain is not analysed further in this project. It would though be interesting to test
the inﬂuence of applying a drain to a bioretention. Furthermore, this project does not
account for the expected increased precipitation caused by climate change. The analyses
in this project might therefore overestimate the future performance of the bioretentions,
as increased precipitation will result in more frequent overﬂows.
5.1.2
Area and Location of LIDs
In the modelling programs it is not speciﬁed where in the catchment the LIDs are situated.
When adding LIDs, the area of the LIDs is subtracted from the impervious area of the
catchment, implying that LIDs only can be implemented on impervious areas. This is a
simpliﬁcation, as LIDs might in reality be implemented on pervious areas. Furthermore,
the location of the LIDs is of big importance. The distance between the LIDs has an
eﬀect, as they shall collect water from individual areas. If many inﬁltration units are
implemented close to each other, it can result in increased groundwater level, which af-
fects the inﬁltration capacity (Locatelli et al., 2015b). It is also important to analyse the
elevation in the catchment, as the LIDs shall be situated downstream in order to collect
water from surrounding areas.
58
==PAGE:68==
5
Discussion
When applying LID controls in both SWMM and MIKE URBAN, the number of LIDs
and the area of each LID have to be speciﬁed. However, in this project it is found that
the results are identical if several small LIDs are implemented compared to one big LID,
as long as the total area of the LIDs and the total collecting area are the same. It can be
argued that this can confuse the user, who might spend time calculating how many LIDs
to implement, when the total area of LIDs actually is suﬃcient.
In both programs the collecting area of the LID must be smaller than the impervious
area of the catchment. This implies that no runoﬀis expected to be generated from the
pervious part of the catchment. Model B, which is applied in both SWMM and MIKE
URBAN, takes runoﬀfrom the pervious surfaces into account. The runoﬀgenerated on
pervious surfaces will though not be directed to the LID. This can be due to the assump-
tion that pervious areas only generate runoﬀin periods with intense or frequent rain. In
these situations the entire capacity of the LID is already used for managing the runoﬀ
from the impervious surfaces.
It can be noted that the water balance for the pervious part in the catchment of Trekanten
shows that 20% of the annual precipitation becomes runoﬀ, see Section 4.6.1. This is a
relative high amount of water, which is neglected as inﬂow to the LID. Other studies have
shown that runoﬀis generated from pervious areas (Boyd et al., 1993). However, the
amount of runoﬀgenerated, depends on the inﬁltration capacity, soil moisture and rain
fall intensity, making it very diﬃcult to estimate. Nielsen (2016) argues that runoﬀfrom
pervious areas can be problematic, and identiﬁes three scenarios that are critical. The ﬁrst
critical scenario occurs if the upper soil layer is saturated and the rain intensity exceeds
the saturated hydraulic conductivity of the soil. The second critical scenario occurs if the
water table is close to the terrain, as the soil cannot contain more water. The ﬁnal critical
scenario occurs if the soil is dry, as dry soil has a much lower conductivity than moist
soil. A current project, run by VTU-Fonden, aims to improve the understanding of runoﬀ
generation from pervious surfaces. The project includes continuous measurements of e.g.
soil moisture and runoﬀ(Nielsen, 2016). As runoﬀfrom pervious areas is very site speciﬁc
and uncertain, it is diﬃcult to evaluate whether the runoﬀestimated in MIKE URBAN
is realistic.
5.1.3
Summary File
The summary ﬁle retrieved after a simulation in SWMM gives the amount of water that
evaporates and inﬁltrates in the catchment, whereas in MIKE URBAN only the total loss
in the catchment and the evaporation and inﬁltration in the LID are reported. When
analysing LIDs the water balance is of great importance and in order to analyse the
hydrology of a catchment, the distribution between evaporation and inﬁltration is essential.
If a 100% impervious catchment is analysed in MIKE URBAN, the distribution can be
calculated based on the summary ﬁle, as the inﬁltration occurring in the LID accounts
for the total inﬁltration in the catchment. The total evaporation is then the total loss
subtracting the inﬁltration in the LID. The calculation based on the summary ﬁle cannot
be used if pervious surfaces are included in the catchment, as inﬁltration also occurs
outside of the LID. In this case the distinction between evaporation and inﬁltration in the
catchment can only be estimated based on the detailed catchment result ﬁle (.res1d-ﬁle).
Depending on the time steps and length of the simulation, the .res1d-ﬁle can be very large
and heavy to process. As described in Section 4.6.1 the results based on the .res1d-ﬁle and
59
==PAGE:69==
5
Discussion
the summary ﬁle, do not show the same results regarding evaporation. This is unexpected,
as the summary ﬁle should be a summary of the .res1d-ﬁle.
5.1.4
Evaporation
There are some issues when applying an evaporation time series in the advanced modelling
programs. In SWMM the evaporation decreases after a rain event. This is not observed
in MIKE URBAN. An explanation for the decreasing evaporation in SWMM is not found
in this project.
In MIKE URBAN, the evaporation time series is only used in the simulation if some
of the default settings in the .ini-ﬁle are changed. This is not very intuitive, and users
of the program might wrongly assume that the loaded time series is used. If the default
.ini-ﬁle is used in MIKE URBAN, no evaporation occurs during rain events and in dry
periods a predeﬁned recovery rate of 1.2 mm/day is applied. The evaporation time series
used in this project has a mean value of 1.7 mm/day, where it reaches the lowest level of
0.2 mm/day during the winter and a maximum of 3.5 mm/day during the summer period.
The default recovery rate will therefore overestimate the evaporation during winter and
underestimate during summer. The recovery rate is however close to the mean value of
the time series, but it will in general underestimate the evaporation. Using the recovery
rate seasonal variation is not taken into account. As intensive rain events usually occur
during summer, it is considered important to include seasonal variation of evaporation.
The time series used in this project deﬁnes one average evaporation rate for each month
in Denmark. The time series does thereby not include daily variations, even though the
evaporation is lower during the night. However, this is not expected to change the results
signiﬁcantly as average evaporation rates are used. The inﬂuence of using diﬀerent evapo-
ration rates could though be assessed through a sensitivity analysis, which is not included
in this project.
The old and the new WSUD-potential tool estimate diﬀerent evaporations for a catchment
without LIDs, where the old tool estimates 7% evaporation and the new tool estimates 17%
evaporation, see Section 4.5.1. The calculations in the old and the new WSUD-potential
tool are based on SWMM and MIKE URBAN simulations respectively. The lower evap-
oration in SWMM has previously been explained by the decrease in evaporation after a
rain event. Brown and Borst (2015) measured the evaporation from impervious asphalt
in New Jersey, which is comparable to a Danish scenario with temperate climate. They
found that 5% of the precipitation evaporated. This corresponds best with the results
estimated by SWMM.
The evaporation for the new WSUD-potential tool is calculated from the summary ﬁle
from MIKE URBAN, as described in Section 2.2.1.3. The catchment analysis of Trekan-
ten showed that a signiﬁcantly lower evaporation is obtained in the .res1d-ﬁle compared
to evaporation based on the summary ﬁle, see Section 4.6.1. The evaporation based on
the .res1d-ﬁle corresponds better to the results in SWMM and the ﬁeld measurements by
Brown and Borst (2015). It is therefore uncertain whether the summary ﬁle reports the to-
tal losses of the catchment correctly. Another possibility is that MIKE URBAN might not
simulate dry periods correctly, as it is mostly used to assess the performance of drainage
systems during rain events. This can though not explain the diﬀerent results obtained in
the .res1d-ﬁle and the summary ﬁle. The evaporation in the new WSUD-potential tool
might be overestimated, as it is based on the summary ﬁle.
60
==PAGE:70==
5
Discussion
5.1.5
Inﬁltration
In SWMM diﬀerent kinds of inﬁltration models, such as Horton inﬁltration and Green-
Ampt, can be applied for the pervious surfaces. In MIKE URBAN only Horton inﬁltration
can be used when Model B is applied. However, the inﬁltration from the LID to the native
soil does not follow any of these inﬁltration models. Instead the conductivity to the native
soil is deﬁned as a constant inﬁltration rate. This can be seen in Section 4.1.1, where the
conductivity to the surrounding soil is constant at 2 · 10−7 m/s (0.72 mm/h), whenever
there is water in the storage layer. This inﬁltration occurs independent on the saturation
of the native soil, which is a rough simpliﬁcation. The saturation of the native soil will in
reality vary, aﬀecting the inﬁltration capacity.
When simulating the inﬁltration in the soil layer of LIDs, MIKE URBAN can either
use a capacity based or conductivity based method. In SWMM only the conductivity
based inﬁltration can be applied. For a rain garden in MIKE URBAN, water in the soil
layer inﬁltrates directly into the native soil, as no storage layer is included. Using the
conductivity based method, saturation of the soil layer is taken into account. The inﬁl-
tration to the surrounding soil can though not be deﬁned. The capacity based method
does, on the other hand, not include saturation of the soil layer, but the inﬁltration to the
surrounding soil shall be deﬁned. If a rain garden is modelled, it is therefore important
to keep in mind, that both the capacity and conductivity based method have limitations.
This is also observed in SWMM (SWMM Knowledge Base, 2014). In order to avoid these
limitations, the bioretention can advantageously be used instead. All the designs in this
project are therefore made as bioretentions, with minimal height (1 mm) of the layers
that are not included. There might be some consequences of deﬁning a minimal height
of the layers, instead of excluding them. To minimize the impact, the layers, which are
not included, are made as similar as possible to neighbouring layers. However, the extra
layers might aﬀect the results if they work as barriers, making the water move through the
layers more slow. This could have been analysed by comparing results from a simulation
with a rain garden and a bioretention.
5.2
Sensitivity Analysis
5.2.1
One-at-a-Time and Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis
The sensitivity analysis performed in this project includes bioretention parameters in
MIKE URBAN. The inﬂuence of the catchment parameters is not analysed. The OAT
sensitivity analysis is used as an initial analysis investigating the inﬂuence of 15 bioreten-
tion parameters. It is a simple analysis providing a clear result easy to interpret. However,
the OAT sensitivity analysis does not analyse how diﬀerent parameters interact and corre-
late. The Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis is therefore performed in order to make a more
comprehensive investigation of the sensitivity of the model. As it is a global analysis,
several parameters are varied simultaneously, testing a range of values for each parameter.
The Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis can reveal interaction and correlation between the
parameters. Only 7 out of the 15 parameters are analysed in the Monte Carlo sensitiv-
ity analysis. A more optimal approach would be to include all the 15 parameters in the
analysis, instead of only investigating the most inﬂuential parameters found in the OAT
sensitivity analysis. However, this required too much computation time, as more model
evaluations would be needed.
61
==PAGE:71==
5
Discussion
In the ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis only area ratio, soil and storage height, ﬁeld
capacity and conductivity of the native soil are analysed. Based on the results area ratio
and conductivity were identiﬁed as the most inﬂuential parameters. The three other pa-
rameters did not appear inﬂuential, neither when looking at the scatter plot nor based on
the SRCs. In the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis, area ratio and conductivity are
kept constant, in order to see if other parameters might be revealed as inﬂuential, when
the most inﬂuential parameters are not included in the analysis. Soil height, ﬁeld capacity
and storage height are once more included in the analysis. Furthermore, surface height
and soil porosity are included. Based on this second analysis both ﬁeld capacity and stor-
age height show to be inﬂuential to some of the outputs in the model. This analysis shows
that inﬂuential parameters might be hidden due to some other very inﬂuential parameters
dominating the sensitivity.
The catchment parameters are not included in the sensitivity analysis. Some of the catch-
ment parameters are however likely to have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the bioretention
performance. Collecting area, impervious fraction, wetting and storage will for example
all aﬀect the amount of water entering the bioretention. Based on a sensitivity analysis
in SWMM, analysing a large urban catchment in California, Barco et al. (2008) found
that land use data generally underestimates the impervious fraction, making it diﬃcult
to estimate the imperviousness of a catchment. Barco et al. (2008) therefore considered
this parameter quite uncertain. In all the simulations carried out in this project, the col-
lecting area for the bioretention only includes the impervious part of the catchment. The
catchments for the case studies are relatively small, where detailed geographic informa-
tion system (GIS) data is available for e.g. buildings and roads. The imperviousness is
therefore not expected to be as uncertain as described by Barco et al. (2008). However,
the sidewalks are deﬁned based on orthophotos and driveways are not taken into account.
The collecting area is considered to be a very uncertain parameter, as it is diﬃcult to
identify the area that will contribute with water to the bioretention. In order to identify
the collecting area a comprehensive analysis of the terrain elevation is necessary. The in-
ﬂuence of the collecting area is not investigated directly in this project. The area ratio is
though analysed, which includes both the collecting area and the area of the bioretention.
So even though the collecting area is not directly analysed, it is included in the area ratio.
When the area ratio is investigated, it is assumed that the performance will be the same
for a small and large scale scenario, as long as the ratio between the bioretention area and
the collecting area is the same.
In both the OAT sensitivity analysis and the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis, ranges
for the parameters are deﬁned. The ranges are based on literature, in order for the ranges
to cover the most realistic values of the parameters. Uncertainties are however connected
to the ranges, which will aﬀect the results of the sensitivity analyses.
5.2.2
Quantitative Estimation of Sensitivity
In the Monte Carlo sensitivity analyses the quantitative inﬂuence of the parameters were
analysed based on the SRCs. This approach assumes that the relation between the input
and output is linear. However, as seen from the values of R2, only a couple of these re-
lations showed linearity. This approach might not be the most optimal approach for the
parameters in MIKE URBAN, since it is not a linear model.
62
==PAGE:72==
5
Discussion
Saltelli et al. (2000) argue that the SRCs often perform poorly for non-linear models.
This corresponds with the low values of R2 found in this project. Saltelli et al. (2000)
furthermore describes several other approaches that can be used to quantify the inﬂuence
of parameter based on results from a Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. SRCs includes
variation of all the input parameters, and do not take the eﬀect of the parameters that are
varied simultaneously into account. Partial correlation coeﬃcients (PCCs) indicate the
correlation between one input parameter and one output, adjusting the result for potential
correlation with other input parameters. If the input parameters are not correlated, SRCs
and PCCs will give the same result (Saltelli et al., 2000). Another approach is to use
rank-transformation, ranking the impact of the parameters based on the raw data from
the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. This approach can be used for non-linear models
(Saltelli et al., 2000). Stepwise regression analysis can also be applied. Using this method,
one regression including several parameters is created. The parameters are added one at a
time starting with the most inﬂuential parameter. Parameters are added to the regression
until the ﬁt does not get any better. This can be evaluated based on the change of R2
after adding each of the parameters. The remaining parameters are excluded from the
regression. The inﬂuence of the parameters can be examined based on the order they are
added, and how they individually changes R2. Saltelli et al. (2000) argues that usually
only few parameters are inﬂuential to the result. Furthermore, the regression might be
unstable if some of the input parameters are correlated, which might lead to incorrect
interpretation of the results. Another method is Sobol’ sensitivity indices, which describe
the inﬂuence based on a ﬁrst order index and a total sensitivity index. This method has
a high computational cost as the model shall be evaluated n · 2p times, where n is the
sample size and p is the number of parameters included in the analysis. An alternative
method can be used, reducing the model evaluations to n · (k + 1). The computational
cost will though still be high (Saltelli et al., 2000).
When quantifying the inﬂuence of the model parameters, no perfect approach exists. The
method for SRCs was chosen in this project as it is an often used method, which is well
described in literature. Locatelli et al. (2015b) uses this approach when analysing the
parameter inﬂuence on inﬁltration trench models and Sin et al. (2011) used the approach
to investigate the uncertainty of a WWTP model. In both studies SRCs were found to
be a useful approach to identify the inﬂuential parameters. In this project the relation
between the input and output can only fulﬁl the requirement of linearity in few cases.
Based on this observation, some of the other methods might have been more appropriate.
The aim of the sensitivity analysis is to identify the most inﬂuential parameters. Even
though it is not possible to compare the inﬂuence directly based on the SRCs, it is still
possible to identify the most inﬂuential parameters based on scatter plots.
5.2.3
Comparing with Other Sensitivity Studies
In the literature only few previous studies analysing the inﬂuence of LID parameters are
found. As part of an analysis of cost-eﬀectiveness, Chui et al. (2016) assessed the sensitiv-
ity of peak runoﬀfrom a bioretention, due to changes in initial saturation, conductivity
of the soil layer and surface height. The analysis was carried out using SWMM. Increas-
ing conductivity and surface height both reduced the peak runoﬀ, where the conductivity
showed to be most inﬂuential. Initial saturation only showed a very small impact. This
justiﬁes the decision of excluding initial saturation from the analysis in this project. Qin
et al. (2013) made an OAT sensitivity analysis in SWMM, testing the inﬂuence of LID
parameters in swales. The surface height was found to be the most inﬂuential parameter
63
==PAGE:73==
5
Discussion
regarding runoﬀreduction. The storage height, the void ratio of the storage layer and
the conductivity of the native soil did not show any inﬂuence on the runoﬀ, as the entire
capacity of the storage layer was never used.
The results from the sensitivity analysis made in this project, show some similarities
with the previous studies described above. Chui et al. (2016) identiﬁed the conductivity
of the native soil as the most inﬂuential parameter. This corresponds to the ﬁndings in
this project. However, both Chui et al. (2016) and Qin et al. (2013) found that the surface
height is inﬂuential. In this project the surface height only shows very little inﬂuence
in the second Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis. For the comparison of the results, it is
important to note that Qin et al. (2013) did not analyse bioretentions, but swales.
Several previous studies have analysed the inﬂuence of catchment parameters.
Barco
et al. (2008) used SWMM to analyse a large urban catchment in California and found
that imperviousness and depression storage of the impervious surfaces were the most in-
ﬂuential parameters.
The roughness of the surface showed the lowest inﬂuence.
This
trend is supported by the study of Zaghloul (1983), who also analysed the inﬂuence of the
catchment parameters in SWMM. Zaghloul (1983) identiﬁed the imperviousness as the
most inﬂuential parameter, where a linear increase of both peak ﬂow and runoﬀvolume
was observed. Besides the imperviousness, Zaghloul (1983) found that the width of the
catchment is inﬂuential, followed by inﬁltration rate, depression storage, surface roughness
and ﬁnally the slope of the catchment. Krebs et al. (2014) also analysed the inﬂuence of
catchment parameters in SWMM and found that imperviousness, depression storage and
roughness was the most inﬂuential parameters. Inﬁltration parameters were also found
to have a signiﬁcant impact on the model output. Li et al. (2014) investigated the sensi-
tivity of peak discharge, total runoﬀvolume and time of peak in SWMM. 12 parameters,
mainly catchment parameters, were included in the analysis. The catchment area, width
and slope, roughness of both impervious and pervious surfaces and the minimum Horton
inﬁltration were identiﬁed as the most inﬂuential parameters. However, the order of inﬂu-
ence depended on the evaluated output. Rosa et al. (2015) performed an OAT sensitivity
analysis in SWMM, investigating the sensitivity of runoﬀvolume and peak ﬂow. The
conductivity of the native soil was identiﬁed as the most inﬂuential parameter. However,
the impervious fraction was not included in the analysis, as the fraction was measured for
the catchment.
Based on the literature just presented, the impervious fraction and depression storage
seem to be the most inﬂuential catchment parameters.
Furthermore, catchment area,
conductivity of native soil, roughness, width and slope also appear to be inﬂuential. As
these parameters inﬂuence the output of hydrological models, they will have an impact
on the inﬂow to the bioretention, which aﬀects the performance of the bioretention. The
sensitivity analysis carried out in this project only takes bioretention parameters into ac-
count. However, when analysing the bioretention performance in a hydrological model it
is important to keep in mind that the parameters of the catchment also will aﬀect the LID
performance. The result of the sensitivity analysis performed in this project shall there-
fore be used in combination with other studies investigating the inﬂuence of catchment
parameters.
64
==PAGE:74==
5
Discussion
5.3
Simple LID Tools
5.3.1
Updating the WSUD-Potential Tool
The WSUD-potential tool is updated by implementing regressions ﬁtted to 1 000 MIKE
URBAN results. The results are based on simulations where all the parameters are ﬁxed,
except area ratio and conductivity of the native soil.
The regressions are made using
polynomial regressions, where evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency can be esti-
mated for diﬀerent area ratios and conductivities. The regressions can thereby not describe
the eﬀect of varying other input parameters.
In the regression analysis it is not taken into account that evaporation and inﬁltration
cannot be higher than 100%. Furthermore, no regressions for the runoﬀare made. In the
new WSUD-potential tool the runoﬀis deﬁned as the excess water that neither evaporates
nor inﬁltrates. This is though a simpliﬁcation, as water might still be stored in the LID or
in the depressions in the catchment when the simulation ends. However, this is considered
an insigniﬁcant amount compared to the entire water balance.
Many of the processes are described with high degree polynomial regressions, as lower
degrees did not ﬁt the MIKE URBAN results adequately. High degree polynomial regres-
sions can though overﬁt the results, implying that lower degrees can be adequate for the
regression. A high degree polynomial regression can thereby result in unnecessary complex
regressions (Montgomery, 2013). When the regressions are identiﬁed for inﬁltration and
overﬂow frequency, even the highest degree of polynomial available in the cftool cannot
describe the processes suﬃciently. Therefore two regressions, one for low and one for high
conductivity ranges, are used to describe each of these processes. As seen in Section 4.5.2,
the use of two regressions introduces a sudden change, when moving from one regression
to the other. This is caused by the displacement of the two regressions at the conductivity
where they meet. However, the processes are generally described much better with two
regressions compared to using only one regression.
In the Monte Carlo analysis MIKE URBAN is evaluated for area ratios ranging from 1% -
30% and conductivities ranging from 8 · 10−10 - 4 · 10−5 m/s. As several of the regressions
cannot adequately describe the results for the lowest conductivities, the WSUD-potential
tool is only considered applicable for conductivities ranging from 5 · 10−9 - 4 · 10−5 m/s.
This range is considered adequate for use in Copenhagen, where the conductivity ranges
from 1·10−9 - 1·10−5 m/s (Bockhorn et al., 2013). The WSUD-potential tool can be used
for the entire range of area ratio (1% - 30%). MIKE URBAN results are thereby avail-
able in the entire range of both area ratio and conductivity in which the WSUD-potential
tool can be used. No extrapolation has been used for any of the regressions, as extrap-
olation is highly uncertain, especially for polynomial regressions (Johnson et al., 2011).
The boundaries deﬁned for the WSUD-potential tool shall therefore be respected, as the
regressions are not analysed outside of these boundaries. The regressions are considered
to ﬁt the MIKE URBAN results relatively well, as R2 generally are high and RMSE are
low. Furthermore, the residuals are considered acceptable. It is though possible that other
regressions that can describe the processes better exist.
It is expected that the regressions will reﬂect the results in MIKE URBAN, as they are
initially ﬁtted to these results. This implies that limitations, assumptions and potential
errors in MIKE URBAN also will be reﬂected in the regressions and thereby passed on
to the WSUD-potential tool. The result of the new WSUD-potential tool does however
65
==PAGE:75==
5
Discussion
look much more realistic compared to the results of the old WSUD-potential tool, as the
processes in the new WSUD-potential tool change smoothly, whereas signiﬁcant jumps are
observed with the old WSUD-potential tool.
5.3.2
Other Tools for Assessing LID Performance
In order to estimate the performance of LIDs, it is important to consider site speciﬁc con-
ditions, such as land use, topography and climate conditions. Detailed site speciﬁc data
is rarely available in the early stage of urban planning, making it diﬃcult to use advanced
modelling tools. A simple model with few input parameters will therefore be of big use.
Similar tools as the WSUD-potential tool have been developed globally.
Lerer et al.
(2015a) found that many diﬀerent aspects are of focus in the diﬀerent tools, including
economic aspects, water quality and placement of LIDs. Furthermore, many of the tools
are adjusted to local settings, making them less applicable for other locations (Lerer et al.,
2015a). Moore et al. (2012) developed a GIS based tool where the placement of LIDs and
the feasibility of diﬀerent designs were investigated. Another example is the tool SUSTAIN
(System for Urban Stormwater Treatment and Analysis INtegration), which combines de-
cision support making, optimal location, water quality and cost eﬀectiveness. SUSTAIN
uses SWMM to estimate the hydrological performance of LIDs (Lerer et al., 2015a). In
Denmark, SVK (2015) has developed a spreadsheet for dimensioning the following four
types of LIDs: Soakaways, rain garden, swale and permeable pavement. The dimensioning
is based on a user speciﬁed return period for overﬂows, where the calculations are based
on IDF curves. Regional climate variation in Denmark is taken into account, as the pre-
cipitation is speciﬁed for about 100 diﬀerent municipalities. The tool by SVK (2015) does
not take the water balance into account.
WABILA (Short for Wasserbilanz; German for water balance) is a simple tool used for
estimating the hydrological eﬀect of LIDs (Henrichs et al., 2016). The model compares
diﬀerent catchments with and without LIDs taking local conditions, such as soil, mean
annual rainfall and annual evaporation, into account. Interaction between the impervious
and pervious part of the catchment is neglected and all simulations are based on a 100%
impervious area. WABILA uses regressions, which are based on simulations in SWMM
(Henrichs et al., 2016). In order to make the regressions, the most inﬂuential parameters
were identiﬁed and a range was assigned to these parameters. Monte Carlo analyses with
1 000 parameter combinations were conducted, for 40 diﬀerent climate stations. This im-
plies that the SWMM model was evaluated 40 000 times. Based on this the partitioning
factors for evaporation, inﬁltration and surface runoﬀwere made with multiple non-linear
regression.
The idea and method for developing WABILA is very similar to the new WSUD-potential
tool. Monte Carlo analyses are carried out when developing both tools and they are both
based on regressions taking the most inﬂuential parameters into account. Only impervious
areas are considered, and water balances can be estimated requiring only few input param-
eters. It would have been very interesting to compare the tools in more detail. WABILA
was though not available for use in this project.
66
==PAGE:76==
5
Discussion
5.3.3
Limitations and Improvements for the WSUD-Potential
Tool
The WSUD-potential tool has several limitations. The regressions are based on a 100%
impervious catchment and interaction with the pervious part is not included. This leads
to an underestimation of the runoﬀ, as runoﬀwill be generated on pervious areas under
certain conditions. The water balance is only calculated for the collecting area of the
bioretention. This shall be taken into account when results are interpreted and diﬀerent
scenarios are compared. For the homeowner’s associations analysed in this project, assess-
ments are performed when including and excluding runoﬀgenerated at roofs. When the
roofs are not included in the collecting area of the bioretention, this runoﬀis not visible in
the water balance. The diﬀerent scenarios are therefore not directly comparable, as they
reﬂect the impact on diﬀerent areas. It can though be argued that the boundaries of the
catchment have to be deﬁned in some way.
Three diﬀerent bioretention designs, Bioretention A, B and C, can be analysed in the new
WSUD-potential tool. Conductivity and area ratio are the only independent variables in
the WSUD-potential tool. As all the other parameters are kept constant, the predeﬁned
bioretention designs cannot be changed. The WSUD-potential tool does though take the
most inﬂuential parameters into account. As the tool is only intended for rough estima-
tions, it is considered acceptable that the exact dimensions cannot be speciﬁed.
The new WSUD-potential tool can be further improved in diﬀerent ways.
In the old
WSUD-potential tool, the hydrological performance of permeable surfaces, detention units
and two bioretention designs can be estimated. In the new WSUD-potential tool, only
three diﬀerent bioretention designs are included. More types of LIDs can be included in
the new WSUD-potential tool. This can be done, in the same way as for the three biore-
tention designs in this project. If other types of LIDs shall be implemented, new sensitivity
analyses need to be carried out, in order to identify the most inﬂuential parameters for
these structures. Based on a Monte Carlo analysis, in e.g. MIKE URBAN or SWMM,
the regressions can be created with the most inﬂuential parameters. Other LID types can
then be implemented in the tool by adding new sheets in the spreadsheet.
In the old WSUD-potential tool it is possible to assess the impact of implementing mul-
tiple LID types simultaneously. This is done based on several assumptions regarding how
the use of diﬀerent types of LIDs inﬂuences the overall hydrological performance. This is
not included in the new WSUD-potential tool. Climate variation, included in WABILA,
could also be taken into account in the WSUD-potential tool. This will though require
several model evaluations using data for diﬀerent climates.
Additional improvements for the WSUD-potential tool are already being planned, in-
cluding an economical assessment of the diﬀerent designs and an uncertainty analysis.
The aim of the uncertainty analysis is to identify and compare the uncertainty caused by
the simplicity of the model and the uncertainty related to unknown parameters (Lerer
et al., 2015b).
5.4
Assessment of Model Performance
Calibration and validation are usually used to enhance and evaluate the model perfor-
mance.
Several studies assess the importance of calibration and validation of models.
67
==PAGE:77==
5
Discussion
McCutcheon and Wride (2013) made a ﬁeld study where rain gardens were implemented
in diﬀerent soil types in Wisconsin, US. Field measurements were compared with results
from SWMM. Both ﬁeld data and simulations showed that for clay and sandy soils (un-
known conductivity) approximately 10% and 15% evaporated respectively. The remaining
water inﬁltrated. These results are in accordance with the results found in this project.
According to McCutcheon and Wride (2013) it is recommended that a model always shall
be calibrated and validated based on ﬁeld data. Furthermore, the study emphasises the
importance of using long rain series when modelling green infrastructures. This corre-
sponds with the ﬁndings presented in Section 4.1.1, showing that some of the processes
occurring in the bioretention happen very slowly. Long rain series are therefore necessary
to evaluate the performance of LIDs.
Rosa et al. (2015) compared predicted runoﬀfrom SWMM and observed measurements
for a catchment with and without inﬁltration units.
Initially the input parameters in
SWMM were based on literature and model defaults. This showed poor agreement be-
tween the predicted and observed measurements for the runoﬀ. The results from SWMM
were about 80% and 20% lower for the runoﬀthan the observed values for the catchment
with and without LIDs respectively. Using the observed data, the model was calibrated,
and a much better ﬁt was found. This shows the importance of calibrating a model with
measurements. Site speciﬁc information also has a big impact on the LID performance.
Calibration of a model using measurements from a diﬀerent region shall therefore be made
with care, since the area might have a diﬀerent climate, terrain, conductivity, etc. (Mc-
Cutcheon and Wride, 2013).
The studies described in this section emphasise the importance of model calibration and
validation. In this project the models are used to design and predict the performance
of bioretentions, which are not yet implemented. It is therefore not possible to calibrate
and validate the models as no measurements are available. Furthermore, little ﬁeld mea-
surements are available for bioretentions in the literature. However, it can be analysed
whether the results obtained in the programs are as expected, and if they are realistic.
In order to evaluate the performance of bioretentions in MIKE URBAN, MIKE URBAN
is initially compared with SWMM. This is done as LID controls have been available in
SWMM since 2009, and they are therefore considered thoroughly tested. Similar results
are obtained in SWMM and MIKE URBAN when analysing a bioretention. However,
SWMM is a diﬀerent model with inherent uncertainties like MIKE URBAN. In addition
to that, the LID controls implemented in MIKE URBAN are highly inspired by the LID
controls implemented in SWMM (DHI, 2016b). As the calculation approach in the two
programs looks very similar, it is expected that they provide similar results, as they con-
tain the same calculations and assumptions.
In this project, the results from the new WSUD-potential tool are compared with MIKE
URBAN results. The comparison is used to verify that the new WSUD-potential tool
gives similar results as MIKE URBAN, which is expected due to the regressions based on
MIKE URBAN results. If the new WSUD-potential tool is used for a speciﬁc area, it is
though important to keep in mind that it is not calibrated and validated. It would be
preferable to calibrate and validate the model for a Danish residential area.
Even though none of the models can be calibrated or validated, they all give reason-
able and realistic results, and the models act as expected. They are therefore considered
68
==PAGE:78==
5
Discussion
useful for assessing the LID performance. MIKE URBAN is a heavy and comprehensive
program requiring both an expensive license and modelling skills. As the new WSUD-
potential tool gives similar results as MIKE URBAN, it is considered suﬃcient to roughly
assess the performance of bioretentions. A rough assessment might be adequate in the
initial phase of LID projects, when many stakeholders are involved. The use of the new
WSUD-potential tool, instead of MIKE URBAN, will ease the analysis, as only few in-
put parameters are necessary. Furthermore, the WSUD-potential tool is developed in a
spreadsheet, which is commonly available. The new WSUD-potential tool is thereby more
user friendly and can easily be used by people without speciﬁc modelling knowledge. The
user shall though be aware of the assumptions and uncertainties included in the tool.
As described in Section 2.2.3, Walker et al. (2003) consider three dimensions of uncer-
tainty: Location, level and nature. In the models used in this project, uncertainties can
be located in e.g. the conceptual model, if the system and processes of the catchment
and bioretention are constructed with too many simpliﬁcations or understood incorrectly.
It can also be located in the calculations as bugs, typing errors or uncertain regressions.
The input parameters are also very uncertain, as they are based on literature data alone.
Catchment parameters can vary signiﬁcantly and are site speciﬁc. The dimensions of the
bioretention might also vary, which cannot be changed in the new WSUD-potential tool.
The level of the uncertainty is unknown and can vary greatly. However, the models of
SWMM and MIKE URBAN are based on several accepted and tested models and pro-
cesses. As the results seem relatively realistic, the uncertainties are not considered to
be extreme. For the nature of the uncertainty Walker et al. (2003) distinguish between
epistemic and variability uncertainty. Both types of uncertainties exist in the models in
this project. More knowledge about e.g. the input parameters and processes occurring in
the system will reduce the epistemic uncertainties. Variability uncertainties are connected
to the simulation of e.g. evaporation and rain. These are natural processes with inherent
randomness.
During this project the sensitivity is investigated, however no other uncertainty analysis
is performed. In order to understand the uncertainties and to use the models successfully,
it is relevant to make a thorough uncertainty analysis, where location, level and nature of
the uncertainties are identiﬁed systematically.
5.5
Performance of Low Impact Development
Several types and designs of LIDs exist, which can be used for diﬀerent purposes. If the
LID enhances inﬁltration or discharge runoﬀinto recipients, it is necessary to be aware
of the restrictions deﬁned to mitigate contamination of water bodies. The restrictions are
described in Section 2.1.3. Due to sedimentation, adsorption, ﬁltration, biological degra-
dation, oxidation and plant uptake, bioretentions containing engineered soil can reduce the
amount of suspended solids, polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon (PAH), heavy metals and
biological and chemical oxygen demand (BOD/COD) (Københavns Kommune, 2016b).
Ermilio (2005) measured the treatment in inﬁltration units and found that suspended
solids were reduced by more than 90%. Dissolved solids, nitrogen and nitrate were all
reduced with about 40%. The reduction of the heavy metals copper, lead, chromium and
zinc were found to range from approximately 15% - 60%, where zinc showed the lowest
reduction. Jia et al. (2015) found that bioretentions reduce zinc by 100%. Furthermore,
nitrogen was reduced by about 40%, COD by 18% and copper by 70%.
69
==PAGE:79==
5
Discussion
If treatment is necessary, Bioretention A is not suﬃcient, as it does not include a layer of
engineered soil. In Bioretention A contaminated water can therefore inﬁltrate and contam-
inate the groundwater. Bioretention B and C on the other hand both include engineered
soil, treating the water while it inﬁltrates. The risk of contaminating the groundwater is
thereby reduced if Bioretention B or C is applied. Furthermore, if a drain is included in
the design, contamination of the recipient is reduced. It can be argued that the restric-
tion regarding inﬁltration is too strict, as the groundwater in Copenhagen is not used for
anything and is already contaminated (Københavns Kommune, 2016b).
As expected the performance of the bioretention designs, investigated in this project,
increases as the complexity of the design increases. The cost does though also increase for
a more complex design. However, the economic expenses are not analysed in this project,
and the cost-eﬀectiveness can therefore not be evaluated based on the results obtained.
Joksimovic and Alam (2014) analysed the cost eﬀectiveness of diﬀerent LIDs and several
combinations of these. The cost eﬀectiveness were evaluated based on the cost per m3
reduced runoﬀ. Rain barrels, vegetated swales and inﬁltration trenches were found to
be the most cost eﬀective solutions, with nearly half the cost of bioretentions. Perme-
able pavement and green roofs showed to be about ﬁve and nine times more expensive
than bioretentions respectively. All the combinations of diﬀerent types of LIDs showed
a higher cost than the cost of bioretentions. The high cost of permeable pavement, sup-
ports HOFOR’s decision of not co-ﬁnancing permeable pavement, as described in Section
4.6.5. Permeable pavement is though useful if the surface shall be used for parking. Guan
et al. (2015) also investigated the eﬀects of various combinations of diﬀerent LID types,
including rain barrels, green roof, permeable pavement and storage units. The eﬀect of
LID combinations showed to be bigger than applying LIDs separately.
Zhou et al. (2012) assessed the ﬂood risk and climate adaptation strategies in a Dan-
ish catchment north of Odense. In order to manage extreme rain events, increasing the
capacity of the drainage system was found to be more cost eﬀective compared to enhanced
inﬁltration. LIDs do though add recreational value to an area, which was not taken into
account in the study by Zhou et al. (2012).
There is no deﬁned service level for LIDs, and diﬀerent opinions regarding the accept-
able return period of overﬂows exist. As LIDs only manage runoﬀ, it can be argued that
overﬂows shall be accepted once every ﬁfth year, as this corresponds to the service level
for separate systems (Københavns Kommune, 2008). According to Sørup et al. (2016)
SCMs designed with a return period of ﬁve years, will manage approximately 95% of the
annual precipitation. If a structure is designed for the day-to-day domain, with a return
period of 0.2 years, about 75% of the annual precipitation is managed (Sørup et al., 2016).
In the case studies analysed in this project, the performance of the bioretentions depend
greatly on the conductivity of the native soil and the area ratio. In some cases it might
not be possible to reach an overﬂow return period of ﬁve years. In such cases, lower return
periods can be accepted, as LIDs are still considered beneﬁcial for residential areas. LIDs
enhance the natural water balance, reduce CSOs and the amount of water treated at the
WWTP and increase the biodiversity (Guan et al., 2015; Zhou et al., 2012; Moore and
Hunt, 2012; Lerer et al., 2015b).
Based on the advantages of LIDs, and the ﬁndings by Zhou et al. (2012), a strategy
combining traditional solutions and LIDs seems beneﬁcial. This is supported by Fratini
et al. (2012) who argue that all three domains of the 3PA shall be addressed when plan-
70
==PAGE:80==
5
Discussion
ning SCMs. Comprehensive SCMs are planned to be implemented in Copenhagen. These
include surface and subsurface solutions, which is in line with the suggestions by Fratini
et al. (2012).
Despite the great interest of LIDs and the comprehensive plans in Copenhagen, there
is currently only little knowledge about the performance of LIDs.
It is therefore very
relevant to measure the performance of constructed LIDs, in order to improve the under-
standing of the technology. Improved understanding can be used to optimize both the
models used for planning, and the designs of the LIDs.
71
==PAGE:81==
6
Conclusion
6
Conclusion
In this project the hydrological impact of bioretention units, using three diﬀerent modelling
tools, was assessed. When simulating a 100% impervious catchment including a bioreten-
tion unit with parameters obtained through literature, similar results were achieved in
SWMM and MIKE URBAN. The two programs did however apply evaporation time se-
ries diﬀerently, resulting in lower evaporation in the annual water balance for SWMM.
A Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis on 15 bioretention unit parameters in MIKE UR-
BAN showed that area ratio and conductivity of the native soil are the most inﬂuential
parameters. Area ratio was deﬁned as the ratio between the area of the LID and the area
of the catchment. When area ratio and conductivity were kept constant two other param-
eters showed more inﬂuence on the output. It was possible to make regressions describing
evaporation, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequently for three diﬀerent bioretention designs.
The regressions were based on MIKE URBAN results, varying the two most inﬂuential pa-
rameters. The regressions were considered to ﬁt the results in MIKE URBAN adequately
as the adjusted coeﬃcient of determination (R2) was above 0.96 and root mean square
error (RMSE) was below 5.4. The regressions can be used with an area ratio ranging from
1% - 30% and a conductivity ranging from 5 · 10−9 - 4 · 10−5 m/s. For both inﬁltration
and overﬂow frequency two regressions were made for high and low conductivity in order
to describe the processes suﬃciently. The WSUD-potential tool was updated by replacing
the lookup tables with the regressions, resulting in a more detailed estimation of evapo-
ration, inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency for three bioretention designs.
Using two homeowner’s associations in Copenhagen as case studies, it was possible to ob-
tain reasonable and similar results in both MIKE URBAN and the new WSUD-potential
tool. For a 100% impervious catchment without bioretentions 17% of the precipitation
evaporated and the rest became runoﬀ. Implementation of bioretentions changed the wa-
ter balance. For a simple bioretention design with a conductivity of 2 · 10−7 m/s, the
water balance showed how approximately 20% evaporated, 60% inﬁltrated and 20% be-
came runoﬀ. Diﬀerent scenarios were analysed resulting in diﬀerent water balances. For
both case studies it was recommended that runoﬀfrom all impervious areas were managed
by LIDs, with an overﬂow return period of 5 years. In order to achieve this, runoﬀfrom
roofs shall be managed with bioretention units in the individual gardens. Furthermore,
conductivity tests shall be carried out, as the conductivity has a high inﬂuence on the
bioretention performance.
None of the models used in this project are calibrated or validated, as they are used
for designing and estimating the eﬀect of constructions not yet implemented. It is there-
fore important to be aware of the uncertainties inherent in the models. The output of
the models depends on site speciﬁc conditions, which shall be taken into account when
using the models. Both SWMM and MIKE URBAN require technical knowledge within
the ﬁeld and many input parameters need to be speciﬁed. The WSUD-potential tool is
more user friendly, as few input parameters are needed and it can be operated in a simple
spreadsheet. The WSUD-potential tool can advantageously be used in the initial phase of
urban planning, where many stakeholders are involved and where rough estimations are
adequate.

A
Matlab Scripts
A
Matlab Scripts
In this appendix the Matlab scripts used in this project are presented. All Matlab scripts
are developed during this project.
A.1
Calculating Overﬂow Frequency
The overﬂow frequency for a simulation in MIKE URBAN and SWMM is calculated us-
ing the Matlab script presented in this appendix. In both MIKE URBAN and SWMM,
surface ﬂow from the bioretention is reported for every time step. In MIKE URBAN this
is reported in the .dfs0-ﬁle showing results for the LID. In SWMM the surface ﬂow is
reported in a .txt-ﬁle.
Several steps are included in the Matlab script:
 Deﬁne simulation time.
 The .dfs0-ﬁle is opened and data for surface ﬂow is loaded. This part of the script is
taken from the DHI Matlab Toolbox (DHI, 2014) and is used if the results are based
on a MIKE URBAN simulation.
 The .txt-ﬁle is opened and data for surface ﬂow is loaded. This part is used if the
results are based on a SWMM simulation.
 Data for surface ﬂow is plotted.
 The total number of overﬂows are counted. Overﬂow is considered a new event if no
overﬂow has occurred the previous hour. Overﬂow frequency is calculated based on
the total number of overﬂows and the simulation time.
82
==PAGE:92==
Matlab Script for Calculating Overflow Frequency 
 
%% Define simulation time 
Simulation_time = 10;                         
% Simulation time [years] 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Import .dfs0-file (MIKE URBAN result file) 
    disp 'Importing data...' 
    NET.addAssembly('DHI.Generic.MikeZero.DFS'); 
    import DHI.Generic.MikeZero.DFS.*; 
    import DHI.Generic.MikeZero.DFS.dfs0.*; 
    infile = 'Result_file.dfs0';               % Change to correct file 
    if (~exist(infile,'file')) 
        [filename,filepath] = uigetfile('*.dfs0','Select the .dfs0'); 
        infile = [filepath,filename]; 
    end 
    dfs0File = DfsFileFactory.DfsGenericOpen(infile); 
    dd = double(Dfs0Util.ReadDfs0DataDouble(dfs0File)); 
    t = dd(:,1);                                % Time 
    SurfaceFlow = dd(1:end,6);                 % Column with overflow data for the LID 
    dfs0File.Close(); 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% Import .txt-file (SWMM result file) 
    disp 'Importing data...' 
    FID=fopen('Result_file.txt');              % Change to correct file 
    datacell = textscan(FID, '%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f', 'HeaderLines', 9, 'CollectOutput', 1);   
% Import without headings 
    fclose(FID); 
    Total_file = datacell{1}; 
    SurfaceFlow=Total_file(:,7);       
% Column with overflow data for the LID 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Plot overflows 
    disp 'Plotting data...' 
    plot(SurfaceFlow); 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Calculate number of overflows 
% An overflow is defined as a new overflow event if there has been no overflows the previous 
hour. 
    disp 'Calculating number of overflows...' 
     
    % Prepare overflow data: 
    Steps_per_hour = 60;                                    % Define the number of values per  
hour 
    Start_data = Steps_per_hour + 1; 
    Initial_dry = flipud(rot90(zeros(1,Steps_per_hour)));   % Makes array with 60 minutes  
without overflow (value = 0) 
    Overflow_data=[Initial_dry;SurfaceFlow];                % Ensures 60 minutes without  
overflow in the beginning of the 
timeseries 
    % Calculate number of overflows: 
    Number_of_Overflows = 0; 
  
    for Number_of_values = Start_data:length(Overflow_data); % Checks only imported data, not  
the initial zeros added to the 
array 
        Value = Overflow_data(Number_of_values); 
==PAGE:93==
        if Value > 0 
            for i = 1:Steps_per_hour; 
 
% Checks the previous 60 steps 
                Previous_value(i) = Overflow_data(Number_of_values-i); % Creates "Dry" array  
for previous 60 
steps 
                Dry = sum(Previous_value);                             % Sum the value in the  
"Dry" array 
            end 
            if Dry == 0; 
                Number_of_Overflows=Number_of_Overflows+1; 
% An overflow event is added to  
the number of overflows 
            end 
        end 
    end 
 
    % Calculate overflow frequency: 
    Overflow_freq = Number_of_Overflows/Simulation_time 
% Overflow frequency [1/y] 
==PAGE:94==
A
Matlab Scripts
A.2
Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis
The Matlab script used to perform the Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis is shown on the
following pages. The Matlab script is developed speciﬁcally for this project, with support
from Numerical Engine Software Developer at DHI Eduardo Javier Mu˜noz Queupumil and
Postdoc at DTU Luca Locatelli and with inspiration from Lindgreen and Dreier (2014),
who performed a Monte Carlo analysis for parameters in an urban drainage systems in
the MOUSE engine.
The Matlab script is divided into ﬁve main parts:
 Deﬁne .m1dx-ﬁle, parameters, ranges and number of simulations.
 Generate the parameter sets based on LHS and plot the input distribution. The
part of the script performing LHS is based on a script provided by Postdoc at DTU
Luca Locatelli.
 Run MIKE 1D (Read and change .m1dx-ﬁle, run MIKE 1D, read and analyse re-
sults).
 Plot results.
 Calculate standardized regression coeﬃcients (SRCs).
85
==PAGE:95==
Matlab Script for Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis 
 
%% Define parameters: 
    disp 'Defining parameters...' 
    tic                                                % Simulation time to define parameters 
     
    % Parameters: 
    Base_file = 'Kloevermarken_year2000-2009.m1dx';    % m1dx-file with base settings 
    New_file = 'Kloevermarken_year2000-2009_new.m1dx'; % m1dx-file used in simulation 
    Simulation_time = 10;                              % Simulation time [years] 
    
    Par_AreaLID = 'A, LID [m^2]';                      % Uniform distribution 
    Par_SurfaceHeight = 'h, surface [m]';              % Uniform distribution 
    Par_SoilHeight = 'h, soil [m]';                    % Uniform distribution 
    Par_SoilPorosity = '\phi, soil [-]';               % Uniform distribution 
    Par_SoilFC = 'FC, soil [-]';                       % Uniform distribution 
    Par_StorHeight = 'h, storage [m]';                 % Uniform distribution 
    Par_NativeK = 'K [m/s]';                           % Log distribution 
    Parameters = {Par_AreaLID; Par_SoilHeight; Par_SoilFC; Par_StorHeight; Par_NativeK}; 
     
    p = 5;                                             % Number of parameters 
    lb = [10 0.25 0.08 0.2];           % Lower bound for parameters with uniform distribution 
    ub = [300 0.75 0.3 1.5];           % Upper bound for parameters with uniform distribution 
    n = 500;                                           % Number of simulations 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% LHS matrix: 
    % Uniform distribution: 
    xn_uni = roundn(lhsdesign(n,p-1),-2); 
    x_uni = bsxfun(@plus,lb,bsxfun(@times,xn_uni,(ub-lb))); 
     
    % Log distribution for conductivity of native soil: 
    NativeK_mean = 1.94e-7;                             % m/s 
    Ksigma = 5.3e-7;                                    % m/s 
    v = Ksigma^2; 
    mu = log((NativeK_mean^2)/sqrt(v+NativeK_mean^2)); 
    sigma = sqrt(log(v/(NativeK_mean^2)+1)); 
    K = exp(lhsnorm(mu,sigma^2,n)); 
  
    % Monte Carlo matrix: 
    x = [x_uni K]; 
    fid = fopen('Results\Parameter_values.txt','w'); 
    save Results\Parameter_values.txt x –ascii; fclose(fid); % Save LHS matrix in .txt file 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% Plot input distribution: 
    figure 
    [h,ax,bax,P] = plotmatrix(x); 
    set(ax,'FontSize',8); 
    for i=1:p 
        ylabel(ax(i,1),Parameters(i)); xlabel(ax(5,i),Parameters(i)); 
    end 
    print('Results\Input_distribution','-dpng')        % Save plot 
    toc                                                % Simulation time to define parameters 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% Run MIKE 1D: 
    disp 'Running MIKE 1D...' 
    tic                                                % Simulation time to run MIKE 1D 
for i=1:n 
    i 
==PAGE:96==
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
 
%% Read m1dx-file 
    path_to_file = 'F:\Data\s103346_s103355\Monte_Carlo\'; 
    Base_settings = fullfile(path_to_file,Base_file);  % Define m1dx-file with base settings 
    New_settings = fullfile(path_to_file,New_file);    % Define m1dx-file with new settings 
    copyfile(Base_settings,New_settings); 
% Reset New_settings to Base_settings 
    m1dx_file = fileread(New_settings);                % m1dx-file run in MIKE 1D 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% New Parameters (Define where in the m1dx-file to change the parameters) 
    AreaLID = '<Area>100</Area>'; 
 
Column_AreaLID = x(i,1); 
 
AreaLID_new = ['<Area>' mat2str(Column_AreaLID) '</Area>']; 
 
    %SurfaceHeight = '<StorageHeight>0.2</StorageHeight>'; 
 
%Column_SurfaceHeight = x(i,1); 
 
%SurfaceHeight_new = ['<StorageHeight>' mat2str(Column_SurfaceHeight) </StorageHeight>']; 
 
    SoilHeight = '<Thickness>0.5</Thickness>'; 
 
Column_SoilHeight = x(i,2); 
 
SoilHeight_new = ['<Thickness>' mat2str(Column_SoilHeight) '</Thickness>']; 
 
    %SoilPorosity = '<Porosity>0.44</Porosity>'; 
 
%Column_SoilPorosity = x(i,3); 
 
%SoilPorosity_new = ['<Porosity>' mat2str(Column_SoilPorosity) '</Porosity>']; 
 
    SoilFC = '<FieldCapacity>0.11</FieldCapacity>'; 
 
Column_SoilFC = x(i,3); 
 
SoilFC_new = ['<FieldCapacity>' mat2str(Column_SoilFC) '</FieldCapacity>']; 
 
    StorHeight = '<Height>1</Height>'; 
 
Column_StorHeight = x(i,4); 
 
StorHeight_new = ['<Height>' mat2str(Column_StorHeight) '</Height>']; 
 
    NativeK = '<Conductivity>2.00000000016E-07</Conductivity>'; 
 
Column_NativeK = x(i,5); 
 
NativeK_new = ['<Conductivity>' mat2str(Column_NativeK) '</Conductivity>']; 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
        
%% Modify m1dx-file (Replace old parameter values with new): 
m1dx_file_new = regexprep(m1dx_file,{AreaLID, SoilHeight, SoilFC, StorHeight, NativeK}, 
{AreaLID_new, SoilHeight_new, SoilFC_new, StorHeight_new, NativeK_new}); 
    fid = fopen(New_settings,'w+'); fprintf(fid,'%s',m1dx_file_new); fclose(fid); 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% Run MIKE 1D

%% Import result from dfs0-file 
    NET.addAssembly('DHI.Generic.MikeZero.DFS'); 
    import DHI.Generic.MikeZero.DFS.*; 
    import DHI.Generic.MikeZero.DFS.dfs0.*; 
    infile = 'LID_Results.dfs0'; 
    if (~exist(infile,'file')) 
        [filename,filepath] = uigetfile('*.dfs0','Select the .dfs0'); 
        infile = [filepath,filename]; 
    end 
    dfs0File = DfsFileFactory.DfsGenericOpen(infile); 
    dd = double(Dfs0Util.ReadDfs0DataDouble(dfs0File)); 
 
 
==PAGE:97==
 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% Calculate overflow frequency 
    % Overflows count as a new event if there has been no overflows the previous hour. 
    t = dd(:,1);                                       % Time 
    SurfaceFlow = dd(1:end,6);                         % Overflow from the LID 
    Steps_per_hour = 60;                               % Define the number of values per hour 
     
    % Calculate number of overflows: 
    Start_data = Steps_per_hour + 1; 
    Initial_dry = flipud(rot90(zeros(1,Steps_per_hour))); % Makes array with 60 minutes 
without overflow (value = 0) 
    Overflow_data=[Initial_dry;SurfaceFlow];    % Ensures 60 minutes without overflow in the 
  beginning of the timeseries 
    Number_of_Overflows = 0; 
    for Number_of_values = Start_data:length(Overflow_data); % Checks only imported data, not 
   the initial zeros added to 
   the array 
        Value = Overflow_data(Number_of_values); 
        if Value > 0 
            for q = 1:Steps_per_hour;                        % Checks the previous 60 steps 
                Previous_value(q) = Overflow_data(Number_of_values-q); 
% Creates "Dry" array for previous 60 steps 
                Dry = sum(Previous_value);       % Sum the value in the "Dry" array 
            end 
            if Dry == 0; 
                Number_of_Overflows = Number_of_Overflows+1;                         
% An overflow event is added to the number of overflows 
            end 
        end 
    end 
     
    % Calculate overflow frequency: 
    Overflow_freq(i,:) = Number_of_Overflows/Simulation_time;  
% Overflow frequency [y^-1] 
    fid = fopen('Results\Overflows_frequency.txt','w'); save Results\Overflows_frequency.txt 
 
Overflow_freq -ascii; fclose(fid);     
% Save result in .txt file 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% Read data 
    % Read and sum water balance data: 
    Sum_ET_m3(i,:) = (sum(dd(1:end,4)/60))/1000.*x(i,1);          % LID Evaporation (m3) 
    fid = fopen('Results\Sum_ET_m3.txt','w'); 
 
save Results\Sum_ET_m3.txt Sum_ET_m3 -ascii; fclose(fid);     % Save result in .txt file 
     
 
Sum_Infiltration_m3(i,:) = (sum(dd(1:end,5)/60))/1000.*x(i,1); % LID Infiltration (m3) 
    fid = fopen('Results\Sum_Infiltration_m3.txt','w'); 
% Save result in .txt file 
 
save Results\Sum_Infiltration_m3.txt Sum_Infiltration_m3 -ascii; fclose(fid);     
     
 
Sum_SurfaceFlow_m3(i,:) = (sum(dd(1:end,6)/60))/1000.*x(i,1); % LID Surface Flow (m3) 
    fid = fopen('Results\Sum_SurfaceFlow_m3.txt','w');  
% Save result in .txt file 
 
save Results\Sum_SurfaceFlow_m3.txt Sum_SurfaceFlow_m3 -ascii; fclose(fid);     
     
 
Sum_DrainFlow_m3(i,:) = (sum(dd(1:end,7)/60))/1000.*x(i,1);   % LID Drain Flow (m3) 
    fid = fopen('Results\Sum_DrainFlow_m3.txt','w');  
% Save result in .txt file 
 
save Results\Sum_DrainFlow_m3.txt Sum_DrainFlow_m3 -ascii; fclose(fid);     
 
    dfs0File.Close();     
end

%% Plot results

for j=1:p

    else set(gca,'fontsize',Font_size,'XTick',[]); 
    end 
end

for j=1:p 
    if j == 1;          
% The if loop ensures y-axis label only on left graphs

    else y_label = ' '; 
    end

% The if loop ensures axis values only on bottom and left graphs

    else set(gca,'fontsize',Font_size,'XTick',[]);

end

% The if loop ensures axis values only on bottom and left graphs
