==PAGE:99==
     
%% Standardized Regression Coefficients: 
    disp 'SRC analysis...' 
    tic                                                 % Simulation time to do SRC analysis 
 
    % Evaporation: 
    for i=1:p 
        y_ET = Sum_ET_m3; 
        mean_x_ET(:,i) = mean(x(:,i)); 
        mean_y_ET = mean(y_ET); 
        x_sub_ET(:,i) = x(:,i)-mean_x_ET(:,i); 
        y_sub_ET = y_ET-mean_y_ET; 
        x_subi2_ET(:,i) = x_sub_ET(:,i).^2; 
        xy_multi_ET(:,i) = x_sub_ET(:,i).*y_sub_ET; 
        sum_x_subi2_ET(:,i) = sum(x_subi2_ET(:,i)); 
        sum_xy_multi_ET(:,i) = sum(xy_multi_ET(:,i)); 
        std_x_ET(i) = std(x(:,i)); 
        std_y_ET = std(y_ET); 
        std_div_ET(i) = std_x_ET(i)./std_y_ET; 
        std_multi_ET(i) = std_x_ET(i).*std_y_ET; 
        b_ET(i) = sum_xy_multi_ET(:,i)./sum_x_subi2_ET(:,i); 
         
        % Sensitivity: 
        beta_ET(i) = b_ET(i).*std_div_ET(i); 
         
        % Linearity: 
        R2_ET(i) = ((1/n).*sum_xy_multi_ET(:,i)./std_multi_ET(i)).^2; 
    end 
     
    % Infiltration: 
    for i=1:p 
        y_Inf = Sum_Infiltration_m3; 
        mean_x_Inf(:,i) = mean(x(:,i)); 
        mean_y_Inf = mean(y_Inf); 
        x_sub_Inf(:,i) = x(:,i)-mean_x_Inf(:,i); 
        y_sub_Inf = y_Inf-mean_y_Inf; 
        x_subi2_Inf(:,i) = x_sub_Inf(:,i).^2; 
        xy_multi_Inf(:,i) = x_sub_Inf(:,i).*y_sub_Inf; 
        sum_x_subi2_Inf(:,i) = sum(x_subi2_Inf(:,i)); 
        sum_xy_multi_Inf(:,i) = sum(xy_multi_Inf(:,i)); 
        std_x_Inf(i) = std(x(:,i)); 
        std_y_Inf = std(y_Inf); 
        std_div_Inf(i) = std_x_Inf(i)./std_y_Inf; 
        std_multi_Inf(i) = std_x_Inf(i).*std_y_Inf; 
        b_Inf(i) = sum_xy_multi_Inf(:,i)./sum_x_subi2_Inf(:,i); 
         
        % Sensitivity: 
        beta_Inf(i) = b_Inf(i).*std_div_Inf(i); 
         
        % Linearity: 
        R2_Inf(i) = ((1/n).*sum_xy_multi_Inf(:,i)./std_multi_Inf(i)).^2; 
    end 
     
    % Overflow frequency: 
    for i=1:p 
        y_OF = Overflow_freq; 
        mean_x_OF(:,i) = mean(x(:,i)); 
        mean_y_OF = mean(y_OF); 
        x_sub_OF(:,i) = x(:,i)-mean_x_OF(:,i); 
        y_sub_OF = y_OF-mean_y_OF; 
        x_subi2_OF(:,i) = x_sub_OF(:,i).^2; 
        xy_multi_OF(:,i) = x_sub_OF(:,i).*y_sub_OF; 
        sum_x_subi2_OF(:,i) = sum(x_subi2_OF(:,i)); 
        sum_xy_multi_OF(:,i) = sum(xy_multi_OF(:,i)); 
        std_x_OF(i) = std(x(:,i)); 
==PAGE:100==
        std_y_OF = std(y_OF); 
        std_div_OF(i) = std_x_OF(i)./std_y_OF; 
        std_multi_OF(i) = std_x_OF(i).*std_y_OF; 
        b_OF(i) = sum_xy_multi_OF(:,i)./sum_x_subi2_OF(:,i); 
         
        % Sensitivity: 
        beta_OF(i) = b_OF(i).*std_div_OF(i); 
         
        % Linearity: 
        R2_OF(i) = ((1/n).*sum_xy_multi_OF(:,i)./std_multi_OF(i)).^2; 
    end   
     
    % Save all SRC results: 
    Outputs = {' ' ;'Evaporation'; 'Infiltration'; 'Overflow frequency'}; 
    Parameters_horizontal = rot90(Parameters); 
    Beta_parameters = [cellstr(Parameters_horizontal); num2cell(beta_ET); num2cell(beta_Inf); 
 
num2cell(beta_OF)]; 
    Beta = [cellstr(Outputs) Beta_parameters]; 
    fid = fopen('Results\Beta.txt', 'wt'); fprintf(fid,'%s \t %s \t %s \t %s \t \n', 
 
Beta{:}); fclose(fid); 
    R2_parameters = [cellstr(Parameters_horizontal); num2cell(R2_ET); num2cell(R2_Inf); 
 
num2cell(R2_OF)]; 
    R2 = [cellstr(Outputs) R2_parameters]; 
    fid = fopen('Results\R2.txt', 'wt'); fprintf(fid,'%s \t %s \t %s \t %s \t \n', R2{:}); 
 
fclose(fid); 
     
 
toc                                                 % Simulation time to do SRC analysis 
 
==PAGE:101==
A
Matlab Scripts
A.3
Processing the Results for the Regression Anal-
ysis
When performing the regression analysis used to update the WSUD-potential tool, a mod-
iﬁed version of the Matlab script presented in Appendix A.2 is used to perform the Monte
Carlo analysis. In the modiﬁed script only the area of the bioretention and the conduc-
tivity of the native soil is varied.
Another Matlab script is used to process the results from the Monte Carlo analysis. This
script is shown in this appendix.
The Matlab script consists of the following steps:
 Initially the total precipitation in the catchment is deﬁned, together with the total
evaporation in the catchment (excluding the evaporation in the bioretention).
 The parameters and results from the ﬁrst Monte Carlo analysis (with log-normally
distributed conductivity) are loaded. The area of the bioretention is recalculated to
area ratio and the evaporation and inﬁltration are calculated as percentage of the
entire water balance in the catchment. For this the precipitation and evaporation
deﬁned in the ﬁrst step are used.
 The parameters and results from the second Monte Carlo analysis (with uniformly
distributed conductivity) are loaded, and processed as described in step 2.
 In the fourth step the parameters and results from the two Monte Carlo analyses
are merged together.
 Finally the curve ﬁtting tool cftool is opened. The analysis of the results is continued
in the cftool, where the best ﬁt is found by testing diﬀerent degrees of polynomial
regressions and customised equations.
92
==PAGE:102==
Matlab Script for Processing the Results for the Regression Analysis 
 
%% Define rain and precipitation 
     Total_Precipitation = 6343.1;    % Total precipitation [m^3] in the catchment 
     ET_in_catchment = 950;           % Total evaporation [m^3] in catchment (excl. the LID) 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% LOG-NORMAL: Results from the first Monte Carlo Analysis with log-normally distributed K 
    load log/Parameter_values_AK;            
% Parameter values for both A and K 
    A_log = x(:,1);                          
% Area of LID [m^2] 
    A_ratio_log = A_log/1000*100;            
% Area ratio [%] 
    K_log = x(:,2);                          
% Conductivity of native soil [m/s] 
     
    load log/Sum_ET_m3;                      
% Total evaporation [m^3] in LID 
    ET_log = Sum_ET_m3; 
    Total_ET_log = ET_log + ET_in_catchment; 
% Total evaporation [m^3] in catchment 
    ET_percent_log = Total_ET_log/Total_Precipitation*100;      % Evaporation [%] 
     
    load log/Sum_Infiltration_m3; 
 
% Total infiltration [m^3] in LID 
    Inf_log = Sum_Infiltration_m3;           
    Infiltration_percent_log = Inf_log/Total_Precipitation*100; % Infiltration [%] 
     
    load log/Overflow_freq;                  
% Overflow frequency [1/y] 
    OF_log = Overflow_freq; 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
     
%% UNIFORM: Results from the second Monte Carlo Analysis with uniformly distributed K 
    load uniform/Parameter_values_AK;        
% Parameter values for both A and K 
    A_uni = x(:,1);                          
% Area of LID [m^2] 
    A_ratio_uni = A_uni/1000*100;            
% Area ratio [%] 
    K_uni = x(:,2);                          
% Conductivity of native soil [m/s] 
     
    load uniform/Sum_ET_m3;                  
% Total evaporation [m^3] in LID 
    ET_uni = Sum_ET_m3; 
    Total_ET_uni = ET_uni + ET_in_catchment; 
% Total evaporation [m^3] in catchment 
    ET_percent_uni = Total_ET_uni/Total_Precipitation*100;      % Evaporation [%] 
     
    load uniform/Sum_Infiltration_m3; 
% Total infiltration [m^3] in LID 
    Inf_uni = Sum_Infiltration_m3;           
    Infiltration_percent_uni = Inf_uni/Total_Precipitation*100; % Infiltration [%] 
     
    load uniform/Overflow_freq;              
% Overflow frequency [1/y] 
    OF_uni = Overflow_freq;  
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Merge results from the two Monte Carlo analyses (log-normally and uniformly distributed K) 
    A_merged = [A_ratio_uni; A_ratio_log]; 
% Area ratio [%] 
    K_merged_original = [K_uni; K_log]; 
% Conductivity [m/s] 
    K_merged = log(K_merged_original); 
% log(K) 
    ET_merged = [ET_percent_uni; ET_percent_log];  
 
% Evaporation [%] 
    Inf_merged = [Infiltration_percent_uni; Infiltration_percent_log]; 
% Infiltration [%] 
    OF_merged = [OF_uni; OF_log]; 
 
% Overflow frequency [1/y] 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Open the curve fitting tool cftool 
    cftool 
% In the cftool x = A_merged, y = K_merged and z = ET_merged, Inf_merged or OF_merged  
depending on what processes is analysed. Different degrees and polynomial fit and costum 
equations can be used to find the best fit to the results from the Monte Carlo analysis. 
==PAGE:103==
A
Matlab Scripts
A.4
Analysing Results from .res1d-ﬁle
Detailed result ﬁles can be extracted from MIKE URBAN as .res1d-ﬁles. This appendix
presents the Matlab script used to read and process catchment results in the .res1d-ﬁle.
The Matlab script consists of the steps below:
 Initially the area of the catchment and the simulation time are deﬁned.
 The .res1d-ﬁle is loaded. This part of the script is based on the DHI Matlab Toolbox
(DHI, 2014), where .res1d-ﬁles are loaded to read network results.
This part is
modiﬁed in order to read catchment results.
 In the ﬁnal part the results are processed and accumulated.
94
==PAGE:104==
Matlab Script for Accumulating Results from .res1d-file 
 
%% Define parameters 
    A_catchment = 19300;                            % Area of catchment [m^2] 
    Simulation_time = 10;                           % Simulation time [years]  
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Import .res1d-file (MIKE URBAN result file)     
    filename = 'Result_file.res1d';                 % Change to correct file 
  
    rdAss = NET.addAssembly('DHI.Mike1D.ResultDataAccess'); 
    import DHI.Mike1D.ResultDataAccess.* 
    rd = DHI.Mike1D.ResultDataAccess.ResultData(); 
    rd.Connection = DHI.Mike1D.Generic.Connection.Create(filename); 
    rd.Load(); 
    catchment = rd.Catchments.Item(0); 
_____________________________________________________________________________________________ 
  
%% Accumulate different processes 
    % Rain: 
    Rain_input = catchment.DataItems.Item(1);               % Rain [m/s] 
    Rain_ms = double(Rain_input.CreateTimeSeriesData(0)); 
    Rain = Rain_ms * 60 * A_catchment;                      % Rain [m^3/min] 
    Total_Rain = sum(Rain)                                  % Accumulated rain [m^3] 
         
    % Runoff: 
    Runoff_input = catchment.DataItems.Item(0);             % Runoff [m^3/s] 
    Runoff_m3s = double(Runoff_input.CreateTimeSeriesData(0)); 
    Runoff = Runoff_m3s * 60;                               % Runoff [m^3/min] 
    Total_Runoff = sum(Runoff)                              % Runoff [m^3] 
    Runoff_percent_A = Total_Runoff / Total_Rain * 100;     % Runoff [%] 
     
    % Evaporation: 
    ET_input = catchment.DataItems.Item(2);                 % Evaporation [m/s] 
    ET_ms = double(ET_input.CreateTimeSeriesData(0)); 
    ET = ET_ms * 60 * A_catchment;                          % Evaporation [m^3/min] 
    Total_ET = sum(ET)                                      % Evaporation [m^3] 
    ET_percent_A = Total_ET / Total_Rain * 100;             % Evaporation [%] 
     
    % Infiltration: 
    Inf_input = catchment.DataItems.Item(3);                % Infiltration [m/s] 
    Inf_ms = double(Inf_input.CreateTimeSeriesData(0)); 
    Inf = Inf_ms * 60 * A_catchment;                        % Infiltration [m^3/min] 
    Total_Inf = sum(Inf)                                    % Infiltration [m^3] 
    Inf_percent_A = Total_Inf / Total_Rain * 100;           % Infiltration [%] 
==PAGE:105==
B
Historical Rain Series
B
Historical Rain Series
The historical rain series from 5740 Kløvermarksvej is analysed in diﬀerent ways. Figure
B.1 shows the annual precipitation for each year, where the mean annual precipitation
is calculated to 600 mm. This is 100 mm lower than the mean annual precipitation in
Denmark (DMI, 2014a). The mean annual precipitation of the 10 and 30 years rain period
is calculated to 630 and 580 mm respectively, see Table B.1. The table also shows the
number of rain events in each period.
Figure B.1: Annual precipitation at Kløvermarksvej, Copenhagen.
Table B.1: Mean annual precipitation and number of rain events in the historical rain series from
Kløvermarksvej, Copenhagen. Results for the 10 and 30 years rain series are also included.
Time seires
Mean annual precipitation [mm]

211
DTU Rain Analyst has been used to further analyse the 10 and 30 years of the historical
rain series. DTU Rain Analyst analyses each rain event by ranking them and calculating
return periods. Return periods are calculated based on the median plotting position shown
in Equation B.1, where T is the length of the time series and r is the rank (Sørup et al.,
2015).
Tmedian = T + 0.4
r −0.3
(B.1)
The calculated return periods are shown in Figure B.2 for both the 10 and 30 years rain.
96
==PAGE:106==
B
Historical Rain Series
Figure B.2: Calculated return periods for 10 and 30 years rain. The results are calculated using
the DTU Rain Analyst.
Based on the calculated return periods, the historical rain series is compared to the re-
gional model. The comparison is expressed in U-values and f-values, which are presented in
Table B.2 - B.5 for the two periods. The U-value is the uncertainty between the historical
rain series and the regional model. The f-value is the diﬀerence compared to the regional
model. If the U-value < 1 and the f-value > 1 it is an indication that the historical rain se-
ries contain less extreme rain events than the regional model. Whereas a U-value > 1 and
a f-value < 1 indicates more extreme events in the historical rain series (Sørup et al., 2015).
For the 10 years rain the U-values vary between 0.65 and 3.7 and the mean f-value =
0.81. The f-values show that the 10 years rain contain more extreme values regarding
events with high return period and long duration, whereas the more frequent events with
shorter duration is less extreme than the regional model. However, the values are consid-
ered acceptable and the 10 years rain can be used for the simulations.

and 3.7.

C
Sensitivity Analysis
C.1
Monte Carlo Sensitivity Analysis
Detailed results for the two Monte Carlo sensitivity analyses are presented in this section.
C.1.1
The Five Most Inﬂuential Parameters
A Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis was made with the ﬁve most inﬂuential parameters
found in the OAT sensitivity analysis. The Matlab script presented in Section A.2 was
used to run 500 simulations with a 10 years rain. The distribution of the input parameters
can be seen in Figure C.1. The area ratio, soil height, ﬁeld capacity and storage height
are uniformly distributed and the conductivity follows a log-normal distribution. Table
C.1 shows the calculated SRCs and coeﬃcient of determination (R2).
Figure C.1: The distribution of input parameters in the ﬁrst Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis.
The model is evaluated 500 times, applying 10 years rain. Aratio: Area ratio. h, soil: Height of soil
layer. FC, soil: Field capacity of soil layer. h, storage: Height of storage layer. K: Conductivity
of native soil.

D
Regression Analysis
D
Regression Analysis
In the regression analysis, Monte Carlo analyses are performed, changing the area ratio
and the conductivity of the native soil. The distribution of the input parameters and the
detailed results of the regression analysis are presented in this appendix.
D.1
Distribution of Input Parameters
For each bioretention design, two Monte Carlo analyses are performed, one with log-
normally distributed conductivity and one with uniformly distributed conductivity. Both
analyses have uniformly distributed area ratio. The distributions of the input parameters
in the two analyses are shown in Figure D.1.
(a) Log-normally distributed conductivity.
(b) Uniformly distributed conductivity.
Figure D.1: The distribution of input parameters in the Monte Carlo analyses performed in the
regression analysis. Aratio: Area ratio, K: Conductivity of native soil.
D.2
Regressions for Bioretention Designs
In this section the detailed results of the regression analysis are presented for the diﬀer-
ent bioretention designs. For each bioretention the results are presented for evaporation,
followed by inﬁltration and ﬁnally the results for overﬂow frequency are presented. The
results include plots of the regression, plots of the residuals, the equation of the regression
and parameters for goodness of ﬁt.
In order to obtain an acceptable ﬁt for inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency, two regres-
sions are necessary for each of the designs, where one regression describes the low range of
the conductivity (5·10−9 - 1·10−7 m/s) and another regression describes the high range of
the conductivity (1·10−7 - 4·10−5 m/s). The regressions can generally only be used when
area ratio and conductivity are within the ranges of 1% - 30% and 5 · 10−9 - 4 · 10−5 m/s
respectively. The range of conductivity corresponds to -19 < log(K) < -10, when looking
at the plotted results.
D.2.1
Bioretention A
The regressions for Bioretention A are presented below.
102
==PAGE:112==
D
Regression Analysis
D.2.1.1
Evaporation
Figure D.2 and D.3 show the regression and the residuals, respectively, for evaporation
when applying Bioretention A. Figure D.2 shows that the evaporation increases for increas-
ing area ratio and decreasing conductivity. The regression does not correctly describe the
evaporation for K > 4 · 10−5 m/s (log(K) > −10), as the evaporation starts to increase
above this point. Furthermore, the evaporation starts to decrease when K < 5 · 10−9 m/s
(log(K) < −19), which is incorrect.
Figure D.2:
Evaporation for Bioretention A. The surface shows the regression for the total
evaporation in the catchment depending on area ratio (Aratio) [%] and conductivity of native soil
(K) [m/s]. The dots represent results generated in MIKE URBAN.
The residuals for the regression reaches nearly 8% for low conductivities. However, within
the range where the regression can be used, the maximum residuals are of about 3%.
Figure D.3: Residuals for the regression for evaporation depending on area ratio (Aratio) [%] and
the conductivity (K) [m/s] of the native soil. The result is based on simulations with Bioretention
A.
The percentage of water that evaporates when applying Bioretention A can be calculated
with the regression shown in Equation D.1, where x = Aratio and y = log(K).
IE[%] = C1 + C2x + C3y + C4xy + C5y2 + C6xy2 + C7y3 + C8xy3 + C9y4
for
1% < Aratio < 30%
and
5 · 10−9m/s < K < 4 · 10−5m/s
(D.1)

D.2.1.2
Inﬁltration
Two regressions used to describe the inﬁltration for Bioretention A are shown in Figure
D.4, for the low and high conductivity range respectively. The inﬁltration increases for
increasing conductivity and increasing area ratio. The inﬁltration seems to reach a max-
imum when the conductivity increases above 3 · 10−7 m/s (log(K) > -15) and the area
ratio is above 10%. For conductivities below 5 · 10−9 m/s (log(K) < -19), the inﬁltration
starts to increase, which is incorrect.
(a) Low conductivity range.
(b) High conductivity range.

[m/s]. The dots represent results generated in MIKE URBAN. Black results are included in the
ﬁtting, whereas grey results are excluded from the ﬁtting.
The residuals of the regressions are presented in Figure D.5. For the regression describing
the low conductivity range, the biggest error is about -5%. For the other regression the
error reaches about 12%.

D.2.1.3
Overﬂow Frequency
The overﬂow frequency regressions can be seen in Figure D.6. The overﬂow frequency
increases for decreasing conductivity and decreasing area ratio, where it reaches about
150 y−1. For very low conductivities, the regression starts to decrease, which is incorrect,
as the overﬂow frequency should increase further. However, this decrease occurs outside
of the range where the regressions can be applied.
(a) Low conductivity range.
(b) High conductivity range.
Figure D.6: Overﬂow frequency for Bioretention A. The surfaces show the regressions for overﬂow
frequency depending on area ratio (Aratio) [%] and conductivity of native soil (K) [m/s]. The dots
represent results generated in MIKE URBAN. Black results are included in the ﬁtting, whereas
grey results are excluded from the ﬁtting.
The residuals of the regressions are presented in Figure D.7. The biggest error is about
25 y−1 for low area ratio in the regression describing the high conductivity range.

==PAGE:129==
D
Regression Analysis
D.4
Single vs. Multiple Regressions
Figure D.20 compares results for Bioretention A from both MIKE URBAN and the new
WSUD-potential tool.
To the left in the ﬁgure, the result of the WSUD-potential tool is based on one regres-
sion ﬁtted to all the results from MIKE URBAN. It can be seen how the regression for
overﬂow frequency is underestimated for low conductivities and gives negative values for
high conductivities. Furthermore, the inﬁltration is overestimated for low conductivities
and slightly overestimated for high conductivities. To better ﬁt the results from MIKE
URBAN, two regressions are made, one for low conductivities (5 · 10−9 - 1 · 10−7 m/s)
and one for high conductivities (1 · 10−7 - 4 · 10−5 m/s). These regressions can be seen in
Figure D.20 to the right. It can be seen that there is a better ﬁt to the MIKE URBAN
results when using two regressions.
(a) One regression.
(b) Two regressions.
Figure D.20: Inﬁltration and overﬂow frequency for Bioretention A in MIKE URBAN and the
new WSUD-potential tool. The area ratio is kept constant at 10%. The results are shown for both
one and two regressions.
Figure D.21 shows the results for the three bioretention designs with four diﬀerent con-
ductivities. The result is shown when one regression is used for each process. The plot
corresponds to the plot shown in Figure 4.10, where the results from using two regressions
are shown.
The results in Figure D.21 shows how one regression cannot correctly describe the pro-
cesses, especially not the overﬂow frequency. At some point the overﬂow frequency starts
to increase for increasing area ratio, which is unrealistic.

per process.

E
Case Study: Trekanten
E
Case Study: Trekanten
Terrain proﬁles of the three roads in Trekanten are plotted in Figure E.1. According to
the proﬁle the elevation in Vestre All´e decreases from about 9.1 m in the south to about
8.5 m further north. In the northern end of the road the elevation slightly increases again
to around 8.7 m. The elevation of Nordre All´e steadily decreases from about 9.0 m in
the eastern end, to about 8.5 m in the western end. Søndre All´e is slightly sloping with
highest elevation in the south-west end and lowest elevation in the north-east end of the
road.
Figure E.1: Elevation (mamsl) of the roads in Trekanten (Septima, 2016).
Figure E.2 shows contaminated sites in the area around Trekanten.
There are some
contaminated sites, in the northern and eastern part of the homeowner’s association.
These show contamination with oil and benzene in both soil and groundwater (Danmarks
Miljøportal, 2016).

Case Study: Utterslevhøj
F.1
Method for Utterslevhøj
Utterslevhøj homeowner’s association, referred to as Utterslevhøj, is a residential area
situated north-west of Copenhagen.

It includes
about 300 houses and is located just south of the wetland Utterslev Mose.
Figure F.1: Utterslevhøj homeowner’s association located north-west of Copenhagen. Based on
ArcGIS analysis. Arrows indicate ﬂow direction.
F.1.1
Land Use
The area of Utterslevhøj is analysed in ArcGIS, using data from the Danish Geodata
Agency (2016). Based on the analysis in ArcGIS, Utterslevhøj is estimated to cover an
area of about 240 000 m2 (24 ha). The land use distribution is presented in Table F.1,
showing that 70% and 30% of the area is pervious and impervious respectively.

F.1.2
Elevation, Geology and Groundwater Level
As for Trekanten, the potential of implementing bioretentions in Utterslevhøj depends on
several parameters, such as elevation, geology and groundwater level. The elevation of the
roads in Utterslevhøj is analysed the same way as for Trekanten. Proﬁles are presented in
Figure F.2, showing that Utterslevhøj is a hilly area, see ﬂow direction in Figure F.1.
125
==PAGE:135==
F
Case Study: Utterslevhøj
Figure F.2: Elevation (mamsl) of the roads in Utterslevhøj (Septima, 2016).
126
==PAGE:136==
F
Case Study: Utterslevhøj
A ﬂood map was retrieved from Utterslevhøj homeowner’s association, see Figure F.3.
After the rain event the 2nd of July 2011, the residents gathered information regarding
ﬂooding of wastewater and water from the terrain. It can be seen that ﬂooding occurs in
many areas especially close to Hyrdevangen, in the area around Enemærket and at the
northern part of Engsvinget.
Figure F.3: Locations of ﬂooding after the rain event 2nd of July 2011. Retrieved from Utter-
slevhøj homeowner’s association (2016).
Several boreholes are located in the area around Utterslevhøj, describing the geology and
groundwater level. The borehole-logs are available in the Jupiter database provided by
GEUS (2016). Based on the borehole information it can be seen that the geology in the
top 3 m consists of mould and peat, followed by boulder clay down to approximately 10
m, with chalk underneath. The groundwater level has previously been measured to vary
between 3 - 4 m below terrain (GEUS, 2016). As described for Trekanten, in Section 3.6.2,
the groundwater might therefore aﬀect the inﬁltration (Locatelli et al., 2015b). In order
to test the inﬁltration potential, inﬁltration tests shall be carried out in the area.
Contamination is unlikely to be a limitation for enhancing the inﬁltration in the area,
as contaminated sites are not expected within Utterslevhøj, see Figure F.4.

Case Study: Utterslevhøj

Miljøportal, 2016).
F.1.3
Utterslevhøj in the Climate Adaptation Plan
Utterslevhøj is a part of the cloudburst branch Utterslev Mose, see Figure F.5, which
covers an area of 208 ha and has a low risk of ﬂooding. 13 projects are expected to be
implemented in the branch. There is one cloudburst road in contact with Utterslevhøj,
where water is supposed to be discharged into Utterslev Mose. Green roads are planned
on Mosesvinget and Engsvinget, and one retention space is planned to be implemented at
Enemærket (Københavns Kommune, 2015). It is therefore interesting to evaluate the LID
potential in Utterslevhøj.

==PAGE:138==

Case Study: Utterslevhøj
Figure F.5: Location of Utterslevhøj homeowner’s association in the cloudburst branch Utterslev
Mose (Københavns Kommune, 2015).
F.1.4
Bioretentions in Utterslevhøj
The analysis of the bioretention potential in Utterslevhøj is carried out using only the new
WSUD-potential tool. The area that can be used for bioretentions is estimated in the
same way as it is for Trekanten. This gives a maximum area of approximately 3 000 m2
on roads, divided on 120 bioretentions with an area of 25 m2 each. The diﬀerent analysed
scenarios can be seen in Table F.2. As for Trekanten diﬀerent conductivities and area
ratios will be analysed both with and without the runoﬀfrom the roofs.

F.2.2
Excluding Roofs
Figure F.7 and Table F.4 show the water balance and overﬂow frequencies obtained from
the new WSUD-potential tool for Utterslevhøj, when the roofs are excluded from the
collecting area.
It can be seen that the biggest runoﬀof about 20% is observed for
Bioretention A with a low conductivity, whereas high conductivity results in almost no
runoﬀfor all three designs. The overﬂow frequencies are therefore 0 for all designs with
the high conductivity and between 30 - 12 y−1 for the low conductivity.

F.2.3
Area Analysis
As for Trekanten, the necessary size of bioretentions are analysed if certain overﬂow fre-
quencies shall be obtained. The result is presented in Table F.5. The maximum area of
roads that can be turned into bioretentions is estimated in Section F.1.4. It is estimated
that about 120 bioretentions, of 25 m2 each, can be implemented on the roads in Utter-
slevhøj. In that case, the total area of bioretentions is 3 000 m2, which is 4.1% or 9.4%
of the catchment if the roofs are included or excluded from the collecting area respectively.
In Table F.5 the black and grey numbers indicate if the area is below or above the estimated
limit. The result for Utterslevhøj looks similar to the result for Trekanten. However, due
to the larger maximum area ratio in Utterslevhøj (4.1% and 9.4%) compared to in Trekan-
ten (3.7% and 7.4%), more of the scenarios are realistic to implement in Utterslevhøj. For
Bioretention C the result is the same as for Trekanten, but for both Bioretention A and
B more of the calculated areas are below the limit.

T: Return period. K: Conductivity of native soil.
In Utterslevhøj bioretentions can also be implemented in the gardens to treat the water
from the roofs. A general lot in Utterslevhøj is estimated to be 500 m2, where the roofs
account for approximately 130 m2.
In order to manage the most demanding scenario
(Bioretention A, with the lowest conductivity and highest return period), a bioretention
area of about 40 m2 is needed. As for Trekanten it is assumed that this is manageable to
implement in the gardens.
According to the application Utterslevhøj made to HOFOR regarding co-ﬁnancing, struc-
tures are designed to overﬂow every third year. The application states that ﬂooding has
been most visible close to the green area at Enemærket. The solutions presented in the
application includes bioretentions with soakaway and drain on an area of 1 200 m2, per-
meable pavement on 1 500 m2 and a detention basin on the green area at Enemærket.
A conductivity of 1 · 10−6 m/s was used and a total impervious area of 61 100 m2 was
estimated (Utterslevhøj homeowner’s association, 2015). The corresponding area of the
bioretention when using the new WSUD-potential tool and Bioretention C is 3 880 m2
when reaching an overﬂow frequency of 0.3 y−1. The drain is though not included in
Bioretention C. The results are therefore not directly comparable, as the necessary area
will be smaller when a drain is included in the design.
